# 周易参同契发挥

The *Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Masters Section, Volume 14*
*Elucidations on the Uniting of the Three in Accordance with the Zhou Changes*
*Daoist School*

**Editorial Summary**

We, your ministers, respectfully observe: *Elucidations on the Uniting of the Three in Accordance with the Zhou Changes* in three *juan*, with one *juan* of *Resolving Doubts*, was composed by Yu Yan of the Song. Yan’s *Collected Explanations of the Zhou Changes* has already been recorded. This work expounds the Dao of cinnabar elixirs through the water and fire, yin and yang of the human body. Though it does not match the specialized Daoist scholarship of the three commentaries by Peng Xiao, Chen Xianwei, and Chen Zhixu, its sources are exceedingly broad. Its three chapters of *Resolving Doubts* examine discrepancies and similarities, surpassing even Zhu Xi’s edition in thoroughness and clarity.

The *Catalogue of the Daoist Canon* by Yunji Daolu states that the two works together comprise fourteen *juan*, while Jiao Hong’s *Bibliographic Treatise of the History of the Realm* records twelve *juan*. Mao Jin’s *Treasured Writings from the Ford of Jin* combines Yan’s commentary with the three commentaries of Xiao and the others into a single compilation, deviating from the original. Moreover, it omits *Resolving Doubts*, which has since been lost. This edition includes illustrations in every *juan* and was printed in the third year of the Zhida era (1310) by Zhang Yufeng, the succeeding Celestial Master. It consists solely of three *juan* of *Elucidations*, with one *juan* of *Resolving Doubts* appended.

An examination of Yan’s preface to *Other Writings on the Changes* also describes the oral instructions of the cinnabar Dao as meticulously preserved in the three chapters of *Elucidations on the Uniting of the Three in Accordance with the Zhou Changes*. The record in Yunji Daolu may have conflated this work with others, while Jiao Hong’s account appears to have been copied and distorted from other sources, rendering it increasingly unreliable.

Respectfully submitted in the sixth month of the forty-fifth year of the Qianlong reign (1780).
**Chief Compilers:**
Minister Ji Yun
Minister Lu Feixiong
Minister Sun Shiyi

**Chief Collators:**
Minister Lu Feichi

**Preface to the Reprinted *Elucidations on the Uniting of the Three in Accordance with the Zhou Changes***

Of old, when the sages composed the *Changes*, it began with Fuxi, who received the *Hetu* and drew the trigrams. King Wen expanded them and appended the hexagram statements; the Duke of Zhou extended the six positions to determine fortune and misfortune and appended the line statements. Confucius, following the *Great* and *Lesser Images*, composed the *Ten Wings*. Its fundamental principle lies in the *taiji*: when it moves, it generates yang; when it rests, it generates yin. Once the Two Modes are divided, the Four Images are established. Movement and stillness mutually serve as their root, giving rise to the Five Phases. Through the interplay of yin and yang, odd and even, the Eight Trigrams are formed. The Yellow Emperor commanded Da Rao to create the *jiazi* cycle: its ten stems and twelve branches merely explore the nature of the Five Phases, yet they too do not depart from the directional positions of the Eight Trigrams. When trigrams are doubled, they become sixty-four hexagrams; when divided, they yield three hundred eighty-four lines. Thus, there is the Dao of Heaven, the Dao of Earth, and the Dao of Man. With the Dao of the Three Powers complete, all things filling Heaven and Earth possess principle, qi, image, and number. Their waxing and waning, advance and retreat, preservation and destruction—none fall outside the sage’s compass, for he discerns and comprehends their underlying causes. Therefore, the *Book of Changes* is vast and all-encompassing. By means of it, teachings are established, and the methods of calendrical calculation and divination are founded upon the Five Phases and Eight Trigrams, sufficient to disclose things and accomplish tasks, thereby guiding the people. Indeed, the *Changes* is for the counsel of the noble person—how can one not employ it?

Yet the world does not embody the ceaseless movement of Heaven and Earth, does not explore the mutual generation and restraint of the Five Phases, nor follow the ebb and flow of hexagrams and lines. Acting recklessly, they fail to return to themselves, unaware of what cultivation and refinement entail. Few are those who preserve their seven-foot bodies and return them whole. How much less can they hope for longevity and enduring vision amidst Heaven and Earth?

Those of the immortal lineage who attain longevity and enduring vision do so through the Dao of the returned elixir. However, the immortals of antiquity necessarily possessed esoteric transmission and oral instruction; their secret formulas were seldom lightly disclosed. Thus, few accounts of them exist in the world. Some, not following the great Dao but relying on private cleverness, claim that crooked shortcuts are easily traversed or that heterodox byways lead to attainment. The worst of them consume desiccating medicines, drink noxious drafts, and ingest poisonous pellets, mistaking them for divine elixirs—only to lose their lives and forfeit their years. How can they preserve longevity?

By the Later Han, Wei Boyang recognized that the Dao of the returned elixir must not be led astray by perverse doctrines but must be rooted in the Dao of the *Changes*. For the Dao of the *Changes* encompasses the mechanism of yin and yang, movement and stillness, and embraces the marvel of hexagram and line transformation—ceaselessly generating, endlessly transforming. Now, when we turn inward to observe our bodies, are they not also a Heaven and Earth? The primordial qi we receive is none other than the qi of Heaven and Earth. If Heaven and Earth endure, why can we not emulate them?

This is why the *Cantong qi* was composed. *Can* means to consult the *Changes* and find no error; *tong* means to align with the *Changes* and find no discrepancy; *qi* means to silently comprehend and naturally preserve the *Changes*. Formerly, I heard that the immortals of old possessed the method of refining the elixir. Once the elixir was completed, one could ascend in flight, achieving longevity. Thus, the famous mountains and scenic places of the world all bear the strange traces of furnaces and crucibles. Alas! The odd stones in the mountains that occasionally resemble furnaces and crucibles are but coincidences. Upon reading this book, I realized that the notion of refining the elixir in furnaces and crucibles is delusion. The teachings of the immortals on guiding and refining cultivation operate within; they do not rely on external means. What is taken lies within oneself; it does not depend on...

What is sought lies within oneself, not in external things. Gold, for instance, is prized for its purity—unmixed, unadulterated. Without refining it with lead and mercury through fire, how could one attain its true essence, its unyielding hardness, to form the elixir and emulate the immortality of Heaven and Earth? Now, the *Cantong qi* borrows this principle: it takes the Qian and Kun of one’s body as the crucible, the Kan and Li—the sun and moon—as the medicinal ingredients. By concentrating the spirit and gathering the *qi*, causing the Dragon and Tiger to unite in essence and interact, advancing the fire phases to await their transformations, the Dao of the elixir may be accomplished.

Yet this book, though examined and annotated by Master Zhu Ziyang, still leaves many doubts unresolved. Though it provides a thread of understanding, the very materials and apertures remain obscure—how much more so for those beneath Master Zhu’s level of insight? In the Zhiyuan era, Master Yuyu, Quanyangzi, first delved deeply into this text. Initially, he failed to grasp its subtleties, finding it exceedingly difficult. Only through long accumulation of effort and unwavering faith did enlightenment come as if by divine revelation. Thereafter, he gathered the secret formulas of various perfected ones and compared them with this book, finding no discrepancy with the teachings of the *Changes*. Thus, he expounded upon this work, adding annotations that illuminated the Dao of the elixir as clearly as pointing to one’s palm.

Yet while the method of the returned elixir is said to be supremely simple and easy—accessible to all—it is also spoken of as requiring three years and nine cycles, appearing easy but proving difficult in truth. It demands prolonged dedication. Therefore, those who study the Dao in hopes of becoming immortals must not measure it hastily or glimpse it superficially. They must seek its doors and windows, examine its gates and pivots, wield the bellows and key, advance the fire phases, and guard the Dragon and Tiger until their union. Then the Yellow Sprout will grow of itself, the Jade Flower will manifest, and the Dao of the returned elixir will be within reach.

With this, one may ascend in flight, achieve eternal existence, ride the long clouds between the eight poles, and brave the fierce winds of the nine heavens—utterly severed from the world, dwelling in the divine isles, forever ascending the immortal’s throne. Once in a hundred generations, one descends to wander among mortals, bestowing long life and salvation, while none can fathom their whereabouts. Looking down upon the ephemeral world, where mayflies flicker in the morning and gnats rise and fall, it is truly not worth even a laugh.

*Preface to the* Elucidation of the *Cantong qi*
*Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Elucidation of the* Cantong qi*, Volume One*
*Upper Chapter*
*By Yu Yan of the Song Dynasty*

The Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*, the father and mother of all hexagrams. Closing the door is called Kun; opening the door is called Qian. One closing and one opening is called *change*; endless coming and going is called *communication*—this is why Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*. Qian gives birth to three sons; Kun gives birth to three daughters. Male and female unite in essence, and the myriad things are transformed and born—this is why Qian and Kun are the father and mother of all hexagrams.

The human body models Heaven and emulates Earth, sharing the same yin and yang as Heaven and Earth. If one understands that this body shares the same yin and yang with Heaven and Earth, then one may discuss the Dao of the returned elixir.

Kan and Li form the encompassing frame, turning the wheel with the axle as its center. Kan is the moon; Li is the sun. The sun and moon travel along the ecliptic, circulating endlessly day and night, like the revolving frame of a wheel. The hub is like the body; the axle is like the heart. If one wishes the hub to turn, one must align the axle. For those who cultivate the returned elixir, circulating the sun and moon within their body to follow the same path as Heaven and Earth’s creative transformations—how could they not rectify their heart?

The four hexagrams of the female and male serve as the bellows and pipe, covering and veiling the Dao of yin and yang, just as a charioteer holds the reins and bit with precision, adhering to the guiding line, following the tracks, maintaining the center to regulate the exterior. The numbers are recorded in the calendar’s monthly nodes: five or six weave the warp and weft, serving the sun to combine into sixty. Hard and soft have their inner and outer aspects. At dawn, *Zhun* governs affairs; at dusk, *Meng* receives its turn. Day and night each correspond to one hexagram, used in sequence. Before reaching the dim light of dawn, the cycle ends and begins anew.

Qian is the pure yang, male hexagram; Kun is the pure yin, female hexagram. Kan contains yang within yin; Li contains yin within yang—these are the hexagrams of female and male union. The elixir method positions Qian and Kun above and below, arranges Kan and Li to the east and west, so that the opening and closing of Qian and Kun, the coming and going of Kan and Li, resemble the form of bellows and pipe. Thus it is said: "The four hexagrams of the female and male serve as bellows and pipe." The bellows and pipe are empty vessels—the bellows is the leather sack, the pipe its tube. The *Daodejing* of Laozi states: "Is not the space between Heaven and Earth like a bellows?" Empty yet inexhaustible, moving yet ever-producing. The great void’s vastness resembles the body of the bellows; the opening and closing, coming and going of qi resemble its function. Knowing this, one has grasped more than half of the Dao of the returned elixir.

Yet this Dao exists when grasped and perishes when released. Therefore, one must guard one’s inner realm and restrain one’s outer conduct, as in the *Liezi: Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing*, which speaks of the charioteering of the Taidou clan. Only then may one engage in this practice. For the charioteering of the Taidou clan is to harmonize the reins and bit with utmost precision, adjusting swiftly or slowly as needed.

The harmony of lips and tongue is precisely measured within the chest, while rhythm is maintained in the grip of the hands. Between palm and grasp, the center is inwardly attained, and outwardly, the horse’s will is met. Thus can one advance and retreat as if treading a taut line, or turn and curve as if compass and square. The method of refining the elixir is likewise.

The numbers in the *lü* and calendric reckonings circulate through twelve positions: twelve pitches of the *lü* correspond to twelve months, and twelve years to a cycle. The monthly nodes of five and six arise from dividing a thirty-day month into six nodes. The warp is the fixed positions of Qian and Kun, north and south; the weft is the subtle workings of Kan and Li, east and west. The "servant of the sun" follows the sun’s command, its timing moving in perfect accord with the heavens, rising and setting without the slightest deviation.

The combination yields sixty: the *Book of Changes* has sixty-four hexagrams, but excluding Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li, exactly sixty remain. Each day has twelve hours, with two hexagrams governing affairs, each line corresponding to one hour—two hexagrams totaling twelve lines, matching the numbers of the *lü* and calendric reckonings. Combined with a thirty-day month, this yields sixty hexagrams. Arranging sixty hexagrams within a thirty-day month, at dawn of the new moon, *Zhun* governs the morning and *Meng* the evening; day and night’s twelve hours precisely correspond to two hexagrams and twelve lines. From there, morning *Xu* and evening *Song*, morning *Shi* and evening *Bi*, follow in sequence until the month’s end, when *Jiji* and *Weiji* are used. From dark moon to new moon, the cycle repeats endlessly. Within these hexagrams, the inner and outer, hard and soft bodies may reverse or oppose one another—none of this need be rigidly adhered to.

As Zhang Ziyang’s *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"Herein grasp the meaning, seek not the image;
To exhaust the lines is but to labor in vain."*
For the sixty hexagrams are not rigidly assigned within a thirty-day month.

Some, failing to comprehend this principle, cling to the doctrine of hexagram images, asserting that two hours of the day serve as the firing times, meticulously measuring the weight of lines to gauge the qi’s timing. Day and night, they anxiously watch the sundial and water clock—how grievously mistaken!

The sun and stars set the measure; movement and stillness vary in earliness and lateness. Chen Niju’s *Cuixu Pian* says: *"The heavens clearly divide twelve forms; all take them as the program for elixir refinement."* This refers to the sun and stars as the measure. The sun is fire; the stars are the timing. If one can reverse the heavens’ sun and stars to seek them within the body, then the position of the sun’s arrival becomes the hour. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: *"To refine the medicine, know dawn and dusk"*—this refers to movement and stillness varying in earliness and lateness. Movement is rising at sunrise to work; stillness is resting at sunset. Within our own bodies, there is naturally a sunrise and sunset, early and late. The firing times and movements of stillness and activity all secretly align with the celestial measures, none but using the pivot of the heavens and the tides of time as the moments for inner work.

In spring and summer, rely on the inner body, from *zi* to *chen* and *si*; in autumn and winter, employ the outer, from *wu* to *xu* and *hai*. Hexagrams have six lines: the lower three are inner, the upper three outer. Take, for example, the morning use of *Zhun* and evening use of *Meng*. Morning *Zhun* is the time of yang fire’s ascent, and *Zhun*’s *chujiu* line corresponds to *zi* within the body, moving from inner to outer—hence *"in spring and summer, rely on the inner body, from *zi* to *chen* and *si*."* Evening *Meng* is the time of yin tally’s descent, and *Meng*’s *shangjiu* line corresponds to *wu* within the body, moving from outer to inner—hence *"in autumn and winter, employ the outer, from *wu* to *xu* and *hai*."*

Yet the so-called spring, summer, autumn, and winter; *zi*, *wu*, *si*, and *hai*; and the doctrines of inner body and outer use are all metaphors. If one rigidly adheres to images and texts, practicing hexagram qi, one merely grasps Qian and Kun as the crucible, kneads the crow and hare as medicine, and cooks them—how could this refer to the Qian and Kun outside the body, the crow and hare outside the body? The seeker must seek them within the body.

Now, the celestial positions...

Above lies the position of earth, below the two qi circulate—ascending and descending in endless alternation, like a bellows. Man, born of harmonious qi between heaven and earth, shares the same original body with them. If one awakens to the bellows’ marvel of heaven and earth, this heart becomes empty, still, and serene, and qi naturally flows throughout above and below. When it opens, qi issues forth; when it closes, qi enters in. Qi issuing forth resembles earth qi ascending; qi entering resembles heaven qi descending. Thus may one equal heaven and earth in longevity.

Master Zihua said: "The ancients who understood the Dao built ramparts to guard against the perverse and dammed the source to nurture the true. Dwelling in seclusion, undisturbed by things, they regulated their breath to unite with spirit and qi. Guarding the po, observing precepts, they sealed their senses, focused without distraction—thus true qi was preserved. Above and below, it irrigated; qi flowed unimpeded, like water’s current, like the sun and moon’s ceaseless motion. Yin nourished the viscera; yang fortified the bowels. The source flowed abundantly, full yet not overflowing, balanced yet not brimming—this is called long life."

To abandon this and claim, "I possess a swift method to ascend the clouds," is but a devious byway of misguided learning, not the great elixir’s Way.

Heaven and earth are the images of Qian and Kun. The established positions are the arrayed stations of yin and yang’s pairing. The *Yi* speaks of Kan and Li: Kan and Li are Qian and Kun’s two functions. Without fixed lines or positions, they circulate through the six voids, their coming and going unfixed, their above and below without constancy.

Qian is heaven; Kun is earth—our body’s crucible. Li is the sun; Kan is the moon—our body’s medicine. The precelestial eight trigrams place Qian in the south and Kun in the north, Li in the east and Kan in the west. South and north array the positions of heaven and earth’s pairing; east and west divide the gates of sun and moon’s ingress and egress. Reversing the search to seek within our body, the principle is one.

Qian and Kun are the substance; Kan and Li are the function. These two, Kan and Li, circulate, ascending and descending through the six voids, coming and going above and below—indeed, without fixed lines or positions. Within our body’s crucible, Kan and Li move: heaven hides in the latent, earth hides in the latent—how could there be fixed lines or positions?

Concealed and submerged, they transform within. "Concealed and submerged" means spirit enters qi, qi enters the navel, and sinks to return to the sea’s depths. "Transforming within" means when the time arrives, qi naturally transmutes; when stillness reaches its extreme, the mechanism spontaneously activates, and the sun’s orb is embraced and brought forth. As Chen Xiyi’s *Zhi Pian* says: "Vast, without trace, returning to the mysterious; latent, with mechanisms, the sacred embryo is knit"—this is the meaning.

Enveloping the ten thousand things as the Dao’s guiding principle, the *Wuzhen Pian* states: "The Dao arises from emptiness and nonbeing; from the one qi, yin and yang are produced. Yin and yang unite to generate the three bodies; the three bodies engender the ten thousand things." The mother of the golden elixir is none other than the precelestial one qi. Split, it becomes two; divided, it becomes three; dispersed, it becomes the ten thousand things—all emerge from this one qi. Thus it is said: "Enveloping the ten thousand things as the Dao’s guiding principle."

To use the insubstantial to govern the substantial, the vessel’s function is emptiness. The *Daode Jing* says: "Thirty spokes share one hub; it is the center’s emptiness that makes the cart useful. Clay is shaped into vessels; it is the hollowness within that makes the vessel useful. Doors and windows are cut to make a room; it is the empty space that makes the room useful. Thus, what is present provides the advantage; what is absent provides the function."

Lin Yanzhai’s commentary states: "The hub is the part of the wheel that holds the axle; the spokes are the wheel’s limbs. The hub is empty in the center, hence it can move the cart. Clay is the potter’s material; hollow and round, it can become a vessel. Doors and windows are the room’s passages for light—these three are all metaphors for the function of emptiness. Thus it is said: 'What is present provides the advantage; what is absent provides the function.' Carts, vessels, and rooms are all real advantages, yet their usefulness as cart, vessel, or room lies in the function of emptiness."

Now, Master Wei’s statement—"to use the insubstantial to govern the substantial, the vessel’s function is emptiness"—truly employs Laozi’s meaning. In the elixir method, the great emptiness serves as crucible and furnace, and within the great emptiness, there naturally exists a wondrous function. Marvelous! Marvelous!

Guan Yinzi says: "Clothing sways in the void to obtain wind; exhaling upon things obtains water." Chen Baoyi’s commentary states: "Knowing that swaying in the void obtains wind, then striking our bellows can generate qi. Knowing that exhaling upon things obtains water, then nurturing our qi can transform essence." If one is ignorant of this and seeks elsewhere, the so-called crucible and furnace are not the Way of emptiness and nonbeing.

Thus, advancing and retreating, Kan and Li vanish and reappear. "Advancing" is the time for kindling the fire, when Kun’s three lines transform into Qian. "Retreating" is the time for withdrawing the tally, when Qian’s three lines transform into Kun. Now, from the new moon when the Zhen trigram governs, passing through Dui to Qian, and from the full moon when the Xun trigram governs, passing through Gen to Kun, Kan and Li’s line positions are unseen—is this not the vanishing of Kan and Li?

Yet Kan and Li do not vanish; they move through the six voids, their coming and going unfixed.

Words are not carelessly crafted; theories are not idly born. Drawing evidence, verifying effects, measuring with spirit and numen, tracing the origins of characters to confirm principles: Kan is the essence of the wu month; Li is the light of the si day. Sun and moon are the *Yi*’s correlates of firm and yielding. Earth rules the four seasons, weaving the warp of beginning and end. Green, red, white, and black each occupy a quarter, all receiving the power of the central palace’s wu and si.

Kan is yin without and yang within—the image of the moon. Li is yang without and yin within—the image of the sun. Kan receives wu, the yang earth; Li receives ji, the yin earth. When the two earths unite, yin and yang harmonize, firm and yielding correspond—like the two characters for sun and moon combining to form the character *yi*. Green, red, white, and black are wood, fire, metal, and water. Wood, fire, metal, and water each occupy a quarter; only earth resides in the center, dividing its flourishing seasons among chen, xu, chou, and wei to weave the warp of the year’s beginning and end. Thus, wood receives its flourishing, fire its rest, metal its birth, and water its cessation—all receive its power.

Yet this earth pervades everywhere...

Within the universe, there is no fixed position for earth—when in Kan, it is wu earth; when in Li, it is ji earth; when in the central palace, it is wu-ji earth; when in the four seasons, it is chen-xu-chou-wei earth. Yet it is the same substance. How profound! The *Wuzhen Pian* poem states: "The Four Symbols and Five Phases all rely on earth," and again: "Only because each holds true earth within does the golden elixir achieve return and restoration." For without true earth to harmonize them, yin and yang remain blocked, firm and yielding sundered—no elixir can form.

Alas! When Master Wei composed this book to reveal the secrets of this Dao, he drew only from verified evidence and personal attainment. Yet fearing this might still fail to convince later generations, he further calibrated the numinous workings of sun and moon as a method and traced the principles of the *Yi* hexagrams as proof—truly profound and luminous. How could he have idly fabricated groundless words or spun useless theories to deceive those who come after?

The *Yi* is imagery. No image is more luminous than that of sun and moon. To fathom the numinous and know transformation: when yang departs, yin arrives; they converge like spokes in a turning wheel, alternating in ebb and flow. The *Yi* is imagery—precisely what the earlier passage meant by "sun and moon forming *yi*."

Master Wei’s purpose in this work was to point out the yin and yang within the body. Yet the body’s yin and yang are formless and ungraspable—ordinary people use them daily without awareness. If not for borrowing images and objects, how could one illuminate them so that others might fathom the numinous and know transformation? Thus, he looked up and down between heaven and earth, where only the images of sun and moon stand out in luminous clarity. By presenting what is manifestly clear, he enabled others to perceive the yin and yang of heaven and earth and silently grasp the wonders of transformation. Then the daily workings of one’s own body, though unspoken, become self-evident.

For sun and moon traverse the ecliptic: one emerges, one retreats; they alternate above and below, intertwining in expansion and contraction, cycling through day and night like a wheel turning endlessly. If one reflects inwardly and extends this insight, one finds that within one’s own body, sun and moon exist no differently than in heaven and earth.

Han Xiaoyao’s *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* states:
*"The Dharma-wheel must turn—always turn—*
*Yet it lies within the body, unseen.*
*When set in motion, it resembles the sun-wheel—*
*The sun-wheel turns toward me, within my body."*
And again:
*"Let the Dharma-wheel turn without cease;*
*Thought by thought, cling not—*
*The wheel turns of itself;*
*What need is there for external seeking?"*

The *Yi* contains three hundred eighty-four lines. "Extracting the tally" refers to the sixty-four hexagrams—a complete *Yi* totals three hundred eighty-four lines, while one *jin* of the great medicine totals three hundred eighty-four *zhu*. This is the resonance between the Dao of the elixir and the Dao of the *Yi*, and thus the *Cantong Qi* was composed.

The lines are the strokes of the hexagrams; the tally is the union of hexagrams. The *Yi* has sixty-four hexagrams. In the elixir method, Qian and Kun serve as the cauldron and vessel, Kan and Li as the medicinal substances, and the remaining sixty hexagrams as the fire phases. Now, observing Qian and Kun: they begin with *Zhun* and end with *Jiji* and *Weiji*, with every inversion and correspondence in between perfectly aligned. Herein lies the Dao: "One yin, one yang." The ancient immortals, by extracting the tally from the lines, metaphorically compared cauldron, medicinal substances, and fire phases—truly ingenious and comprehensive.

From the dark of the moon to the dawn of the new moon, Zhen arrives to receive the tally. At this moment, heaven and earth conjoin their essence, and sun and moon mutually sustain each other. "From the dark of the moon to the dawn of the new moon" refers to the interval between the last and first days of the lunar month.

When *Zhen* arrives to receive the tally, it is Qian interpenetrating Kun to form *Zhen*, wherein the single yang is born within the body. At this moment, spirit and qi intermingle, qi and spirit unite—akin to heaven and earth conjoining their essence, sun and moon merging as one. Thus it is said: "At this moment, heaven and earth conjoin their essence, sun and moon mutually sustain each other." Heaven and earth are the Qian and Kun within our bodies; sun and moon are the Kan and Li within us. As heaven and earth and sun and moon unite in due season, they refine the myriad things to accomplish the year’s work. As Qian and Kun and Kan and Li unite in due season, they seize the transformations of creation to form the sacred embryo.

Master Wei sighed: scholars, failing to comprehend the yin-yang transformations within their own bodies, borrow the moment of sun-moon conjunction as a metaphor. Yet it is not that only the interval between the dark and dawn of the new moon may be employed for inner work. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: "Sun and moon meet once every thirty days; the method of exchanging days for seasons lies in spiritual work." If practitioners grasp the subtlety of exchanging days for seasons, they may seize heaven and earth’s mechanism to complete the Great Elixir.

"Yang, the male, scatters the mysterious; yin, the female, gathers the yellow transformation." "Yang, the male, scatters the mysterious" refers to heavenly qi descending to earth; "yin, the female, gathers the yellow transformation" refers to earth receiving heavenly qi to generate beings. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "Sweet dew descends when heaven and earth unite; the yellow sprout arises when Kan and Li intermingle." The generation of medicinal substances in elixir arts resembles the generation of beings in heaven and earth—both proceed from the intermingling of yin and yang, one scattering, one transforming, to produce the mysterious yellow.

When primordial chaos intermingles, the root and foundation are established. As yin and yang qi ascend and descend to intermingle and merge as one, it is called "primordial chaos." Primordial chaos is the outer enclosure of heaven and earth, the womb of the myriad things. In elixir arts, taking this as the starting point to establish the root and foundation means that heaven, earth, and the myriad things all reside within one’s body, with no need to seek them externally.

Cultivating and nurturing within the *yinen*, spirit congeals to form the body. *Yinen* is the root and stem. When elixir arts are cultivated here and the inner light is turned inward, spirit clings to qi and congeals, qi clings to spirit and abides—naturally interweaving to form the embryo. If the spirit’s light leaks outward and the qi-steed gallops abroad, then sun and moon lose their way, gold and mercury part crucibles. How then may one hope to complete the elixir? The *Baizi Qingzhu Yuye Dansui Ge* says: "In former days, I met my teacher and received the oral transmission: simply focus spirit to enter the qi-cavern." This is why it is called "spirit-elixir" and named "spirit-immortal."

All beings tread this path to emerge, even the wriggling and crawling. The way of yin-yang intermingling is the source of all generation and transformation. Not only do humans and beasts emerge thereby; even the flying, wriggling, crawling, and breathing—down to the most minute creatures—all proceed from this. Thus it is said: "All beings tread this path to emerge; the wriggling and crawling none are without it." Applied to others, it generates people; preserved within oneself, it generates the medicinal substance. The only distinction lies in whether one is sagely or common, moving with or against the current.

Thus Confucius praised the *Hong Meng*, saying: "The virtue of Qian and Kun pervades the empty." Ancient texts esteemed the *Yuan Huang*; the *Guanju* established the beginning. The *Book of Rites* took capping and marriage as weighty; the *Spring and Autumn Annals* made the first year paramount. From this we know that the sages’ composition of classics all had a foundational reference. If one practices elixir arts without investigating their origins, how may one thoroughly comprehend yin-yang and deeply penetrate the transformations of creation?

The sage is not born in vain; above, he observes the celestial tally. The celestial tally has advance and retreat, contraction and extension, in accord with the seasons. The tally is the union: the moon travels through the heavens, once each month uniting with the sun, hence called the celestial tally. The celestial tally has advance and retreat, contraction and extension in accord with the seasons: from the first day of the month onward, its light gradually advances, the *hun* lengthens, the *po* wanes, yang extends, yin contracts—like the progression from midnight to noon. From the sixteenth day onward, its light gradually retreats, the *po* lengthens, the *hun* wanes, yin extends, yang contracts—like the progression from noon to midnight. The perfected of old observed heaven’s way and grasped heaven’s movements, then borrowed the celestial tally’s advance and retreat, yin-yang’s contraction and extension, to establish the method of fire phases as a model for others. For heaven and earth are like a single crucible, sun and moon the medicinal substances. The sun and moon’s movements through heaven and earth—coming and going, rising and setting—are the fire phases. If one can turn inward to seek this within oneself, one may silently grasp the subtlety of the fire phases’ advance and retreat.

Thus the *Yi* unites the heart of heaven; the *Fu* hexagram establishes the beginning. The *Yi* is called "one yin, one yang."

The heart of heaven lies at the center of the northern *zi* hour. The *Fu* hexagram corresponds to the eleventh month. As a hexagram, *Fu* precisely aligns with the central *qi* of the northern *zi* position, responding to the winter solstice. As Shao Kangjie’s *Jirang Ji* states in its *Winter Solstice Chant*: "At the winter solstice, the midpoint of *zi*, the heart of heaven remains unchanged." Zhu Xi praised this poem as most excellent. For the *lidong* solar term falls in the first half of the tenth month, *xiaoxue* in the middle of the tenth, *daxue* in the first half of the eleventh, the winter solstice in the middle of the eleventh, *xiaohan* in the first half of the twelfth, and *dahan* in the middle of the twelfth. Thus, the midpoint of *zi* in the winter solstice is the midpoint of the eleventh month. Some say the midnight *zi* hour is the winter solstice, but this is incorrect, for the first half of the *zi* hour belongs to the preceding period. Most who calculate the Five Phases are unaware of this, yet the reckoning invariably begins here without the slightest deviation—this is why it is called the heart of heaven. Now, when Master Wei established the *Yi* as uniting the heart of heaven and the *Fu* hexagram as marking the beginning, he referred to the yang *qi* lying dormant in the first month of winter, the month of pure yin, beginning beneath the *Kun* hexagram. There, it accumulates into a single yang line, transforming into the *Fu* hexagram. While it is commonly known that the tenth month is *Kun* and the eleventh month sees the emergence of a single yang line beneath five yin lines, forming *Fu*, few realize that the eleventh month’s winter solstice does not spontaneously generate a single yang line to become *Fu*. Rather, in the tenth month’s *xiaoxue*, a yang line has already begun to stir within the lowest line of *Kun*. Yet within a single day or month, it grows by only one-thirtieth. Only after accumulating for a full month, by the eleventh month’s winter solstice, does it complete a single line, forming *Fu*. This, too, is a metaphor. The year takes the winter solstice as *Fu*, the month takes the new moon’s dawn as *Fu*, and the day takes the *zi* hour as *Fu*—all serve to elucidate the transformations within the body, and one need not rigidly adhere to years, months, days, or hours.

The *Cuixu Pian* states: "A month has three decades, a day shares the same cultivation. The alchemical method emulates heaven’s work." For the year corresponds to the month, the month to the day, and the day to the hour. Compressing a year into a month, a month into a day, a day into an hour—within a single hour, there lies the mechanism of a single yang’s return. This mechanism does not reside in the winter solstice, nor in the new moon’s dawn, nor in the *zi* hour. Without profound insight into heaven and earth’s yin and yang, none may know this mysterious mechanism, so secret is it.

The eldest son inherits the father’s body and establishes the foundation through the mother. The *Qian* father’s lowest line intermingles with the *Kun* mother’s first line, forming *Zhen*, which is the eldest son. Thus it is said: "The eldest son inherits the father’s body." *Zhen* arises from *Kun*’s body, just as an infant is born from its mother’s womb. Hence it is said: "Establishes the foundation through the mother." The *Wuzhen Pian* states: "The Golden Duke was originally the Eastern House’s son, sent to the Western Neighbor to inherit the body." This is the same principle. Understanding this, one knows that in refining the elixir, there is no need to seek the winter solstice—within the body, a single yang naturally arises, and one need not cling to the constraints of years, months, days, or hours.

The waxing and waning respond to the *zhong* pitch; ascent and descent follow the Dipper’s pivot. From *zi* to *si* is waxing; from *wu* to *hai* is waning. The waxing and waning respond to the *zhong* pitch: within a year’s twelve months, the pitch pipes change each month, cycling through all twelve. The waxing and waning of the body’s fire phases are the same. From below to above is ascent; from above to below is descent. Ascent and descent follow the Dipper’s pivot: within a day’s twelve hours, the Dipper’s handle shifts each hour, traversing all twelve earthly branches. The body’s fire phases’ ascent and descent are likewise. The *Zhixuan Pian* states: "Vast, the ninefold earth shifts the pitch pipes; dim, the boundless heavens turn the Dipper’s handle." This is but a metaphorical comparison—it does not mean using the length of pitch pipes or the Dipper’s direction as a measure.

On the third day, brightness emerges; *Zhen* receives *geng* in the west. On the third day of the lunar month, the crescent moon appears at dusk in the west, at the *geng* position, resembling *Zhen*. This corresponds to *Zhen*’s reception of *geng*. In terms of the body’s fire phases, this is the moment when the river chariot must not pause even briefly.

On the eighth day, *Dui* receives *ding* at the upper *cui*, level as a stretched cord. By the eighth day, the moon reaches the upper *cui*, appearing at dusk in the south at the *ding* position, resembling *Dui*. This corresponds to *Dui*’s reception of *ding*, symbolizing the halfway ascent of the body’s yang fire.

On the fifteenth, *Qian*’s body is full and complete, *jia* in the east. By the fifteenth, the moon opposes the sun, appearing at dusk in the east at the *jia* position, resembling *Qian*. This corresponds to *Qian*’s reception of *jia*, symbolizing the body’s yang fire at its fullest.

The toad and the hare’s essence—sun and moon shine without dual brightness. The toad is the moon’s essence; the hare’s spirit is the sun’s light. "Sun and moon shine without dual brightness" means the sun illuminates the day, the moon the night. When the sun rises, the moon sets; when the moon rises, the sun sets. Day and night alternate in illumination, their appearances and withdrawals reciprocally unfolding. How is human breath different? As the *Huangting Jing* states: "Exhale the sun, inhale the moon—breath is preserved." For breath is none other than the sun and moon.

The toad observes the hexagram’s node; the hare spits forth radiance. In the alchemical method, the first half of the month is yang, belonging to *Zhen*, *Dui*, and *Qian*; the latter half is yin, belonging to *Xun*, *Gen*, and *Kun*. Thus it is said: "The toad observes the hexagram’s node." The moon is supreme yin; the sun is supreme yang. Yang governs emission; yin governs absorption. The moon itself has no light but receives the sun’s light to become bright. Hence it is said: "The hare spits forth radiance."

At seven and eight, the Dao is complete, bending and descending low. Seven and eight refer to the fifteenth. The yang fire ascends from *Zhen* to the fifteenth, where pure *Qian* reaches its fullness—this is the culmination of the first half of the month. Its momentum is exhausted, its path perilous. Thus, it must inevitably bend, turn, and descend. As Chen Chaoyuan’s *Yuzhi Shu* states: "Within the ninefold city, the dragon chariot departs; before the twelvefold tower, the tiger carriage returns." This is the same principle.

At sixteen, it turns to receive the mandate; *Xun*’s *xin* appears at dawn’s clarity. By the sixteenth day, the moon begins to wane, appearing at dawn in the west at the *xin* position, its brightness just diminished. This resembles *Xun*, corresponding to *Xun*’s reception of *xin*. In terms of the body’s fire phases, this is the moment when yin receives yang’s succession, the peak turning back.

*Gen* aligns with *bing* at the southern lower *cui* on the twenty-third. By the twenty-third day, the moon reaches the lower *cui*, appearing at dawn in the south at the *bing* position, half its brightness diminished. This resembles *Gen*, corresponding to *Gen*’s reception of *bing*, symbolizing the halfway descent of the body’s yin tally.

*Kun*’s *yi* on the thirtieth day in the east guards its brightness. By the thirtieth day, the moon’s brightness is concealed, appearing at dawn in the east at the *yi* position, guarding its light. This resembles *Kun*, corresponding to *Kun*’s reception of *yi*.

By the thirtieth day, the moon becomes *hui* (dark), appearing at dawn in the east at the *yi* position, its light entirely concealed. This resembles *Kun*, corresponding to *Kun*’s reception of *yi*, symbolizing the moment when the body’s yin tally reaches its utmost.

When one phase ends, succession passes to the next, and the corporeal form revives like the dragon. Master Wei divides the thirty days of a month into six phases: from the first day of the new moon to the fifth day is the first phase, belonging to *Zhen*; the sixth to the tenth day is the second phase, belonging to *Dui*; the eleventh to the fifteenth day is the third phase, belonging to *Qian*; the sixteenth to the twentieth day is the fourth phase, belonging to *Xun*; the twenty-first to the twenty-fifth day is the fifth phase, belonging to *Gen*; and the twenty-sixth to the thirtieth day is the sixth phase, belonging to *Kun*. When the six phases conclude, the sun and moon conjoin at the new moon, and yang once more receives yin’s succession, transforming again into *Zhen*. *Zhen* is the dragon—a single yang stirring beneath two yins. Beneath the depths of the abyss, is there not a moving creature? Is this not the dragon?

*Ren* and *gui* pair with *jia* and *yi*; *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end. *Qian* receives *jia* and *ren*, while *Kun* receives *yi* and *gui*. Thus it is said: *Ren* and *gui* pair with *jia* and *yi*. The ten celestial stems begin with *jia* and *yi* and conclude with *ren* and *gui*. Thus it is said: *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end. The *na jia* method is but a metaphor drawn from the fire phases. The waxing and waning of the fire phases differ not from the moon’s waxing and waning. Now, arranging the six hexagrams across a month: *Zhen* corresponds to the third day, when the moon emerges at *geng*; *Dui* to the upper *cui*, when the moon appears at *ding*; *Qian* to the full moon, when the moon is full at *jia*; *Xun* to the sixteenth day, when the moon wanes at *xin*; *Gen* to the lower *cui*, when the moon diminishes at *bing*; and *Kun* to the dark moon, when the moon vanishes at *yi*. This is merely a borrowing to discuss the advance and retreat of the six hexagrams’ fire phases within the body—it is not truly the thirty days of a month.

How can this be clarified? When measured against the calendar, day and night vary in length. If the day is short and the sun sets at *shen*, the moon conjoins at *shen* and reaches fullness at *yin*. If the day is long and the sun sets at *xu*, the moon conjoins at *xu* and reaches fullness at *chen*. Over twelve months, the three-day-old moon does not always appear at *geng*, nor does the fifteen-day-old moon always appear at *jia*. With the new moon’s conjunction varying in timing, the upper and lower *cui* do not invariably fall on the eighth and twenty-third days, nor do the full and dark moons invariably fall on the fifteenth and thirtieth days. When Master Wei speaks of the three-day moon emerging as *shuang*, *Zhen* receiving *geng* in the west, or the sixteenth day’s turning receiving *Xun*’s succession with *xin* appearing at dawn, he refers to the equinoxes when day and night are balanced, using them merely as metaphors—not truly advancing the fire when the moon emerges at *geng* or retreating the tally when the moon wanes at *xin*.

Scholars need only observe the moon’s waxing and waning and reflect upon their own bodies. The arising of one yang within the body is the *Zhen* image of the three-day moon’s birth. The growth of two yangs is the *Dui* image of the eight-day moon’s *cui*. The fullness of three yangs is the *Qian* image of the fifteen-day full moon. The arising of one yin is the *Xun* image of the sixteen-day waning moon. The growth of two yins is the *Gen* image of the twenty-third-day moon’s *cui*. The fulfillment of three yins is the *Kun* image of the thirty-day moon’s concealment. How can one rigidly adhere to the moon’s directions of emergence and setting, regulate them by the hexagrams’ line structures and numbers, or constrain them by the calendar’s methods of expansion and contraction?

Seven and eight make fifteen; nine and six also correspond. Together, the four sum to thirty, matching the thirty days of a month—this is the *Yi*’s image of concealment and disappearance. Seven is the fire number, eight the wood number; combined, they make fifteen. Nine is the metal number, six the water number; combined, they also make fifteen. Together, the four sum to thirty, corresponding to the thirty days of a month. When the thirty-day count concludes, the sun and moon unite as one, and the *Yi*’s image vanishes without trace. The *Fuming* chapter states: “One month, one return, makes one revolution; one year, nine revolutions, nine returns.” It relies solely on the two hexagrams to reckon punishment and virtue, with five and six revolving back to *wu* and *si*. Five and six together make thirty.

This resembles the midwinter node, when grasses and trees wither and decay, aiding yang’s interrogation of *shang*. The traveler and the lord deeply conceal themselves, following the season’s rhythm, closing their mouths and refraining from discoursing on the Dao of Heaven—so vast, so profound, formless and boundless, empty and unseen. To rectify errors and dispel delusions, one must first dissolve the outer frame.

In the alchemical method, the winter solstice and the moments between dark and new moons are all metaphors for the time when yin reaches its extreme and yang is born. Speaking of a month, it is like the dark moon’s night, when the moon’s light vanishes without trace. Speaking of a year, it is like the midwinter node, when grasses and trees wither and decay. Their meaning is one and the same. The *Yi* states: “Thunder lies within the earth—*Fu*. The former kings, on reaching this day, closed the passes, and merchants and travelers did not journey; the lord did not inspect the regions.” This is to cultivate stillness in preparation for movement, aligning with the Dao of Heaven.

Yet the Dao of Heaven is vast and boundless; the true mechanism lies within a single moment. The profound and formless finds its wonder in the hidden and obscure. Thus, at the moment of the winter solstice, one must first seal the *Dui*, calm the heart, and guard silence, causing metal and mercury to return together to the crucible, like the sun and moon uniting as one. Conceal the outer frame, sinking it into the abyssal void, so that spirit congeals, qi gathers, and the golden liquid coagulates. If one forgets this sealing of silence and recklessly lets the heavy tower’s vastness pour forth, one invites only injury and defeat.

This separate arrangement of the four images is to enlighten later generations who are blind. The four images are seven, eight, nine, and six—the same as the “seven and eight make fifteen; nine and six also correspond” mentioned above. Combined, seven, eight, nine, and six make thirty, corresponding to the thirty days of a month—all are metaphorical images. Master Wei, seeing that scholars failed to grasp his meaning and blindly led one another to mistakenly take the waxing and waning of the thirty days as the fire phases of a month, thus separately arranged these four images of seven, eight, nine, and six to enlighten the unenlightened, hoping to reveal that the thirty days of a month are all metaphors—not truly advancing the fire on the third day of the moon or retreating the tally on the sixteenth day. This is like the branches *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*; the earthly branches *zi*, *shen*, *yin*, and *xu*; the seasons of spring, summer, autumn, and winter; the solar terms of solstices, equinoxes, beginnings, and closings; day and night; dawn and dusk; and the return to one’s dwelling—all share this meaning. If scholars understand that seven, eight, nine, and six correspond to the thirty days of a month, they can silently comprehend all other broad metaphors. Why cling to the text and rigidly adhere to the images?

The eight hexagrams are arrayed, and the luminaries move without losing the center.

The center is the Yellow Way. The eight directions are arrayed with the eight trigrams, and the twenty-eight lunar lodges encircle them—this is the path along which the sun and moon travel. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: "Once you drive the two substances back to the Yellow Way, how could the numinous elixir fail to form?" When refining the elixir, if the "monkey of the mind" gallops wildly outward, you must gather your vision, return your hearing, and concentrate your spirit for a moment, so that the two substances return to the Yellow Way without losing the center. Then, in harmonious union, they condense into a single drop of dew, which descends into the Cinnabar Field.

The primordial essence is subtle and hard to perceive; its effects must be inferred and verified. The primordial essence arises from the profound and obscure, invisible to the eye. The *Daodejing* says: "Profound! Obscure! Within it lies essence. Its essence is most real, and within it lies sincerity." The Way of the Great Elixir accords with the transformations of nature. When the celestial sign arrives, the jade bell tolls once, and the grotto of the immortals opens wide—this is its verification. An ancient song says: "Beneath the first green, the moon and sun rise together on either side, merging into one." Without penetrating the yin and yang of heaven and earth or deeply grasping the transformations of one’s own body, how could one hastily proceed?

When dwelling in stillness, observe its images and model its forms. When refining the elixir, externally set up a gnomon and water clock to measure the celestial movements of the sun’s shadow, while internally cultivate the True One to verify the body’s own timekeeping. Only then will you not lose the mechanism of heaven and earth. When spirit is settled and qi harmonized, inner and outer align; when spirit is dull and qi restless, the moments diverge—this cannot be left unregulated.

Set up the gnomon as a standard and observe the signs to determine auspiciousness. The fire times of the Great Elixir do not rely on fixed hours—why set up a gnomon and observe the signs? It is to avoid missing the moment when heaven and humanity jointly activate the mechanism. If one does not guard this mysterious moment but instead busies oneself from dawn to dusk with the sundial and water clock, taking them as the fire times, one errs gravely.

Issue commands in accord with the seasons, and do not miss the time when the lines move. When *Dizhen* (Earth Thunder) stirs and the *Xun* gate opens, the dragon leaps toward the eastern pool—this is the moment when the single yang line stirs within the body. At this time, warm qi surges and merges, and the heart feels a fleeting trance; one must swiftly advance the fire without delay. The *Zhuyu Jihuanyuan* says: "When the wind first rises in the myriad hollows and the moon first rounds the thousand peaks, hasten to issue commands and circulate the Celestial Cycle." This is what it means. The *Zhixuan Pian* says: "Guard the child in the radiant light, and employ the general amid the drumbeats"—this, too, refers to the same.

Above, examine the patterns of the *Hetu*; below, sequence the flows of earth’s forms; in the center, investigate the human heart. Cross-reference and verify the Three Powers. The *Xiqizhen Sanzhu Mingxu* says: "Revolving with the sun and moon, wheeling with the Milky Way, the turning is endless, and life knows no bounds." This means the Way of the Great Elixir aligns with the Way of Heaven. The *Shizhong Yin* in *Xiqizhen* says: "Its wondrous workings flow like rivers, endless in their circling; it nurtures the mysterious valley and irrigates the Jade Pond." This means the Way of the Great Elixir aligns with the Way of Earth.

Why does the Way of the Great Elixir align with heaven and earth? It all lies in the silent operation of this heart. The ancients who refined the elixir looked up to observe the celestial patterns, looked down to examine earth’s principles, and centered their inquiry on the human heart. Thus, they emptied their hearts, moved their spirits, turned the Celestial Pivot, and rotated the Earth’s Axis. Above, they responded to the revolving brilliance of the Milky Way; below, they matched the rising and falling of the ocean tides. Though heaven and earth are vast and their transformations wondrous, the luminous movements of the sun, moon, and stars, the cyclic array of the Five Phases and Eight Trigrams—all were gathered into their own bodies. Some became their crucibles, some their medicinal substances, some their fire times. Turning inward to observe, the Three Powers were complete within them, for they never sought outside their own bodies.

The *Jirang Ji Guanyin* says:
*"One thing comes with one body,
One body still holds one Qian and Kun.
If you know the myriad things are complete within you,
Why set the Three Powers apart as separate roots?
Heaven’s pivot lies within the center,
Creation arises from the heart.
How could heaven and humanity have two different principles?
The Way does not act in vain—it lies deep within the human."*
How could there be any doubt?

When active, follow the changes of the hexagrams; when still, adhere to the judgments of the *Tuan* texts. The *Daodejing* says: "Attain the utmost emptiness, guard stillness firmly. The myriad things arise together, and I observe their return. Things flourish in abundance, each returning to its root. Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called returning to destiny." How perfect! This is why the myriad things return to their destiny.

Within this body lies the great elixir of golden cinnabar—is its embryo nurtured in the midst of utmost stillness and born at the moment when yin reaches its extreme?

The *Book of Changes* says: "Supreme indeed is the primal power of Kun! All things owe their birth to it, for it follows Heaven in due sequence." Kun’s profound capacity to bear all things is a virtue boundless in scope. As Kun is the hexagram of pure yin, the earth, it receives and enfolds Heaven’s qi, and thus the hundred creatures all emerge from the soil. The alchemical method, at the moment of containing light and returning illumination, conceals the spirit within—this is no different from the pure yin of the moon, the closure of winter’s depths. The "Returning to Destiny" chapter says: "Having received the true immortal’s formula, one refines the utmost yang within yin." This is precisely what it means.

Yet when stillness reaches its extreme, movement must follow. In an instant, one yang stirs beneath the six yins, transforming first into *Fu* (Return), then into *Lin* (Approach), *Tai* (Peace), *Dazhuang* (Great Strength), *Guai* (Breakthrough), until at the sixth change it becomes pure *Qian*—the yang qi then pervades the six voids, and a single wheel of the sun emerges from the deep pool.

When Qian and Kun circulate in their workings, Heaven and Earth are then well-ordered. The functions of Qian and Kun are Kan and Li. When Heaven and Earth obtain Kan and Li to operate between them, yin and yang harmonize in great peace, and auspicious qi arises. If for a single day Heaven and Earth were without Kan and Li, the workings of creation would nearly cease. Is the wondrous use of the great elixir any different from the workings of Qian and Kun?

How could one not be cautious? The first stirring of the yang is the beginning of elixir-making—just as it is the beginning of a ruler’s governance. When issuing decrees and commands, how could one not be cautious?

The subtle mechanisms must be tightly sealed, then expansively deployed to distribute the treasure. "Tightly sealed" means the eyes contain their light, the ears concentrate their resonance, the nose regulates its breath, the tongue seals its qi, the feet are crossed in proper sitting, and the spirit is inwardly guarded—without a single thought diverted outward. For when the eyes do not see, essence naturally returns to the liver; when the ears do not hear, essence naturally returns to the kidneys; when the tongue does not speak, spirit naturally returns to the heart; when the nose does not smell, the po soul naturally returns to the lungs; when the four limbs do not move, intent naturally returns to the spleen. Thus, essence resides in the liver yet does not leak through the eyes; the po soul resides in the lungs yet does not leak through the nose; spirit resides in the heart yet does not leak through the mouth; essence resides in the kidneys yet does not leak through the ears; intent resides in the spleen yet does not leak through the four limbs or the nine apertures. When all five are without leakage, spirit, essence, hun, po, and intent blend and fuse, transforming into a single qi that gathers in the *dantian*. When the breath shifts through the nose and the heart suddenly awakens, the Dragon and Tiger surge through the pass, the great path opens, and a single channel flows into the nectar of jade.

The crucial pivot of the Dao is the Dipper’s handle, unifying the warp and woof of transformation. The essence of the elixir Way lies entirely in the Dipper. The Dipper governs the turning of yin and yang throughout the body and commands the myriad transformations within—just as a net has its warp or a garment its fastening. For the three *dantian* are divided in three places; if the mechanism of the Dipper’s handle is activated, true qi circulates up and down like the flow of the Celestial River. This is called the crucial pivot. As the *Fuming Pian* states: "Drive back the Northern Dipper, turn the Celestial Stem; grasp the Southern Asterism and enter the Grotto Chamber"—this is the crux within the crux.

When the lines and images stir within, fortune and misfortune arise without. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "At the moment of receiving qi, it is easy to obtain; in the work of withdrawal and addition, beware of danger." The first yang arises within the body—this is the moment of receiving qi. Though easily attained, if one acts at this time, fortune and misfortune, regret and humiliation arise from movement. Not the slightest deviation is permissible.

The five planets move in disarray, responding to the seasons; the four and seven fall into discord, separating and uniting, rising and bowing. The five phases must be gathered, the metal and fire must share the same crucible—all depends on a single thought to return to the root and revert to the source. Otherwise, the constellations within the tripod will all be misaligned, and Heaven and Earth will be as distant as if suspended apart.

The Literary Star governs and records, interrogating the Terrace and Assistants; the hundred officials and functionaries each oversee their respective domains. The *Records of the Grand Historian: Treatise on the Celestial Offices* states: "The six stars of the Dipper’s bowl are called the Palace of Literary Florescence." Here, "governing and recording" refers to the Crimson Palace’s sovereign, who unites Kun and Qian. Within the human body, where is there a so-called Literary Star? It is nothing but a metaphor. Heaven has its Three Terraces and Four Assistants; the elixir has its Three Dragons and Four Tigers. To interrogate the Terraces and Assistants means that *jia* and *yi* must not lose *geng* and *xin*, nor should there be the slightest error. Heaven has its multitude of stars and their corresponding regions; the elixir has its multitude of hexagrams and fire signs. The hundred officials and functionaries each oversee their domains—this refers to arranging the twenty-four qi of the year, aligning the twenty-eight lunar lodges of the celestial circuit, and meticulously calculating the fire times of the tripod and furnace. From this, it is clear that the myriad images of Heaven all reside within this single body.

The sun contains the essence of the five phases; the moon receives the rhythm of the six pitch-pipes. Five and six make thirty degrees; when the degrees are complete, the cycle begins anew. The sun has fifteen *du* of hardness and fifteen *du* of softness; the moon has twelve pipes, six of yin and six of yang. In the elixir Way, the sun is fire, and "containing the essence of the five phases" means one thing contains five colors. The moon is the medicine, and "receiving the rhythm of the six pitch-pipes" means refining white metal with the six hexagrams. "Five and six make thirty degrees; when the degrees are complete, the cycle begins anew" means the Great Yang moves one degree each day; when it has moved thirty degrees, it once again conjoins with the Great Yin, cycling endlessly without pause.

The beginning and end, the threads of existence and extinction—"beginning" is the new moon; "end" is the dark moon. The "threads of existence and extinction" lie between the dark and new moons, when yin is nearly exhausted yet not entirely so, and yang is about to arise yet has not yet done so. However, this "dark and new moon" is a metaphor—it does not truly refer to the end of the thirtieth day or the beginning of the first day.

If the ruler is arrogant, excessive, and overflowing, violating the Dao; or if the ministers are devious and obsequious, acting contrary to the proper path—then the waxing and waning of the lunar phases will fall into discord, leading to misfortune and calamity. The law enforcers will admonish, criticize, and interrogate, leaving fault with the ruler. The ruler is spirit; the ministers are qi. When making the elixir, lead and mercury return to the earthen crucible; the body and mind must be still and unmoving. For if the body moves, qi scatters; if the mind moves, spirit scatters. Only by concentrating spirit and gathering qi, with heart and breath mutually dependent, can the numinous embryo be formed. Otherwise, the waxing and waning within the body will fall into discord, leading to misfortune and calamity. Tracing the cause, it is because the heart-ruler has become unrestrained and violated the Dao, so the qi-ministers also act deviously and contrary to the proper path. Thus, it is said: "The law enforcers admonish, criticize, and interrogate, leaving fault with the ruler."

When the Celestial Pole is properly aligned, one may roam freely and entrust matters to subordinates. The *Fuming Pian* says: "Beneath the Northern Dipper and Southern Asterism, at the brows and eyelashes, with a mind like ashes, practice water and fire, stabilize the breath, and gather true lead." It also says: "Essence and qi are the original foundation; spirit and numinosity share one household. If you can capture the five thieves, you may naturally unite the Three Flowers." When containing light in silence, one must be continuous yet seemingly present, allowing things to proceed naturally—do not labor the spirit.

The Bright Hall enacts governance, and the state suffers no harm from the Dao. The *Records of the Grand Historian: Treatise on the Celestial Offices* states: "The Eastern Palace’s Azure Dragon, the Chamber and Heart—the Heart is the Bright Hall." In the human body, this is the Grotto Chamber, the Purple Ultimate, the Numinous Gate and Portal. The *Huangting Jing* says: "Between the Bright Hall and Golden Cabinet, the Purple Chamber, the Perfected of Highest Clarity stand before me; the Yellow Skirt Child’s cinnabar qi frequently troubles me. I ask: where is it? Between the two brows, from which light hangs down and shines." Thus, the ruler sits in the Bright Hall to enact governance, communicating the Dao to the nine barbarian tribes and the eight wild regions. The *Fuming Pian* says: "The Grotto Gate is ever tranquil, illuminating Penglai, guarding eternal spring"—this is the same principle.

Inwardly, one nurtures the self through tranquility and emptiness. Wang Chongyang’s *Complete Reality Collection* says: "The wondrous use of the Mysterious Pass is not difficult to fathom—it lies only in wordless silence." For when the heart is at peace and empty, the Dao naturally comes to reside; when emptiness is extreme and stillness profound, the original yang true qi naturally returns.

Return to the root and illuminate within to transform the body. The *Complete Reality Collection* says: "To penetrate, one must search for the origin; to see, one must look within." Returning to the root and reverting to the source, turning the light inward—this is the great principle of cultivation. The former Confucians said: "The Daoist method of nurturing life is to withdraw the gaze and return the vision, like metal and water concealing their light within." How true are these words!

Seal the mouth and fortify the numinous root. The mouth is *dui*; the numinous root is the Kan Palace. Seal the mouth so that the upper does not leak; fortify the numinous root so that the lower does not escape. The *Taiqing Jing* of Master Yin says: "Conceal the heart in the abyss to beautify the numinous root." For when spirit does not stray outward, harmonious qi fills and beautifies the within.

The Three Lights sink into obscurity to nurture the Child Pearl. The Three Lights are the numinous images of the Cavern Chamber. If one can gather the Three Lights and reflect them inward, then the central true position will produce the Mysterious Pearl. The *Huangting Jing* says: "The Cavern Chamber’s numinous images—the Dipper, sun, and moon—shine with the Three Lights, illuminating the Chamber of the Child." Those with clear eyes, pray consider this.

Seen yet unseen, near yet easily sought—
The Golden Elixir, the great medicine, lies only before the eyes. Is it not near? Yet why is it unseen, ungrasped? Though near, it is as distant. If one obtains the method and seeks it, then in but a moment’s effort, golden blossoms fill the sight—how could it be far? The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "The effort is easy, the medicine not remote." Once revealed, one must laugh—laugh at how near and easily sought it is, yet people seek it in the lofty and distant.

The *Fuming Pian* says: "Clearly, it lies only before the eyes—yet people of this age do not see it." Precisely because they do not see, they abandon the internal to pursue the external: some gather the essence of the sun and moon, some refine metals, stones, herbs, and woods, some practice chamber arts—truly, "the Dao is near yet sought afar, the task is easy yet sought in difficulty." How lamentable!

The Yellow Center gradually opens, its principle moistening and suffusing the skin. When the Elixir Field contains substance and the one qi flows unimpeded, harmony accumulates within and radiant splendor manifests without.

At the outset, rectify the foundation—then the end of cultivation may be grasped; establish the root, and the branch may be held. When the exterior is upright, the shadow is straight; when the source is deep, the flow is long. Attain the One, and the myriad affairs are complete.

This One is what veils the world—none know it. The Ancestor of the Great Dao arises from nothing but qi to accomplish transformation: divided, it becomes black and white; separated, it becomes blue and yellow. Metaphorically, it is called sun and moon; named, it is called dragon and tiger. All these manifold forms are but the two words *yin* and *yang*—yet in truth, they are a single thing.

The names "divine water" and "flower pool," the designations "crucible" and "earth cauldron"—all refer to one place. Some say it is the *zi* hour of the Winter Solstice; others, the interval between the last and first days of the lunar month. People do not know that the return of the first yang must occur at the *zi* hour of the Winter Solstice, or that the union of sun and moon must take place between the last and first days of the month. Thus, they pore over calendars, count the hours, and err gravely.

Yet who knows that within the compression of years into months, months into days, days into hours—even within a single hour—lies the creation of a year, a month? Still, that hour is but one place; that place, but one thing.

Thus, the Winter and Summer Solstices, the Spring and Autumn Equinoxes, the *mao* and *you* hours, the *jia* and *geng* days, the last and first days of the lunar month, the waxing and waning phases, the *zi* and *wu* hours, the *ji* and *hai* days, the *zi* and *shen* hours, the *yin* and *xu* hours—all twenty-four solar terms and seventy-two seasonal markers, the annual conjunctions and monthly revolutions, the moments of *li* and *kan*, the months of the Hare and the Rooster, the cavities of *xun* and *qian*, the gates of the two eights, the *zhun* of dawn and the *pi* of dusk, the *gou* of day and the *fu* of night—all reside within this single center. As the *Chuixu Pian* states:

*"Later, it returns as before—
Go ask those wild fox spirits how many they’ve deceived.
None know this single place,
All mistaking side doors for the true Way."*

How many in this world truly know this One?

The *Daodejing* says: *"The highest virtue is non-action, without deliberate seeking; the lesser virtue acts, its function unceasing."* Here, "the highest virtue is non-action, without deliberate seeking" means the spirit abides in the Mysterious Palace in silent non-doing. "The lesser virtue acts, its function unceasing" means *qi* rises in the Gate of the Female, continuous and unbroken. This borrows Laozi’s words to reveal the secret of the interpenetration of *kan* and *li*.

When the upper gate is closed, it is called "having"; when the lower gate is closed, it is called "nothing." "Nothing" serves "having"; above, the spirit-virtue dwells within these two apertures, following the mutual necessity of having and nothing. "When the upper gate is closed, it is called 'having'"—the *li* Palace has form, concealing true Water. "When the lower gate is closed, it is called 'nothing'"—the *kan* Gate is formless, the Crimson Dragon hidden. "Nothing" serves "having"; above, the spirit-virtue dwells—when the Original Spirit rests in its palace, *qi* follows and ascends. Thus, the wonder lies in the eight gates being firmly sealed. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says:

*"The eldest son sips wine from the West,
The youngest daughter first opens the Northern flowers.
If the green moths meet thereafter,
The Yellow House’s gates must be locked at once."*

The Yellow House is the palace where the Original Spirit resides. "These two apertures follow the mutual necessity of having and nothing"—if the lower is not closed, fire does not gather; if the upper is not closed, the medicine does not rise. As the *Wuzhen Pian* states:

*"Send it back to the earthen crucible, seal it tightly,
Then let the flowing pearl unite in proper match."*

What are the "flowing pearl" and "earthen crucible"? They are alternate names for the two cavities of *kan* and *li*.

*"Know the white, guard the black—
The numinous will come of itself."*

"White" is the essence of Metal; "black" is the foundation of Water. "White" is Metal; "black" is Water. In the elixir method, Water is the foundation, and the essence of Metal is born within Water. Thus, to "know the white" is only to guard the black; when the black is guarded, the white appears of itself. How to guard it? Like a cat watching its hole, waiting for the mouse—its body unmoving, its eyes unblinking, its mind fixed solely on the mouse, without any other thought. The "numinous" is the heavenly mechanism. To steal this mechanism, one must first empty the heart. When the heart is empty, the spirit congeals; when the spirit congeals, the breath settles; when the breath settles, both sides ascend as one, and the numinous clarity comes of itself. As the *Li Sao Yuan You* says:

*"Without disturbance to guard it,
It will naturally be one.
The spirit’s aperture is open—
In the midnight hour, preserve emptiness to await it,
Non-action comes first."*

This aligns with the commentary of Master Zhu of Ziyang: *"Master Guangcheng’s instruction to the Yellow Emperor was nothing more than this—truly the essential secret of immortals."*

Water is the pivot of the Dao, its number named One, the beginning of *yin* and *yang*. It contains the Yellow Sprout, master of the Five Metals, the Northern River Chariot. Thus, the crucible is black without but holds golden radiance within—*wearing coarse cloth while hiding jade*. The outer appears mad, yet Water is the pivot of the Dao, the beginning of *yin* and *yang*. Why? Heaven’s One generates Water, which stands first among the Five Phases. Water’s One, when added to Earth’s Five, becomes Water’s completion number—is this not the image of the Mysterious containing the Yellow Sprout? The "Mysterious containing the Yellow Sprout" is Lead born within Water. Lead is the master of the Five Metals, produced within the Northern Mysterious Barrier when Earth is added. The Yellow Sprout is the golden radiance, the essence of the crucible. Thus, the crucible’s body is black without, while golden radiance is concealed within—like a priceless treasure hidden in a coarse-clad man’s embrace. The Northern River Chariot is the Emperor’s Chariot, so named because it wheels along the Celestial River. Now, when true Lead is born within Water and refined by true Fire, the River Chariot does not dare to pause in its turning but ascends to the summit of Kunlun Peak.

Metal is the mother of Water; the mother conceals the child’s embryo. Water is the child of Metal; the child harbors the mother’s womb. Metal generates Water, hence "Metal is the mother of Water." Water is the child of Metal; Water contains Metal, hence "the mother conceals the child’s embryo, the child harbors the mother’s womb." If scholars understand the meaning of Metal within Water, they know the source of the medicine’s production.

The Perfected One is most wondrous—seeming to exist, yet not; faintly resembling the great abyss, now sinking, now floating. The Perfected One is the Original Spirit. "Seeming to exist, yet not" means it is continuous yet not forced, faintly present. "Faintly resembling the great abyss, now sinking, now floating" describes the Perfected One’s comings and goings in the great abyss—vague and indistinct, floating and sinking without fixed form. As the *Jindan Dacheng Ji* of Xiao Zixu says: *"On Kunlun’s peak, the Yellow Flower’s path—at times leading the Original Spirit away, then returning."* The "great abyss" is Kunlun.

Advance and retreat, distribution and arrangement—each guards its own domain. In the elixir method, one advances from *zi* and retreats from *wu*; advance and retreat, distribution and arrangement—each guards its own boundary, not overstepping. As Yang Xubai’s *Jindan Chun Cui Yin* says: *"Before the *zi-wu* furnace, advance and retreat are divided; within the *qian-kun* crucible, floating and sinking are distinct."*

Gather the white of *kan* and refine it into the vermilion. "White" is the white Metal; "vermilion" is the vermilion Mercury. "Gather the white of *kan*" means to gather the white Metal from the Northern *kan* Palace as the elixir’s mother—white Metal and vermilion Mercury are of the same root and follow one another. "Refine it into the vermilion" means daily applying the Fire to withdraw the Lead’s *qi* and add Mercury in its place. As Chen Momo’s *Chongzheng Pian* says:

*"Within, guard the River Chariot in the Northern Palace—
Since ancient times, Heaven’s One, Water, comes first.
When the tally matches, the Fire of the Zhou Heaven is established,
The meaning akin to a ferryboat crossing to the other shore.
Day and night, the turning is ceaseless,
The subtle mechanism of the armillary sphere.
Cultivating elixir, the superior scholar—
Only by understanding this method can one know true Lead,
Not needing Lead."*

Scholars merely know that the medicine is formed by the union of Lead and Mercury, yet do not understand the Fire’s work of withdrawing Lead and adding Mercury. Thus, even if the elixir’s head is formed, it cannot avoid dissipation.

Refine it to become the outer defense, the white within steadfastly dwelling. "Refine it to become the outer defense" means the true Fire operates in the outer furnace. "The white within steadfastly dwelling" means the golden elixir rests in the inner furnace. The inner furnace’s elixir requires the outer furnace’s Fire, refined and nurtured day and night, before it matures. As Hao Guangning’s *Taigu Ji* says: *"At the crown, the Black Pearl’s radiance appears; in the abdomen, the secret treasure shines resplendent—is this not the Fire operating without while the elixir dwells within?"*
