A square and circle, spanning but an inch, blend and support one another—born before Heaven and Earth, towering in majestic height. Flanking it are ramparts and gates, resembling Penglai’s isles. Encircled and sealed, the four directions flow unimpeded; vigilant defense ensures steadfast security, barring all wickedness. Winding pavilions connect, ever watchful against the unforeseen.

The "square and circle, spanning but an inch" refers to the Mud-Pill Palace. Today, people merely call the heart "the square inch," unaware that the three fields of the human body are all hollow, each spanning an inch, with qi and vessels interconnected. "Blend and support one another" means the head contains nine palaces, with the Mud-Pill at their center. As the *Huangting Jing* states: *"The Mud-Pill’s nine spirits each have their chambers; the square and circle, spanning an inch, reside therein."* "Born before Heaven and Earth" signifies that the Mud-Pill’s single cavity is the ancestral aperture of the body’s myriad orifices—when this aperture opens, all others follow. "Towering in majestic height" means the Mud-Pill Palace sits atop Kunlun’s peak, the abode of the primordial spirit, corresponding above to the Dark Capital, the realm where the ten thousand spirits gather.

"Flanking it are ramparts and gates" refers to the seven orifices arrayed around it, with the Cavern Chamber and Yellow Gates positioned before it. "Resembling Penglai’s isles" likens it to the three immortal mountains upon the sea, with Penglai at their summit. "Encircled and sealed" means withdrawing vision and returning hearing, carefully closing the senses. "The four directions flow unimpeded" describes the primordial harmony circulating internally, qi flowing freely as the Vermilion Bird encircles the Jade Terrace in flight. "Vigilant defense ensures steadfast security" is like the dragon nurturing its pearl, mind unwavering, or the hen brooding its egg, qi unbroken. "Barring all wickedness" means that though demonic realms may encroach, one wields the axe with self-assurance. "Winding pavilions connect, ever watchful against the unforeseen" means that if the spirit departs, it must be recalled, with twin youths ever guarding the cavern’s gate.

Thus, one may be free from anxious thought but must not succumb to toil and weariness. Tan Changzhen’s *Shuiyun Ji* states: *"Do not hang threads upon the numinous terrace; within, the spirit embryo coalesces—only when the mind is without action does qi harmonize, and when qi harmonizes, the supreme treasure forms. When the mind acts, qi scatters, and when qi scatters, the essence dissipates."* Therefore, one may be free from thought but must not indulge in toil.

When spirit and qi fill the chamber, none can retain them—those who guard them prosper; those who lose them perish. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, they ever accompany one. Through long accumulation and sustained effort, primordial harmony overflows. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, one must ever guard them—guarding them brings prosperity; losing them brings ruin. One must never, even for a moment, depart from vigilance.

As for those who delve into the esoteric, practicing inner visualization while lost in thought, treading the Dipper and pacing the asterisms, invoking the Six Jia, observing the days and hours, or employing the Yin Path’s suppression of the Nine Ones—all these muddle the primordial womb. Those who consume qi, expelling the old and drawing in the new, cause their intestines and stomachs to groan emptily. Those who sit in stubborn vacuity suffer through sleepless days and nights; those who exert themselves in labor disregard bodily exhaustion. Some shake their heads, roll their eyes, clench their fists, and strain their strength, only to set their hundred vessels seething, giving rise to abscesses and ulcers. Others heap earth to erect altars, worshipping at dawn and sacrificing at dusk, only to fall prey to demonic unions in their dreams, ensnared by malevolent spirits. At first, their hearts rejoice, believing they will surely prolong their years, yet suddenly, midway, they meet untimely death, their forms decaying and exposed. All these actions are perverse and contrary, blatantly violating the Yellow Emperor’s texts, their conduct errant and wholly at odds with the Old Master’s safeguards. Clinging to delusion, they reach this end—who else is to blame for their transgressions?

Thus, Wang Liangqi composed the *Breaking Delusion Song*, and Chen Niniwan wrote the *Luofu Lament*, enumerating the errors of the world’s heterodox arts in torrents of two or three thousand words, lamenting that later generations fail to grasp the true path and err in treading deviant ways. If today’s scholars could but peruse the words of these two masters once, they would surely recognize their errors and inwardly reflect.

The enlightened will examine their defenses and suddenly understand the proper course.

Lord Wei composed this *Triplex Unity*, radiant as a torch illuminating a darkened thoroughfare, precise as a needle guiding through a bewildering sea—his intent being that later enlightened ones, through these words, might fortify their resolve and suddenly comprehend the path of cultivation. Lord Wei’s heart may truly be called benevolent. Yet some dismiss his work as mere words on paper, never once fixing their gaze upon it—this is indeed self-abandonment, self-neglect; is it not lamentable?

"Diligently practice it, ceaselessly day and night." Qiu Changchun’s *Mingdao Collection* states: "It is not through mere sitting or walking at dawn and dusk, twisting and contorting, that one forms the crucible. The great elixir of ninefold transmutation—golden liquid cinnabar—differs entirely from the minor comforts of heterodox methods. One must sever worldly affairs, concentrate the mind, and devote oneself wholly, practicing day and night without respite; only then can success be attained." Peng Zhenyi did not say in vain: "The aspirant must cast aside sensual pleasures, forsake desires, renounce fame and profit, retreat to sacred mountains, sever ordinary ties, and cultivate in seclusion, diligently day and night without slack. Only thus may one harbor hope. Otherwise, all effort is in vain." This teaching is abundantly clear. Yet some, blind to this truth, insist on limiting their practice to the two daily periods of firing, and upon hearing the admonition to practice ceaselessly day and night, they recoil in astonishment and ridicule, deeming it unbearably laborious and vexing—how utterly thoughtless!

The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Those who cling rigidly to form may not attain the true; dawn practice abandoned by dusk is not sincere. One cannot lightly accept the notion that the two daily periods suffice for firing." Formerly, Huangfu Lüdao visited Wang Sanyi, who set out counting rods and an hourglass. Asked their purpose, he replied, "To mark the two cardinal points of noon and midnight." Huangfu said, "Among the twelve hours, should there be any moment of interruption? Why limit it to noon and midnight?" In essence, the Dao of the elixir and the Dao of Heaven are one. Does the Dao of Heaven ever pause, even for a single breath? If the Dao of Heaven never ceases for a breath, how can the Dao of the elixir tolerate even a moment’s interruption?

Ma Danyang’s *Gradual Awakening Collection* says: "The master’s grace is profound and difficult to repay; I vow to encircle the walls and refine the true elixir to the death." "Encircling the walls" means cutting off human contact to focus solely on cultivation. After three years of ingesting the elixir, one may ascend and wander afar, walking through fire unscathed, entering water undampened, able to preserve or relinquish life, ever joyful and carefree. When the Dao is perfected and virtue accomplished, one conceals oneself to await the summoning of the Great One, relocating to the Central Land. When merit is complete, one ascends, receives the register, and is granted the chart.

Refining the elixir requires but a moment’s effort—why then speak of three years of ingestion? The *Cui Xu Pian* clarifies: "A moment’s effort refines and instantly manifests aged completion. One must pass three years, for after the moment of refinement, the elixir must be nurtured and warmed; one cannot abruptly depart afar." Zhongli Quanyang’s *Thirty-Nine Pointers to the Mysterious* states: "The infant, young and unformed, must rely on the mother’s nurturing grace." This is correct. But after these three years, one moves like drifting clouds, strides like the wind, can ascend lightly and wander afar. Though the great flood reaches the heavens, one does not drown; though severe drought melts metal and stone, scorches earth and mountains, one remains unharmed. Life and death are at one’s command, and everlasting joy is attained.

Yet once the nurturing is complete, one must still conceal oneself among people, accumulate merit, and amass virtue to enlighten and deliver the deluded masses before one may ascend in flight. Thus, [the text is lacunose] Linggen entrusted Huangfu Lüdao to guide later disciples; Jinhua Dongshan admonished Yang Xubai not to seclude himself but to benefit all beings before ascending as an immortal. How could one attend only to one’s own perfection?

The *Fire Record* is not falsely composed; the *Yi* is expounded to clarify it. Of old, there were six hundred chapters of the *Fire Record*—why so many? Peng Zhenyi’s *Golden Key to the Returned Elixir* states: "The six hundred chapters of the *Fire Record* are all similar." Thus, the so-called six hundred chapters are like the sixty hexagrams: the numbers of firing correspond to the sixty hexagrams, and when extended, they become six hundred chapters. The sixty hexagrams represent the phases of a single month, while the six hundred chapters represent those of ten months. Hence it is said, "The *Fire Record* is not falsely composed; the *Yi* is expounded to clarify it."

The crescent moon forms the crucible and furnace; the White Tiger is the pivot of refinement; mercury is the flowing pearl. The Green Dragon rises with it, east joins west, and the essences mutually constrain one another. The crescent moon furnace is the lead furnace, its position in Kan, where the White Tiger resides. The flowing pearl palace is the mercury crucible, its position in Li, where the Green Dragon resides. The White Tiger as the pivot of refinement means the White Tiger resides in the cinnabar field, and fire issues from below the navel. The Green Dragon rising with it means driving the Green Dragon down to meet the White Tiger. East and west are Kan and Li; the essences are the sun and moon.

The origins of dragon and tiger and the sources of lead and mercury are not two separate paths. Wang Yixuan’s *Red Dragon Elixir Formula* states: "If one discerns the dragon following the tiger and knows mercury resides in lead, then the adept, understanding the secret of driving the dragon to meet the tiger, will grasp the method of merging mercury with lead." The *Fuming Pian* says: "The master points to the Green Dragon mercury, matching it with the White Tiger lead. The two essences are conjoined, water and fire refined for a year." And again: "Once dragon and tiger unite in mutual embrace, Kan and Li couple and form the embryo. A molten handful of the marrow of Heaven and Earth—seek it earnestly and draw it near to sip." The method of the elixir is supremely simple and easy: merely unite the essence of the Green Dragon of the east with the spirit of the White Tiger of the west, and east and west are no longer separate. Naturally, dragon and tiger mate, essences merge, and the purple gold is produced.

The *Jinbi Longhu Jing*, with annotations by Wang Baoyi, explains: "When the medicine is refined to the point of emergence, water and fire have already merged. Once water and fire unite, the dragon and tiger qi within the crucible harmonize and form the golden liquid." This elucidates the matter clearly. When refining the elixir, if one can empty the mind, still the thoughts, concentrate the spirit, and enter the qi cavity, then east and west unite, metal and wood merge, south and north blend, water and fire achieve balance, and the four images and five phases gather in one place, congealing without any need for exertion.

Above, the trigram Dui governs the number eight; below, the trigram Gen also governs eight. The two eights combine their essence, and the body of Qian and Kun is formed. Two eights correspond to one jin; the Dao of the *Yi* is perfectly balanced, not deviating by a single zhu. Three hundred eighty-four corresponds to the number of hexagrams and lines. When the upper firing period is governed by Dui, the moon is half bright—hence it is said, "Above, Dui governs the number eight." When the lower firing period is governed by Gen, the moon is half dark—hence it is said, "Below, Gen also governs eight." Observing the two firing periods together, the crucible of Qian and Kun is established, and medicine, materials, and firing are all contained within.

The two eights correspond to one jin, and the Dao of the *Yi* is perfectly balanced, for the moon has an upper and lower firing period. The upper firing period, governed by metal, weighs half a jin, totaling one hundred ninety-two zhu; the lower firing period, governed by water, also weighs half a jin, totaling one hundred ninety-two zhu. Together, they amount to three hundred eighty-four zhu. The *Yi* has sixty-four hexagrams, each with six lines: one hundred ninety-two yang lines and one hundred ninety-two yin lines, totaling three hundred eighty-four lines. Master Wei borrows this to expound the subtlety of the elixir Way, intending nothing more than the perfect balance and correspondence of yin and yang—there is no actual three hundred eighty-four zhu, nor are there three hundred eighty-four lines.

The *Wuzhen Pian* states, "Two eights correspond, naturally uniting in intimacy." The *Chensu Yangzhi Daopian* says, "In my crucible’s silence, I harmonize the two eights." For "two eights" means two sets of eight *liang*, equal in weight, neither inclined nor biased. If one were to rigidly adhere to zhu and lines, counting breaths while fingering prayer beads, this would be self-inflicted toil—how could it be the Way of utmost simplicity and ease?

Moreover, the *Fuming Pian* says, "When square and round gather, things divide in pairs; both ascend, sharing one jin." The *Jindan Dacheng Ji* states, "In the gate of two eights, those who comprehend are rare; the firing periods before and after are precisely timed." This refers to the *xun* gate within the body, not the upper eight *liang* and lower eight *liang* of the firing periods.

When gold enters fierce fire, its color remains unaltered, its essence luminous. Since the opening of heaven and earth, the sun and moon have never waned in brightness; gold has never lost its weight. The moon’s form endures as ever, for gold is born of the sun. At dawn, it receives the sun’s tally; at dusk, it returns to its mother, the moon, embracing and concealing its frame, sinking into the cavernous void. Gold regains its original nature, and the crucible’s radiance blazes.

All things in the world perish in fire, yet true gold does not. Among the five phases, generation culminates in metal, which is most precious. When smelted, it yields to water; when struck, it yields to fire. Its softness resembles wood, its color resembles earth—water, fire, wood, and earth, all four natures are complete within it. Thus, true gold grows ever firmer through repeated refinement, never losing the weight of its original body. Since the opening of heaven and earth, countless millennia have passed, yet the forms of the sun and moon remain unchanged, their brightness never diminished. For the essence of yin and yang each dwells in its proper abode, and thus the sun and moon endure eternally. Among celestial phenomena, none are more luminous than the sun and moon; in the art of the elixir, none are more luminous than gold and fire.

Gold is the moon; fire is the sun. People know only that gold is the moon’s light, yet they do not know that the moon’s light is born of the sun. The moon, being Greater Yin, has substance but no light; its waxing and waning depend on the amount of sunlight it receives. At the new moon, it first conjoins the sun; after three days, its light begins to grow; by the eighth day, the upper firing period, its light is half; by the fifteenth day, it is full. After the full moon, it gradually wanes; by the twenty-third day, the lower firing period, it is half dark; by the thirtieth day, it is completely dark.

At the new moon’s dawn, as it separates from the sun, it receives the sun’s light and begins to brighten, its body gradually whitening. At dusk, it embraces the sun, its essence lingering while its light vanishes, its body turning entirely black.

In the elixir Way, this is explained as follows: gold, born of the sun, receives the sun’s tally at dawn—True Yin gold arises in the Kun Palace, transformed by the True Yang fire. Its initial reception of qi is like the moon receiving the sun’s light, beginning at the new moon. When gold returns to its mother, embracing the moon at dusk, it is like gold in the Kun Palace guarding the fire, akin to the moon at dusk embracing the sun. When the crucible’s frame sinks into the cavernous void, spirit and qi deeply enter their root, gradually descending to the sea bottom, like the moon’s essence disappearing from sight. When gold regains its original nature and the crucible’s radiance blazes, the moon’s body, originally black, becomes white upon receiving the sun’s light. Now, embracing the sun and concealing its frame, it returns to its original blackness. By guarding its blackness and not seeking its whiteness, after long perseverance, spirit and illumination naturally arrive. In an instant, light and flame penetrate the crucible; the fire’s power blazes intensely. Then, on the third day, *geng* is born, the Dui gate opens, and black silver melts, yielding white silver.

The numbers of *zi* and *wu* combine to make three; the numbers of *wu* and *si* total five. When three and five harmonize, the eight minerals are properly ordered. *Zi* water is one, *wu* fire is two; the numbers of *zi* and *wu* combine to form three, the number of earth. Thus, the central *wu* and *si* are called five. Three and five together make eight—hence the term "eight minerals." When three and five harmonize, water, fire, and earth unite as one. Though earth overcomes water and water overcomes fire, how can they harmonize? Now, earth acts as the mediator between water and fire, reconciling and uniting them, so that water and fire become husband and wife—thus they harmonize. When the eight minerals are properly ordered, water, fire, and earth unite as one, and the guiding principles are set right, all things fall into place.

Breathing contains and nurtures; pausing, they become husband and wife. The ascent of yang and descent of yin—this is the breathing of heaven and earth.

Within it, heaven and earth breathe—thus they endure. If one can emulate heaven and earth’s breathing within, one may likewise share in their endurance.

The *Jindan Dacheng Ji* states: "Exhaling connects the heart and lungs; inhaling connects the kidneys and liver. Exhale, and the dragon chants as clouds arise; inhale, and the tiger roars as wind stirs. Through this breathing, wind and clouds congeal into golden liquid—this is the natural respiration of the single *qi*, not the breathing of mouth and nose."

The *Hunyuan Baozhang* of Li Changyuan says: "At the place of the True Man’s breathing, let the maiden come and go in flight. Empty the heart, concentrate the spirit, turn the light inward, and observe at the True Man’s breathing—follow its ascent and descent, comply with its spontaneity, and abide in it for a moment. When breathing contains and nurtures, stillness arises of itself, then all coalesces into one, husband and wife unite."

What is the place of the True Man’s breathing? Liao Chanhui’s *Sancheng Neipian* says: "Facing the navel wheel, behind the kidneys, at the center lies the True Golden Tripod—this is the place of the True Man’s breathing."

The *Huangting Jing* says: "Behind is the secret gate, before is the gate of life. Sun emerges, moon enters—breathe and abide in the person." Yet most do not reach this, so their breathing does not reside here.

The *Zhuangzi*’s *Nanhua Zhenjing* says: "The True Man breathes from the heels; the multitude breathe from the throat." Breathing from the throat is shallow and short; breathing from the heels is long and continuous. In cultivation, when the breath becomes profoundly deep, one’s life rests in oneself, no longer molded by the great forge.

The *Huanyuan Pian* says: "Below the heart, above the kidneys, west of the liver, left of the lungs—neither intestines nor stomach, a single *qi* flows freely through it." This is clearly and directly pointed out—what doubt remains? What doubt remains?

Yellow earth is the father of metal, flowing pearl the mother of water. Water takes earth as its dam; earth fills water so it does not rise. The Vermilion Bird is the essence of fire, holding the balance to adjust victory and defeat. If water surges, fire is extinguished—both perish and return to the thick earth. When the three natures unite, they guard their original nature and share a common ancestor.

Earth dwells at the center, hence it is called yellow earth. Earth generates metal, so it is the father of metal. Flowing pearl is mercury—wandering and restless, it is called flowing pearl. When flowing pearl enters water, it congeals into true gold. Gold generates water, so flowing pearl is the mother of water. Now, when earth enters water, water is restrained by earth and cannot bear metal upward, so it is said: "Water takes earth as its dam; earth fills water so it does not rise." If the Vermilion Bird’s fire from the south is used to fiercely boil and intensely forge, fire flourishes and water boils, causing the metal to ascend with the water.

The Vermilion Bird is the essence of southern fire, governing summer. Now, when Kan water is restrained by earth’s dam, fire can boil it, making it rise. Thus it is said: "The Vermilion Bird is the essence of fire, holding the balance to adjust victory and defeat." When boiling water ascends into the southern Li palace, Li fire is in turn overcome by Kan water. If fire is extinguished by water, water must not overflow. Thus, earth overcomes water, and water, restrained by earth, stops. It is said: "If water surges, fire is extinguished—both perish and return to the thick earth." When water and fire encounter earth, this is called the three natures. Once the three natures guard and unite, they merge into one and all return to the Kun palace. Thus it is said: "When the three natures unite, they guard their original nature and share a common ancestor."

*Giant sesame* still extends life; *returned elixir* may be ingested. Metal’s nature does not decay, so it is the treasure of the myriad things. When alchemists consume it, their lifespan is prolonged.

Giant sesame is sesame—consuming it still extends life. The nine-times-cycled great returned elixir is the flourishing essence of metal, secretly drawing the upright *qi* of heaven and earth to form the sacred embryo. How could it not grant long life? Moreover, metal’s nature is firm and unyielding, enduring ten thousand years without decay, so it is the supreme treasure among the myriad things. Rightly do immortals value it. Thus, it is not called the elixir of water, fire, wood, or earth, but uniquely named the *golden elixir*.

Earth travels through the four seasons, guarding boundaries and setting rules.

Earth has no fixed position but circulates among Chen, Xu, Chou, and Wei. In Chen, it is late spring; in Wei, late summer; in Xu, late autumn; in Chou, late winter. Spring, summer, autumn, and winter are all places where earth travels, so it is said: "Earth travels through the four seasons." Earth can exhaust water and conceal fire. Guarding water, water does not flow; guarding fire, fire does not flame. Now, using it to guard Kun while gathering medicine, and to guard Qian while circulating fire—thus it is said: "Guarding boundaries and setting rules."

When golden sand enters the five viscera, mist scatters like wind and rain.

When golden sand ascends the tripod, it pierces the two kidneys, guides the spine, passes through the heart meridian, enters the sea of marrow, rushes through the lung’s transport point, traverses the liver, passes the spleen, and returns to the cinnabar field. As it ascends, it billows like clouds and mist filling the four directions; suddenly, it arrives like a violent wind and rain. Vaguely, it is like awakening from a daydream; clearly, it is like a grave illness leaving the body. Spirit and energy unite like husband and wife in embrace; bones and flesh melt and harmonize like rising from a bath. These are all true scenes, not metaphors.

The *Fuming Pian* says: "Not knowing, one might think *qi* arrives afterward—only through direct experience can it be known."

Fumigating and steaming reach the four limbs; complexion becomes radiant and lustrous. White hair all turns black; fallen teeth regrow in their original places. Old men return to youth; aged women become maidens. Their form changes, freeing them from worldly calamities—they are called True Men.

After obtaining the elixir, nurture it day and night without interruption. Then true *qi* fumigates and steams the four limbs, naturally refreshing spirit and energy, brightening the complexion. White hair returns to black; fallen teeth regrow. Old men become youths; aged women become maidens. From this, they transform their form, transcend the world, leap from the cage, and are called True Men.

Moreover, it has been said: if humans stand alongside heaven and earth as the three powers, they may share their beginning and end. Now then...

Heaven and earth are without end, yet human lifespan does not reach a hundred years—why is this? It is because humans, within heaven and earth, are but one thing among many. Though more numinous than other creatures and thus called "human," how can they truly stand alongside heaven and earth? Yet if one seizes the mechanism of heaven and earth to refine the Golden Elixir of Liquid Gold, then one may share their beginning and end—this is called a True Man. Having become a True Man, one transcends the mundane and enters sagehood, no longer bound to their former form. Roaming beyond the world of things, they forever escape the calamities of the dusty realm.

Lead powder cast into fire loses its color and reverts to lead; ice and snow, when warmed by hot water, melt and become primordial water. Cinnabar serves as the foundation, harmonizing with mercury—transformation arises from their true nature, and beginning and end follow one another. To concoct an elixir for immortality, one must use substances of the same kind: just as planting grain requires millet, and hatching chicks requires eggs, so too must things of the same nature be employed to achieve natural transformation. Smelting fish eyes cannot produce pearls; artemisia cannot become sandalwood. Only like begets like—disparate things cannot form treasures. Thus, swallows do not give birth to phoenixes, foxes and rabbits do not nurse horses, water does not blaze upward, and fire does not moisten downward.

Lead powder is originally black lead; when refined by fire, it becomes white. Yet if cast back into fire, it reverts to lead. Ice and snow are congealed yin *qi*; when scalded with warm water, they dissolve into water. Why is this? It is the principle of returning to the root and reverting to the source—this cannot be otherwise. The method of refining gold takes cinnabar as its foundation, blending it with mercury, for mercury is born from cinnabar and shares its nature. Thus, beginning and end follow one another, and transformation arises from their truth.

The *Wuzhen Pian* states: "When bamboo splits, use bamboo to mend it; to hatch a chick, use an egg." All things that are not of the same kind are but vain labor, striving in vain to match the true mechanism. For when true mercury meets true lead, the *qi* of one yin and one yang resonate as one—this is the meaning of "same kind." It is like planting grain, which requires millet, or hatching chicks, which requires eggs. When like follows like, the Way proceeds naturally, effortlessly achieving transformation. But if one abandons the like and seeks elsewhere, it is like trying to make fish eyes into pearls or artemisia into sandalwood—since they are not of the same kind, how can transformation occur? Just as swallows give birth to swallows and sparrows to sparrows, they cannot give birth to phoenixes. Foxes nurse foxes, rabbits nurse rabbits—they cannot nurse horses. Fire’s nature is to blaze upward; can it be made to moisten downward?

Shall water’s nature, which moistens downward, be made to blaze upward? Thus, those who wish to refine elixirs for transcendence must employ what is of the same kind—only the like follows the like; if affairs are contrary, no treasure is formed.

Yet within our own bodies there is naturally a heaven and an earth, naturally a yin and a yang. The *Huanyuan Pian* speaks of "seizing the marrow of another heaven and earth" and "copulating for a fleeting moment," claiming one must steal the *qi* of heaven and earth, the *qi* of the sacred father sun and numinous mother moon, to transform the *qi* of our own bodies—the *qi* of flesh and blood from our mundane parents—into the *qi* of true parents, transmuting the mundane body into a body of pure yang, a true immortal, thereby attaining the same lifespan as heaven and earth.

Yet some, benighted in their search for the like, fail to recognize the like within themselves and the Qian and Kun of their own bodies, instead seeking it outside. Falsely designating virgin maidens as true lead, they invoke the affairs of the *Huangdi Neijing* to adorn their heterodox doctrines, catering to the desires of scholar-officials. Thus, wealthy families and noble houses practice their arts, often meeting with death—this was not limited to the Tang Chancellor Xiahou Zi alone.

Therefore, Ge Hong in the *Baopuzi* likened it to holding ice in a cup to boil soup or wrapping fire in feathers, while Tao Hongjing in the *Zhen’gao* compared it to embracing jade and leaping into fire or a golden coffin descending into a dog’s den. Fang Xuanling called them "dogs and swine in human form," and Li Shangyin branded them "seeds of hell." Ancient and modern alchemical texts unanimously condemn this practice in the harshest terms, for it harms others and ultimately destroys the practitioner as well.

The *Nanhua Zhenjing* states: "Do not labor your form, do not shake your essence—only then can you attain longevity." Never has there been mention of so-called "mastery of women" as a method. Who has sown this pernicious seed, which to this day remains an obstacle, defaming the ancient perfected ones and misleading later students? Among the myriad heterodox arts, is this not the most harmful to the Dao?

Many scholars in the world, though learned and talented, meet with no opportunity. They exhaust their fires and squander their wealth, following texts and fabricating interpretations, their beginnings without cause, their measures without control. They pound and refine arsenolite, mica, arsenopyrite, magnetite, and sulfur, burn camphorwood, mix mercury, fly and smelt the Five Minerals and copper, using them as auxiliary agents. Yet their natures are disparate—how can they willingly unite as one? A thousand attempts lead to ten thousand failures; what they seek to refine becomes folly.

Fortune never favors them, and even the wise grow old with white hair, midway beset by doubts. They turn from the Dao and cling to delusion, stepping from the correct path into deviant byways. Their vision is narrow, their understanding limited—they cannot fathom what lies ahead.

The *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"Though your wisdom surpasses Yan and Min,
Without a true master, do not force conjecture.
For the alchemical classics lack oral transmission—
Where shall you form the numinous embryo?"*

Indeed, the world abounds with talented and diligent scholars, yet few encounter a true teacher and receive the correct transmission.

Those who burn and refine the Three Yellows and Four Spirits, deludedly believing the Dao lies therein, fail to recognize that the Five Metals and Eight Stones are mundane, tangible substances. Their kinds differ, their natures and properties vary—how can they willingly unite as one?

Thus, all who practice such arts meet with a thousand failures for every ten thousand attempts; what they seek to refine becomes folly. Why? Because their beginnings lack cause, their measures lack control.

The *Zhiyuan* chapter thirty-nine states:
*"Seeking teachers and friends, they learn to refine cinnabar,
Making the Great Return, thinking external elixirs transform inner medicine.
Yet metals and stones share no connection—
The Golden Elixir and Great Return Elixir arise from nothing,
While cinnabar and mercury are but metaphors.
Alas! People fail to recognize true lead and mercury,
Taking mundane cinnabar and mercury as real.
They exhaust their fires and waste their wealth,
Achieving nothing, their hair turning white in vain,
Only to sigh at the emptiness of it all."*

Ah! The true Dao is simple, not complex; its words are plain, without embellishment. Who believes it? Who practices it? Even those who receive the correct transmission may midway grow doubtful, thus stepping from the correct path into deviant byways.

The *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* states:
*"Those who rely on cleverness to force right and wrong,
Even if they hear the essential teachings, contradict themselves."*

Turning from the great Dao to cling to delusion, peering at the heavens through a tube—how can their narrow vision suffice to discuss the boundless mysteries of what lies ahead?

As for the utmost sages: none surpass Fuxi, who first drew the Eight Trigrams, modeling them on heaven and earth; King Wen of Zhou, who followed the trigrams and composed the line statements; and Confucius, the paragon of all sages, who added the Ten Wings to assist them. These three lords, raised by heaven, arose in succession to govern their times. Though their steps varied in excellence, their merits and virtues shared the same principle, their creations building upon one another with meticulous care.

Fuxi modeled heaven and earth to create the *Yijing*, first drawing the Eight Trigrams. In the Primordial Unity Diagram, Qian-heaven is above, Kun-earth below; Li-sun and Kan-moon are arrayed in the east and west, while Zhen-thunder, Xun-wind, Gen-mountain, and Dui-marsh occupy the four corners. Was this without precedent?

King Wen followed the structure of the trigrams to compose the *Yijing*, writing the line statements. In the Later Heaven Diagram, Zhen and Xun, the two woods, generate Li-fire; then Kun-earth stores them. Dui and Qian, the two metals, generate Kan-water; then Gen-earth halts them. Was this without precedent?

Confucius followed Fuxi and King Wen to expound the *Yijing*, composing the Ten Wings. Though he held no imperial throne in his time, he became the teacher of emperors for ten thousand generations—truly the paragon of all sages.

These three lords were the great sages of antiquity. Fuxi lived in high antiquity, King Wen at the end of the Shang, and Confucius in the late Zhou. They arose in succession to govern their times. Though their steps varied in excellence, in terms of merit and virtue, the former and later sages shared the same principle.

Moreover, in the drawing of the Eight Trigrams, the composition of the line statements, and the writing of the Ten Wings, there are no superfluous words, no superfluous characters. Their deliberations on weight and measure are exceedingly precise, for their creations all had precedents.

Now, Wei Boyang composed the *Cantong Qi*, borrowing the symbolic trigrams of the *Yijing* to elucidate its principles—truly, he had precedent.

Tangible things can be measured, signs can be contemplated; affairs can be planned to serve as models for the world. This book, though lacking prior knowledge, relies on the master’s enlightenment—like drawing back a curtain or ascending a high terrace. Tangible things with signs can be measured and contemplated, but the Golden Elixir and Great Cinnabar are matters without form or sign—they cannot be measured or contemplated.

Should it not be expounded in words, how then could later generations take it as their model? This is why the *Cantong Qi* was composed. Yet in composing the *Cantong Qi*, did Wei Boyang merely express his own views? No—he but recorded the principles transmitted by his master. As the *Xisheng Jing* states: "Without the guidance of an enlightened teacher, how can one resolve doubts and difficulties?" Even in worldly arts and techniques, one must bow in reverence to seek a master before attaining their safeguards—how much more so for the Golden Elixir and Great Cinnabar, the Dao of longevity and transcendence? Should the scholar not humble their heart and inquire widely, hoping for an encounter?

Ge Zichuan’s *Divine Immortals’ Records* tells of Liu Zheng, who sought the arts of nurturing life, diligently pursuing rare teachings and traveling a thousand *li* without hesitation. If he found one superior to himself, though they be a servant or guest, he would take them as his teacher. If people today could emulate Liu’s refusal to feel shame in asking those beneath him, true immortals would come to their door, shattering the cinnabar caves. Once they receive the oral instructions, all doubts dissolve—bright as drawing back a curtain or removing its cover, vast as ascending a high terrace to gaze upon the boundless distance. The heart and eyes would open in clarity—would this not be a great joy?

The *Six Hundred Chapters on Fire* and its aims differ not in essence. The *Awakening to Reality* states: "The *Canon*, *Treatises*, *Songs*—all expound the utmost truth, yet never commit the fire-phase to writing." Know that to fathom the subtle oral instructions, one must discuss them in detail with an immortal. Later learners, failing to grasp the true trap, see only the so-called *Six Hundred Chapters on Fire* and are left bewildered, believing the fire-phase to be so intricate and difficult. Little do they realize that the six hundred chapters are but like the sixty hexagrams: the sixty hexagrams mark the phases of a single month, while the six hundred chapters span ten months. For in one month, sixty hexagrams, each alike; in ten months, six hundred chapters, each similar. Thus it is said, "their aims differ not in essence."

The written word is solemn—yet people do not ponder deeply to trace its source and flow, though the hidden and manifest originally dwell together. I dare speak only to the worthy, not presuming to write lightly. To remain silent would sever the Dao and incur blame; to commit thoughts to bamboo and silk would risk leaking the celestial tally. Thus I hesitate, sighing, looking up and down, ever deliberating. There is a method to refinement, yet I dare not reveal it all. I shall but outline its framework, its branches appearing sparse.

From ancient times to now, alchemical texts fill storehouses like oxen laden with books, their thousand names and myriad words offering endless analogies—could this not be called solemn? Yet tracing their source and flow, one finds nothing but the interplay of yin and yang. These two emerge from the same origin though named differently. The ancient immortals poured out their livers and gallbladders to instruct the world, yet people, their karmic blessings shallow, have no leisure for deep reflection.

Now, Master Wei composed this book, pointing out the origins of the crucible and vessel, the sources of the medicines, the methods of the fire-phase, and the secret teachings on existence and nonexistence, host and guest, priority and sequence. His words reach the root, each character grounded in truth—composed for the worthy of the world, not lightly written to fish for empty fame. Know that books are transmitted to the enlightened, affairs valued by the discerning. A farmer given a vermilion bow would use it to scare birds; a southerner given a dragon robe would use it to carry firewood. The great Dao of the golden elixir is cherished only by the worthy—how could I speak much to those who do not understand?

The *Supreme Mysterious Regulations* states: "To meet a person and not transmit is to lose the celestial Dao; to transmit recklessly to the unworthy is to leak the celestial treasure. To transmit to the right person brings merit to the body; to transmit recklessly brings dark punishment to the seven ancestors." Thus, Master Wei, about to seal his lips and remain silent, would sever the lineage of the Dao and incur blame. Yet to commit his thoughts to bamboo and silk would risk leaking the celestial tally and inviting retribution. Thus he hesitated, sighing, looking up and down, ever deliberating. Though he wished to speak plainly and directly to instruct later learners, he dared not lightly reveal the true mechanism. Instead, he briefly described the medicines and fire-phase in broad strokes, so that later generations might grasp the intent behind the words—thus, perhaps, this Dao would not be lost in transmission.

Having neither the courage to speak plainly nor the heart to keep silent, he could only present its grand framework and distill its essential mechanism, scattering the methods of refinement across three chapters, like branches sparsely arrayed.

To use gold as a dike, water enters and flows freely. Gold is reckoned at fifteen, and the water’s number is the same. When tending the furnace, fix the weights: five parts water in excess. These two form the true gold, heavy as its original state, while earth does not enter. The two move together, and the three substances mutually embrace, their transformations wondrous as a spirit.

To use gold as a dike, water enters and flows freely: above, half a *jin* of gold serves as the outer furnace’s southeastern semicircular dike. Then below, half a *jin* of water descends from the west into the northern inner furnace, its force unhurried, flowing naturally. "Gold is reckoned at fifteen" refers to the first fifteen days, meaning the upper half-*jin* of gold. "The water’s number is the same" refers to the sixteenth to thirtieth days, meaning the lower half-*jin* of water.

The elixir method first ascends with civil fire, then descends with martial fire. Without this, it cannot deeply reach the nine springs below. Thus it is said, "When tending the furnace, fix the weights: five parts water in excess." Gold and water, each half, together form the weight of sixteen *liang*—one opening and one closing, endlessly circulating—these are the true yin and yang of our bodies. Yet water must exceed half a *jin*, while gold must not fall short of its half-*jin* weight. Thus it is said, "These two form the true gold, heavy as its original state."

"Its earth does not enter": around the crucible and vessel, there is only the upper half-*jin* of gold and the lower half-*jin* of water—earth has no fixed place. The phrase "does not enter" does not mean it does not enter at all. Earth has no fixed position; there is nowhere it does not enter. Consider the twenty-four positions of heaven and earth—though there is no *wu-ji*, is there truly no *wu-ji*? From *ren-zi* to *gan-hai*, it moves without constancy, above and below without permanence—all are the realms where *wu-ji* roams. "The two move together with it": throughout the crucible and vessel, earth is everywhere, and the two—gold and water—accompany it. When gold, water, and earth move together, the three substances mutually embrace and merge into one. Merged into one, they abide in the green mountains, a myriad acres of clouds, dredging from the azure pool a wheel of moon—such are their transformations, wondrous as a spirit.

The *Chapter on the Utmost Dao* states: "Ascending and descending are called the movement of gold and water; when *xun* is true, it is called the wind." Ascending is gold, descending is water, and that which enables their ascent and descent is earth. Yet gold, water, and earth are but metaphors and analogies. In truth, the great Dao has always been beyond names and forms; the true immortals originally had no flowers or grasses—what gold and water could there be? What earth could there be?

Beneath lies the latent *qi* of the Great Yang, steaming and seething; in but a moment, it first liquefies and then congeals—this is called the Yellow Chariot. The *Book of Jade Mushrooms* states: "In refining the elixir, follow the *zi* hour when the Yang *qi* arises to kindle the fire, for only then is its power complete. If the fire is kindled at any other time, it will not be so." For at the *zi* hour, the Great Yang resides in the north, and the *qi* of the human body reaches the *Weilü* Pass. If the fire is kindled at this moment, inner and outer align, allowing one to steal the mechanism of Heaven and Earth and complete the elixir.

At first, the Great Yang lies below, and water and fire unite in intercourse; the two *qi* intertwine, steaming to become liquid. Next, fire arises within the water, and the Yang *qi* gradually blazes; the liquid then congeals within, pressing forth the golden radiance—this is called true lead. When it is set in motion and ascends, it surges upward like a chariot coursing along the Yellow Way, and so it is named the Yellow Chariot.

As the years and months draw to their end, nature is harmed and lifespan diminished. On the thirtieth day of the twelfth month, the celestial revolution nears completion: the sun reaches its limit in the lunar mansion, its cycle exhausted in the reckoning. In the Way of the elixir, the human body likewise has its own revolution of Heaven, no different from that of Heaven and Earth.

The bodily form resembles gray ash, like dust upon a bright window. An ancient song says: "Use the bud, not the lead—you must work within the lead." Yet when it comes to using the lead, to use the lead is still mistaken. It further states: "Lead is the mother of the bud, the bud the child of lead." Once the golden radiance is obtained, to discard the lead is to invite deep peril.

People merely know that at the hour of *zi*, the *qi* of the kidneys arises; when refined by fire, it congeals into liquid, and they take this to be true lead, seeking to use it to transform the true mercury of *Li* Palace. Yet they do not consider that once it has become liquid, it possesses form and substance—its weight is turbid, how could it reverse its flow and ascend? The work of immortals in making the elixir does not employ its substance at this moment, but only ignites fire below to stir its *qi*. When the fire’s power grows strong, its *qi* surges upward like the rising clouds of mountains and rivers. Only after ascending into the *Niwan* Palace does it transform into sweet rain, descending into the *Chonglou*. Thus, the elixir method speaks of "taking the clear and discarding the turbid"—this is precisely what it means. The clear floats above, resembling dust upon a bright window; the turbid sinks below, resembling the body as gray ash.

Those who refine the external elixir take what flies and congeals upon the lid of the crucible, calling it "bright window dust." Master Wei used this to elucidate the internal elixir, wishing practitioners to grasp and extend the principle. "Pound and blend together, then hasten through the Crimson Gate": "Pound and blend together" means capturing from two places to unite in one furnace, where divine water coalesces into true butter. "Hasten through the Crimson Gate" means seizing the essence of the hare and crow, the marrow, and swiftly gathering it into the crucible to refine.

The great medicine of the golden elixir is born in *Kun* and planted in *Qian*. *Qian* above serves as the crucible, *Kun* below as the furnace. Without fierce refining and extreme forging, it cannot emerge from the furnace; without reversing its course and revolving counterflow, it cannot ascend to the crucible. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "Grasp with both hands and force them to fight, transforming into a lump of purple gold frost." It further states: "The river chariot dare not pause, swiftly transporting it to the peak of Kunlun." This describes the post-heavenly work of initial practice, distinct from the pre-heavenly moment of medicine production. Within it, there are also the dual practices of observing the mind and absorbing the spirit, both aiding the power of the fire-timing. Ancient immortals often kept these secret, and who would know them? Some cling to the methods of initial practice, engaging in massage, guiding, and revolving the windlass—vainly exhausting spirit and strength. How could they recognize the wondrous use of moving the spirit-fire to observe the true mind, or stirring the *Xun* wind to absorb the true spirit?

Secure the barriers and fortify the defenses to ensure perfection and firmness. The *Jindan Dacheng Ji* says: "Know the time, begin the work, gather what comes, and secure the divine hut without careless leakage." It further states: "Carry it to the crown, where the three flowers gather; tightly seal the Jade Pass and golden lock." When the golden sand ascends to the crucible, one must secure and seal it meticulously—only then will the sacred embryo be complete and firm.

Blazing fire spreads below, and the sounds of dragon and tiger grow earnest. The thirty-ninth chapter of the *Zhixuan* says: "Simply slip into the Hua Chi crucible, where true fire blazes heavenward to forge." The *Jianwu Jian* states: "Because the fire of the elixir blazes below, the waters of the Yellow River reverse their flow"—this is the fire of refinement, which cannot but blaze. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "Tiger leaps, dragon soars, the wind and waves coarse; at the central true position, the mysterious pearl is born." The *Cuixu Pian* states: "Dragon chants, tiger roars, lead and mercury burn together, revealing yellow buds and white snow." When the fire in the elixir field blazes fiercely, clouds steam and mists surge; wind stirs in the *Niwan*, and the sounds of dragon chanting and tiger roaring naturally arise. Peng Helin’s *Yuanshu Ge* says: "Having received the formula, return to test the refinement—see the dragon and tiger contend in an instant. The nine flowers bloom in the heavens, and those who know this will witness a night of wind and thunder shaking ten thousand mountains." Those who have undergone trials will understand this for themselves.

Begin with civil and end with martial; the array is complete. At the outset of gathering the medicine, concentrate the spirit, gather the *qi*, and harmonize the breath—inhale and exhale should follow the hand. When the spirit and *qi* enter their root, close tightly, but not too urgently; release freely, but not too loosely. Only by making it continuous and unbroken will the spirit naturally congeal over time, and the breath naturally settle. After a while, the *Xun* gate thunders, dragon soars and tiger leaps—then drive it back to the tail cavity, where flames surge into the celestial thoroughfare, rushing straight upward. Wang Baoyi said: "Civil fire is the fire of generation; martial fire is the fire of fruition." The beginning is generation, the end fruition—begin with civil and end with martial; they cannot be pursued by a single method.

Observe and inspect with utmost care, carefully adjusting warmth and coolness. "Observe and inspect with utmost care" means sealing the *Dui*, lowering the gaze, containing the light, and silently awaiting the arrival of Heaven and Earth’s *qi*. Only then should you stabilize the breath to gather it, concentrate the fire to refine it—you must examine its maturity, not acting carelessly. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "It all depends on the power of cultivation; even a hair’s difference will fail to produce the elixir—how could it be done lightly?" "Carefully adjusting warmth and coolness" means regulating the fire’s power, examining and proceeding cautiously, gradually shifting from gentle to fierce—do not rush or advance hastily. The *Fuming Pian* states: "The fire-timing must be firmly stable; the blowing and breathing entirely rely on the *Xun* wind—how could it be rushed?"

Revolve through the twelve nodes, each one more intimate than the last. The twelve nodes are the twelve celestial stems of the Zhou Tian, corresponding in the body to the fire-timing and positions. The twelve nodes of fire-timing ascend from *zi* to *wu*, then descend, circling the exterior of the crucible like the twelve celestial stems of Heaven. When the twelve nodes have all been traversed, the Zhou Tian completes, and the celestial ridge once more points to *zi*—thus it is said: "Revolve through the twelve nodes, each one more intimate than the last." The *Cuixu Pian* says: "The *Zhen* trigram moves to return to the west, the *Dui* region; the three Yangs and the alchemical maiden play with bright pendants. The *Xun* wind stirs the coral tree, entering *Gen* to return to *Kun*—another Yang shares this same defense."

When *qi* is exhausted and life nears its end, rest and let the *po* and *hun* souls die. In refining the golden elixir to seek immortality, why does Master Wei speak of *qi* being exhausted, life nearing its end, and resting to let the *po* and *hun* souls die? The *Cuixu Pian* says: "Hasten to return the hundred vessels to their source; when the pulse halts and *qi* ceases, the elixir begins to form." When the golden liquid congeals, the jade pivot and balance wheel stop turning at once, and the sun’s *hun* and moon’s *po* sink into the northern sea’s depths, hidden away—thus it is said: "*Qi* is exhausted, life nears its end, rest and let the *po* and *hun* souls die." The so-called death is not true death—at this moment, one returns to the root and restores life; the spirit congeals, essence solidifies, the eight vessels all halt, and breath ceases. The *qi* seems exhausted, as if cut off. Yet after this death, one revives anew—this is what the *Shangqing Ji* calls: "This time, truly dead, only now alive." Alas! To know the moment when the great medicine forms, when the six pulses all stop and *qi* does not flow—without having personally experienced such scenes, how could one speak of them with certainty?

When the hue shifts further to a radiant purple, the Returned Elixir is thus formed. The *Cuixu Pian* states: "Shedding yellow to don purple—what is the reason? Only because the River Chariot has turned many times." For when the ninefold firing sequence is complete, the Returned Elixir blazes forth in luminous splendor, transforming into the color of purple gold.

Consume it as a single pellet—even the tiniest *daogui* is most wondrous. The *Cuixu Pian* says: "Gather it, refine it—before a moment passes, the *qi* drifts subtly through the Three Passes. The Three Passes come and go, the *qi* endless; a single white channel ascends to the Mud Pill. Atop the Mud Pill stands the Purple Gold Tripod, within which a lump of purple gold coalesces, transmuting into jade nectar that flows into the mouth, fragrant and sweet, refreshing and cool upon the tongue. Swallow it, ingest it—it enters the five viscera, harmonizing the organs, the body at ease and secure. For the Returned Elixir, when taken, is as sweet as licorice, as cool as calamus—this is what is called the *daogui*: merely the tip of a knife’s edge, a corner’s fragment. Yet when its work is complete, it undergoes a thousand transformations, ten thousand changes, its wonders unfathomable—no minor art of side doors can compare. Rightly is it called divine!

Expounding the numbers of the Five Phases is concise yet not cumbersome. The *Yi* says: "Heaven one, Earth two; Heaven three, Earth four; Heaven five, Earth six; Heaven seven, Earth eight; Heaven nine, Earth ten"—these are the generative and completed numbers of the Five Phases.

Master Zihua said: Of the great numbers of Heaven and Earth, none surpasses five, none centers upon five. For five is the number of Earth, positioned at the center. Combined with the one of northern Water, it becomes six; with the two of southern Fire, it becomes seven; with the three of eastern Wood, it becomes eight; with the four of western Metal, it becomes nine—nine being the utmost of numbers. The numbers of the world end at nine. In terms of the nine numbers, five stands amid one, two, three, four, six, seven, eight, and nine, truly the central number.

Numbers originally have no ten. What is called the completed number ten of Earth arises when the one of the north, the two of the south, the three of the east, and the four of the west converge at the center and form ten. Thus, when the five of the center disperses to the four directions, it becomes six, seven, eight, and nine, and Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal all rely on Earth to be completed. If the one, two, three, and four of the four directions return to the center and form ten, then Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal all revert to their root and return to Earth.

Ah, how profound! The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "Two things are born of the child, producing the mother." The *Hunyuan Baozhang* says: "The four positions are distinct, their use inverted; the Five Phases arise together and return together." Both expound this meaning. Though the generative and completed numbers of the Five Phases may be recited even by a child of three feet, how many truly grasp their significance? If one does not know their significance, how can one attain their practical use?

Herein lies the subtlety of the Dao: profound and distant. To the enlightened, it is simple and easy; to the deluded, it grows ever more complex and difficult.

When water is cast to quench fire, the flame is suddenly extinguished. When the sun and moon eclipse, it is always at the new and full moons. When Water flourishes, Kan encroaches upon Yang; when Fire declines, Li’s daylight dims. Yin and Yang consume each other, their interaction the natural course of the Dao.

Shao Kangjie’s *Huangji Jingshi Shu* says: "The mutual eclipsing of the sun and moon is the intersection of numbers. When the moon obscures the sun at full moon, the moon is eclipsed; when the moon covers the sun, the sun is eclipsed—just as Water and Fire overcome each other." Now, when water is cast to quench fire, fire is overcome by water, and the flame is suddenly extinguished. When Greater Yin obscures Greater Yang, Yang is vanquished by Yin, and the sunlight darkens in broad daylight. Why? When Yin and Yang interact, they inevitably consume each other—this is the natural course of the Dao.

Master Wei borrowed the mechanism of solar and lunar eclipses to illuminate the wonders of the Elixir Way, and further employed the mutual overcoming of Water and Fire to expound it. Truly, he was thorough.

Yet though solar and lunar eclipses always occur at the new and full moons, those who refine the Golden Elixir steal from Heaven and Earth, seize the workings of Creation—how could they not seek that which lies between the new and full moons?

Names define essence; words arise from nature. When Metal returns to its nature, it is first called the Returned Elixir. The *Fuming Pian* says: "One thing divides into two. Knowing the two is called naming the two." The two are Metal and Wood. Metal and Wood are originally not two bodies, so when Metal is named, Wood is termed; when Wood is nature, Metal is essence. In truth, they are one thing. For the Metal Lord is originally the child of the eastern household, sent to lodge in the western neighbor—its body born, it cannot yet be called the Returned Elixir. Only when it is poured into the eastern Yang’s crucible of Creation, returns home to meet the Blue Maiden, and produces a luminous pearl like the full moon, is it truly called the Returned Elixir.

Some say: "When stillness is unmoving, essence returns to nature—hence it is called the Returned Elixir." This is correct, yet it does not capture the original meaning of Metal’s essence and Wood’s nature in this chapter of Master Wei’s work. I dare not fabricate, nor imitate the ancients.

The *Ancient Records* speak of Dragon and Tiger; the *Yellow Emperor* praises Golden Flower; *Huainan* refines Autumn Stone; Wang Yang cherishes Yellow Sprout—none but veiled terms to convey their subtle meaning. Now, would Master Wei, in composing this work, recklessly invent empty theories to indulge his whims? No, he too followed the ancients’ subtle intent.

Master Zhu said of his use of words: "All are based on ancient texts." This is no empty claim.

The worthy can uphold and practice it; the unworthy must not be allowed to partake. When Guzi followed Zihua Zi on his travels for twelve years, his studies complete, he took his leave. Zihua Zi admonished him, saying: "Now, what you have cultivated, I find no fault in. Yet your aspirations are too broad and indiscriminate. Alas! You will later be one who selects. There will be those who strip away your outer defenses and build their own palaces; who mount your chariot and ride it to gallop across the four outskirts; who take what you hold as jade, shatter it, and divide it fivefold. The Dao inherently detests imbalance, for imbalance obstructs the Dao. Yet it also detests not finding its proper balance, for improper balance harms the Dao. Now you depart—if you reflect on how to guard your balance, then in this too I find no fault."

Master Wei’s words—*"The worthy can uphold and practice it; the unworthy must not be allowed to partake"*—reveal his profound concern. For the unworthy are surely frivolous and shallow; being frivolous and shallow, they are surely devoid of righteousness. Few among them would not draw the bow of Yi. The *Fu Ming* chapter states: *"This Dao is most divine and sagely—do not let it leak or be lightly disclosed. It relies entirely on virtue and conduct, both in harmony. Speech must be guarded, and taboos observed. Know that the great Dao of the Golden Elixir may only be balanced with the worthy. If one is not such a person, not only should it not be spoken of, but they should even be kept at a distance."*

Alas! From ancient times to the present, those who emerge into the world and transmit the Dao have all been heroes of the highest order, riding cranes and commanding mists and clouds—certainly not the foolish or clumsy. How could the unworthy ever comprehend it? From ancient times to the present, the Dao has been passed down through a single conversation, a single exchange of words. *"He who attains the One accomplishes all things. How great is the One! It is the source of a thousand scriptures and ten thousand discourses, the ancestor of a thousand transformations and ten thousand changes."* Truly, there is no second Dao in the world, no second heart in the sage. And the myriad volumes of immortal scriptures all converge in their language: the Golden Elixir is this very root and origin.

Scholars, exert yourselves, hold these words in mind, and ponder deeply. These are the most essential teachings, laid bare and luminous, never deceiving us. Master Wei says: *"If scholars repeatedly savor and contemplate this book, one day their minds will grasp and their spirits guard it. Then they will know that my most essential words are indeed laid bare and luminous, without a trace of deception or concealment."* Ah! Master Wei’s intent to guide later learners is most earnest. Should scholars not trust and receive his teachings, recite and uphold this book, and hold its words in mind with deep reflection?

---

**Upper Scroll of the *Elucidation of the Union of the Three in Accordance with the Book of Changes***

The firmness of Qian and the softness of Kun unite and embrace one another. How great is the breath of man! Zhang Hengqu’s *Zheng Meng* states: *"Man’s possession of breath is the image of firmness and softness rubbing together, of Qian and Kun opening and closing."* What is breath? One exhalation, one inhalation. With each exhalation, the pulse moves twice, and qi travels three inches; with each inhalation, the pulse also moves twice, and qi travels three inches. In one complete cycle of breath, qi travels six inches. In the hundred divisions of a day and night, there are thirteen thousand five hundred breaths, and qi travels eight hundred ten zhang, circulating up and down like an endless ring, its limits unfathomable.

Before a person is born, dwelling in the mother’s womb, they breathe as the mother breathes—without sight, without hearing, only a single breath remaining. At birth, the umbilical cord is cut, and the primal qi of true origin gathers below the navel. Day by day, the spirit departs and qi shifts, and one no longer guards the single breath of the womb. Now, the method of cultivation practiced by divine immortals is to turn the light inward, harmonizing breath with the Great Harmony. This is to return to the origin and revert to the source, restoring the state at the moment of receiving qi at birth.

Xu Jingyang’s *Drunken Thoughts on Becoming an Immortal* says: *"Internally harmonize the true qi, preserve breath, and naturally transform to restore youthful countenance."* The *Song of the Great Dao of the Numinous Source* states: *"A thousand scriptures and ten thousand discourses expound on the subtle mysteries, but the root of life originally lies in the true breath."* All else is heterodox practice and reckless action, not the true and essential natural Dao.

Now, Master Wei speaks of *"the firmness of Qian and the softness of Kun uniting and embracing one another"*—this refers to the moment of elixir-making, when the yang of Qian descends to unite with the yin of Kun, causing breath to intermingle, firmness and softness to correspond, and husband and wife to be paired as one. Then spirit and qi return to the root, nature and life unite, and the medicine gestates within. It is also called *"the mating of Dragon and Tiger,"* *"the union of Metal and Wood,"* *"the coiling of the serpent and the dragon,"* *"the blending of red and black,"* *"the harmony of Heaven and Earth,"* *"the intermingling of the Mysterious Yellow,"* *"the fusion of Metal and Earth,"* *"the sharing of the crucible by Metal and Mercury,"* *"the sharing of the furnace by Metal and Fire,"* *"the union of red and white,"* *"the dwelling of the sun and moon in the same palace,"* *"the sharing of the den by crow and hare,"* *"the joyful union of husband and wife,"* *"the meeting of the Oxherd and Weaver Girl,"* *"the following of female and male,"* *"the merging of hun and po."* In the end, it is nothing more than the interdependence of heart and breath, the internal resonance of yin and yang, and the union of spirit and qi.

*"Yang bestows, yin receives; male and female depend on one another."* *"Yang bestows, yin receives"* is like jade flowers blooming and dewdrops congealing, the blossoms gradually opening as the bud’s dew deepens. *"Male and female depend on one another"* is like the Jia Dragon and Geng Tiger guarding each other, lead and mercury sharing the same crucible—only then can it be achieved. The so-called *"bestowing and receiving,"* the so-called *"depending on one another,"* are but explanations of the mating.

Heaven and Earth generate beings through the mating of yin and yang; the method of the elixir generates medicine through the mating of yin and yang. Truly, there has never been a creation of transformation that did not arise from mating. The *Book of Jade Mushrooms* states: *"If the Mysterious Yellow does not mate, how can yang fly up from below Kan?"* This is the great principle of elixir-making, the foremost doctrine of immortal cultivation.

Yuan Yangzi’s *Song of the Great Dao* says: *"The vastness of the Mysterious Heaven pairs with the Yellow Earth; the two natures harmonize and enter the bridal chamber."* This is its meaning. The *Returning Gold* chapter states: *"The former sages clearly revealed the cinnabar terrace, where the numinous crow perched on the cassia branch several times."* This is its meaning. The *Returning to the Origin* chapter states: *"The Metal Mother of the West is most firm and strong, entering the watery abode of Ren."* This is its meaning. The *Awakening to Reality* states: *"The Red Dragon and Black Tiger meet east and west; the Four Images unite in the center of Wu and Ji."* This is its meaning. Wang Liangqi’s *Awakened Understanding* chapter says: *"Skillfully guide the hun to join the po, transforming yin entirely into pure yang."* This is its meaning. The *Fu Ming* chapter states: *"To know the great Dao’s principle of the Inaudible and Invisible, the Great Yang shifts to illuminate within the moon."* This is its meaning.

The *Upholding the Orthodox* chapter states: *"The two numinous substances naturally unite; this bit of divine mechanism is sought here."* This is its meaning. The *Upper Clarity Collection* says: *"There is no other method—only that the divine water enters the Flower Pool."* This is its meaning.

Knowing this, one may then produce the medicine and form the embryo. Further obtaining the fire-phase secret, refining it causes the celestial drum to rumble, shaking the earth as jade dust scatters like reed-ash, ascending into the Mud Pill. If one merely knows this but fails to grasp the fire-phase secret, it only warms the lower origin—this is not the Returning Elixir.

An ancient song says:
*"From red to black is true refinement;
black to red, the Celestial Immortal is complete."*

The elixir method differs in the Prenatal and Postnatal, the effortless and the effortful. In the Prenatal, spirit congeals and enters the Kun navel to produce the medicine; in the Postnatal, spirit shifts and enters the Qian crown to complete the elixir. The Prenatal is effortless, the Postnatal effortful—they cannot be treated as one.

When the essence and qi of creation unfold,
The *Emerald Void* chapter states: *"At every moment when heaven and earth unite, seize the mechanism of yin-yang transformation."* This mechanism is the moment when heaven and humanity jointly activate. The transformations within the human body correspond to those of heaven and earth. Now, to speak of *"uniting with creation"* is to discuss its utmost subtlety, which lies entirely in the celestial mechanism and the human mechanism being set forth together. If one can empty the heart and congeal the spirit, aligning with the mechanism of heaven and earth, then creation rests within one’s grasp.

The *Master Hidden by Heaven* says: *"If within three hundred and sixty days one circulates one’s own qi to harmonize with the true qi of heaven and earth two or three times, one will naturally feel the body’s clarity and harmony, different from ordinary times. How much more so if practiced long and accumulated in profound accord—the Dao of immortals is not difficult to attain!"*

Some say: *"Simply sit when one must sit—why distinguish rivers and sources, recognize times and days?"* This is merely ignorance of creation; such a one is not worth debating.

The *Kan and Li* crown the head, their radiance spreading forth.
*Kan and Li* are the sun and moon.

The alchemical method encompasses both inner and outer sun and moon. The *Yellow Court Scripture* states: *"The sun emerges, the moon enters—breathing and visualization pertain to the inner sun and moon."* It further says: *"The three luminaries shine brilliantly, entering your chamber,"* which refers to the outer sun and moon. These two are mutually dependent and cannot exist without each other. The *Thirty-Ninth Chapter of the Pointer to the Mystery* states: *"When the outer [sun and moon] conjoin, the inner [sun and moon] also conjoin; the three passes become unobstructed without laborious effort."* At the moment of the elixir’s formation, the inner sun and moon unite, refining essence within, while the outer sun and moon merge their radiance without. When inner and outer converge and remain undispersed for long, then—dimly, faintly—something takes form within. Profoundly, obscurely, essence fills the void, and white snow scatters in disarray.

The sun and moon illuminate heaven and earth: the spiral shell and clam absorb them to generate pearls, and stubborn stones store them to transform into jade. How much more so for the human body, which possesses its own sun and moon—can it not reflect its own light to crystallize its true pearl and produce its true jade? The *Shangqing Collection* states: *"The operations of heaven and earth largely revolve around Kan and Li. Why does the stone hold jade? Why is the pearl concealed in the clam’s belly? Through this, the celestial mechanism is revealed—truly, the Dao lies before one’s eyes."* Yet though the Dao lies before their eyes, scholars often fail to awaken, for the celestial mechanism is too near, and the blind repeatedly overlook it even when facing it directly.

The mysterious and unfathomable cannot be depicted in diagrams. The sages measured and arranged the primordial foundation. The *Red Dragon Great Elixir Formula* says: *"There is a thing called the mysterious and unfathomable; the golden elixir arises from this."* Silent and vast, subtle and immeasurable—how could it be captured in diagrams? Since it cannot be depicted, it can only be known in the heart and grasped in the mind. Yet what lies within oneself may be known in the heart and grasped in the mind, but when teaching others, how can one not employ images and texts to silently convey its mechanism? Thus, the sages delved into the profound and sought the hidden to elucidate the ultimate mystery of the primordial. They employed broad analogies and layered metaphors to arrange the primordial foundation of the golden elixir—hence the proliferation of alchemical classics and the composition of the *Cantong Qi* in accord with the *Book of Changes*. The "primordial foundation" is the root.

The *Awakening to Reality* states: *"To ensure the Valley Spirit never dies, one must rely on the Mysterious Female to establish the root."* For subduing lead and restraining mercury depend entirely on the Mysterious Female; thus, the Mysterious Female is the root of the returned elixir. When a man establishes the Mysterious Female, his essence and qi are secured; when a woman establishes the Mysterious Female, her monthly courses cease. In cultivation, if one does not know this, from where will the medicinal materials arise? From where will the returned elixir coalesce? From where will the fire phases be applied? The four—Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li—merge directly into the formless.

When merged into one, it is called "primordial chaos." The *Mysterious Writ* states: *"Sprouting, spreading, ceaselessly—principles of south and north, east and west naturally converge."* This is how Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li merge into one. Once merged into one, spirit and qi return to the root and directly enter the formless—profound, subtle, beyond comprehension.

The *Song of the Numinous Source and the Great Dao* says: *"Mixed into one, then forgetting one—thus may one merge with the transformations of merit, emerging and vanishing together."* Yet what the alchemical tradition calls "formless" does not mean the absence of mind or thought, the stillness of withered wood or dead ashes. The *Wenzi’s* *Comprehending the Mysterious and Perfected Classic* states: *"Seeing in the dark, hearing in silence—within the darkness, alone there is clarity; within stillness, alone there is illumination."* This is the ineffable subtlety.

Some, failing to grasp this, mistake it for the Chan school’s quiescence—this is a grave error. If the golden elixir were identical to Chan study, one could simply engage in Chan meditation. Why undertake the manifold processes of Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li? Why must one thoroughly understand yin-yang and deeply penetrate the workings of creation before attaining mastery?

The sixty hexagrams are spread and arrayed as a chariot; the dragon-horse is harnessed, and the enlightened ruler drives—when times are harmonious, one follows the level path without deviation. Should the path turn crooked, danger and peril will topple the state.

In the alchemical method, Qian and Kun serve as the crucible and vessel, while Kan and Li become the medicinal substances. The remaining sixty hexagrams, from *Zhun* and *Meng* through *Jiji* and *Weiji*, are arranged in cyclical sequence around the crucible to regulate the firing times of the celestial revolution. Thus it is said: *"The sixty hexagrams are spread and arrayed as a chariot."* Once this chariot is spread and arrayed, all sixty hexagrams become instruments for our use, and the dragon-horse returns to our harness and guidance.

The *Shangqing Jade Perfection Embryonic Breathing Formula* states: *"I take spirit as my chariot and qi as my horse, driving them all day without weariness."* Without weariness means *"subtly continuous, as if present"*—using them without strain. *"Without strain"* does not mean forgetting, nor does it mean rigidly holding on; it is a state of *"as if present, as if absent,"* never descending into forced labor or urgency.

How excellent were the words of Guangchengzi to the Yellow Emperor: *"I preserve the One and abide in harmony; thus I have cultivated my body for one thousand two hundred years, yet my form has never declined."* The body is like the state; the mind is like the ruler.

When the mind settles, spirit congeals and qi harmonizes; the Three Palaces naturally ascend and descend, and the hundred meridians flow unimpeded. Those who diligently practice this will without exception attain transcendence. As Master Gushen states in his *Lucid Treatise*: *"The Dao of cultivating the body takes utmost spirit as its root, utmost essence as its medicine, harmonious balance as its function, and non-action as its governance. Through non-action, spirit congeals; when spirit congeals, harmonious qi gathers; when harmonious qi gathers, roots deepen and foundations solidify; with deep roots and solid foundations, the Dao of long life and enduring vision is accomplished."* To depart from this is not the method of the Golden Elixir and Great Return.

Generally, the dwelling of qi and blood within the human body values undisturbed cultivation. The practice is most simple and easy: merely embrace the origin, guard the One, concentrate qi, and attain suppleness like an infant. Thus, in the moment of elixir formation, there is no other technique—only emptying the mind, stilling the self, and congealing spirit into the qi cavity, following its ebb and flow with unbroken continuity. Over time, spirit naturally congeals, breath naturally settles; when breath settles, qi gathers; when qi gathers, the elixir forms. There is no need for the labor of massage, daoyin, exhalation and inhalation, or visualization.

The *Internal Pointers to the Mysterious and Secret* states: *"When the sun departs, depart with it; when the moon arrives, arrive with it—following its ingress and egress without leaving the Elixir Terrace."* From this perspective, the Great Elixir’s Dao relies solely on the singular flavor of primordial harmonious qi to accomplish its transformations; there is no other path.
