The noble one dwells in his chamber, and when his words are good, they resonate a thousand *li* away—just as the lord of ten thousand chariots, secluded in his nine-fold chamber, issues commands that align with the rhythms of yin and yang. The ruler, enthroned in majesty and secluded in profound depths, speaks well, and his words echo a thousand *li* away. Likewise, the cultivator, embracing stillness and silence, turns inward to reflect; when emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness its utmost, the qi of Heaven and Earth naturally comes to him. Why? Because this follows the natural principle that when one thing is stimulated, another responds.

When issuing commands in accord with the rhythms of yin and yang, the sage kings of antiquity, in the first month of spring, would order their ministers to spread virtue and harmonize decrees; in the first month of autumn, they would command generals to train soldiers and sharpen weapons—all in accordance with the seasons and the rhythms of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang. If this is how governance is conducted, how much more should the Dao of the Elixir be so?

*"Store the vessel and await the time; do not transgress the hexagram’s day."* The *Returning Gold Chapter* states: *"Do not let the moment slip; remain always within the single yang."* In the method of elixir refinement, one must first know the time, and even more, await it. If the time has not yet come, one should only embrace stillness, silence, and emptiness to await it—never forcing it prematurely. As the *Three Medicines Song of Master Zhengyang* says: *"Preserve the mind, cut off thoughts, and await the crystallization."* The *Thirty-Ninth Chapter of the Mysterious* states: *"Seal the senses, lower the gaze, and silently observe."* This is what is meant by *"storing the vessel and awaiting the time."*

Alas! When the hour arrives, without laboring the mind, the internal energies naturally intermingle, naturally coalesce, and congeal. Entering the chamber, one must await the moment when the yellow path of the sunwheel ascends of itself—how could one force it ahead?

The hexagram *Tun* employs *zi* and *shen*, while *Sui* employs *yin* and *xu*. Each of the sixty hexagrams has its own day; here, I merely present two images without exhaustive detail. In *Tun*, below is *Zhen* and above is *Kan*. *Tun* employs *zi* and *shen*: the initial nine of *Zhen* is the *gengzi* line, and the six in the fourth place of *Kan* is the *wushen* line.

Below is *Kan* and above is *Gen*. *Sui* employs *yin* and *xu*: the initial six of *Kan* is the *wuyin* line, and the six in the fourth place of *Gen* is the *bingxu* line. Now, *zi* and *shen*, *yin* and *xu* are none other than *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*—spring, summer, autumn, and winter—Metal, Wood, Water, and Fire—the Serpent, the Dragon, and the Tiger. All are allegories.

Viewing the sixty hexagrams as a whole, they begin with *Tun* and end with *Jiji* and *Weiji*. Between them, yin and yang lines intermingle and intertwine. Though each differs from the next, with their inverted and corresponding forms each finding its own resonance, in truth, hexagram after hexagram follows the same pattern. When Master Wei remarked, *"I merely present two images without exhaustive detail,"* he intended for the learner to contemplate the images of *Tun* and *Sui*, and from this initial insight extend it further, thereby intuitively grasping the meaning of the remaining hexagrams.

Each hexagram has six lines; two hexagrams together comprise twelve lines, corresponding to the twelve hours of a day. The sixty hexagrams together comprise three hundred sixty lines, corresponding to the three hundred sixty hours of a month. Master Wei’s original intent was merely to use this framework to discuss the divisions, arrivals, openings, and closings of the fire-phase within our own bodies. For example, if applied to *Xu* and *Song*, one would say: *"Xu* employs *zi* and *shen*, *Song* employs *yin* and *wu*." If applied to *Shi* and *Bi*, one would say: *"Shi* employs *yin* and *chou*, *Bi* employs *wei* and *shen*." The other hexagrams follow this same pattern. Thus he said, *"I merely present two images without exhaustive detail."*

The *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"The Southern and Northern Schools reverse the hexagrams’ images,
Morning and evening fire-phases align with the Celestial Pivot."*
This, too, serves only to elucidate this same principle. If one clings rigidly to the text, mired in the images, and insists on calculating the numerical configurations of the hexagrams’ structures to match the yin and yang of their lines, I fear one will toil endlessly without ever achieving success—wearily laboring, yet never knowing where one is bound. How lamentable!

In principle, *"establishing punishments where there is righteousness, bestowing virtue where there is benevolence."* The West represents righteousness, the East represents benevolence. Punishment governs killing and subjugation; virtue governs generation and ascent. Now, *"establishing punishments where there is righteousness"* refers to forging the lead of the West, while *"bestowing virtue where there is benevolence"* refers to refining the mercury of the East. Lead belongs to Metal; its nature is utterly unyielding. Concealed within *Kan*, it cannot ascend unless subjected to fierce firing and extreme forging. Thus, one employs martial fire to compel it, and must not apply civil fire. Mercury belongs to Wood; its nature is utterly yielding. Hidden within *Li*, upon encountering true lead, it naturally ceases movement. Thus, one employs civil fire to refine it, and must not apply martial fire.

The *Chongzheng Pian* states:
*"Defending the city requires the application of civil virtue,
While waging war in the field demands the deployment of martial prowess.
These two modes of action must be clearly distinguished;
Proceeding in accord brings good fortune, while going against invites misfortune."*
This is what is meant.

According to the almanac, one must be utterly sincere, focused, and meticulous. The *Gaohongmeng Mengxian Yao* states:
*"Gathering has its time, harvesting has its day.
Gather, harvest—be ever vigilant and discreet."*
Thus, one must consult the almanac to know the oral instructions for the proper moment. Only with utter sincerity, absolute focus, and no trace of extraneous thought can the divine mushroom be plucked. Likewise, if one remains secluded for a year, from dawn to dusk inwardly circulating the numinous banner, one must maintain utter sincerity and absolute focus, undivided in purpose—only then can the elixir be completed and the body attain immortality. If one treats the Great Dao as child’s play, lightly and carelessly tampering with the celestial mechanism, practicing in the morning only to abandon it by evening, this is not true sincerity.

The *Huangting Jing* states:
*"Accumulating merit and refining the elixir do not occur naturally;
They arise from precision and sincerity, and from unwavering focus."*
From ancient times to the present, those who have cultivated the elixir have never succeeded without sincerity, nor have they ever achieved their aim without wholehearted dedication.

In ancient times, Yi Qiu instructed two men in the game of *yi*. One devoted himself wholeheartedly, listening only to Yi Qiu’s teachings. The other, though he appeared to listen, was in fact preoccupied with the thought of a swan goose approaching, imagining himself readying his bow and arrow to shoot it. Though he studied alongside the first, he did not match his skill. Now, *yi* is but a minor art—without wholehearted dedication, one cannot master it. How much more so with the Dao?

The *Zuowang Lun* of Sima Ziwei states: "Only in idleness and tranquility can one cultivate the Dao. For the study of the Dao must attain pure stillness and non-action, and not allow the slightest external matter to burden the mind. If one is inwardly entangled by family affairs or outwardly occupied by official duties, this too is a lack of wholehearted dedication to the Dao."

The *Dadao Ge* of Zhang Xujing declares:
*"The spirit guides qi, qi sustains the form—*
*No need for miscellaneous arts to attain long life.*
*The art is easy, yet the Dao is hard to encounter;*
*Even when encountered, without single-minded practice,*
*Of a thousand or ten thousand who study, barely one or two succeed—*
*This saying is indeed true."*

Scholars of today do not understand that immortality is achieved through accumulation. They merely wish to attain it through fleeting moments of playful effort, never considering that even a brief interval is part of the work of coalescing the elixir, while the year-long firing and nurturing—whether walking, sitting, sleeping, or eating—must remain unwavering, lest the fire cool and the elixir’s power wane. How could there be the slightest interruption?

One must carefully observe the celestial cycles and examine the waxing and waning of qi. The *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"Heaven and earth wax and wane in their own time;*
*Only by scrutinizing these changes can one grasp the mechanism."*
And again:
*"The moon’s waxing and waning corresponds to the rise and fall of spirit;*
*The sun’s rising and setting aligns with the cold and warmth of construction and defense."*

For within a month, there is the waxing and waning of a month; within a day, there is the waxing and waning of a day. The dark and new moons, the crescent and full moons—these are the waxing and waning of a month. Day and night, dawn and dusk—these are the waxing and waning of a day. The human body models itself on heaven and earth, and the waxing and waning of its qi and blood follow the same path as the transformations of heaven and earth.

The *Suwen* says: "At dawn, human qi begins to flourish; at noon, yang qi reaches its peak; by dusk, yang qi is depleted, and the qi gates close." It also says: "When the moon first appears, blood and qi begin to refine, and defensive qi begins to circulate. When the moon is full, blood and qi are abundant, and the muscles are firm. When the moon is empty, the muscles diminish, the meridians are depleted, defensive qi departs, and only the form remains."

Thus, just as heaven and earth have day and night, dawn and dusk, so too does the human body. Just as heaven and earth have dark and new moons, crescents and full moons, so too does the human body. The shifting of cold and heat, the alternation of yin and yang—all correspond to heaven and earth. Therefore, in the elixir method, heaven serves as the crucible, earth as the furnace, and the moon as the agent of the medicine. The gathering of the medicine must follow the moon’s waxing and waning, while the firing process must align with the sun’s rising and setting. From beginning to end, there is not a single moment that does not harmonize with heaven and earth.

As the *Huangji Jingshi Shu* states: *Yin* marks the opening of things, like the *Jingzhe* solar term when the yearly count begins; *Xu* marks the closing of things, like the *Lidong* solar term when the yearly count ends—are these not the firing times of the elixir method? The three hours of *Hai*, *Zi*, and *Chou* are when the sun sinks below the earth and disappears, when the count halts and does not proceed. Within this interval lies the moment when the first yang stirs, when the myriad things have yet to arise—this is how the sage perceives the heart of heaven and earth, and how one models oneself on heaven and earth to complete the myriad things, with creation at one’s command. Is this not the time for gathering the medicine in the elixir method?

In all matters—drawing and adding, circulating and bathing, joining and binding—one follows the transformations of heaven and earth. This is why it is called the supreme Dao. If one does not rely on the transformations of heaven and earth but seeks other methods, these are but side doors and minor arts, mere conjectures of the mind, not the supreme Dao.

The *Huainanzi* says: "The clam’s pearl waxes and wanes with the moon," and "When the moon is empty, the fish’s brain diminishes; when the moon dies, the snail’s flesh withers." Even such insignificant creatures correspond to the transformations of heaven and earth—how much more so for humans?

From this, we see that when cultivating the elixir at the full moon, qi and blood are full, and the medicine’s power is complete. After the full moon, qi and blood diminish, and the medicine’s power wanes. Thus, the *Cuixu Pian* says: "On the night of the full moon, one can gather the celestial hun and earthly po to congeal the numinous elixir," and also: "The union of the two eights is the mother of the elixir; seeking the tiger and dragon beyond the full moon is but vain labor."

The *Wuzhen Pian* further states:
*"On the fifteenth day of the eighth month, when the toad’s radiance shines,*
*It is precisely when metal essence is at its peak.*
*When the first yang arises at *Fu*, then one may advance the fire—*
*Do not delay!"*

The *Chongzheng Pian* says:
*"The metal of *Dui* holds myriad treasures, completing in the west;*
*At the osmanthus moon’s mid-autumn, its brightness doubles.*
*This is the time to refine the head and apply the work of gathering—*
*Wait not for the yang to arise!"*

The *Jindan Dacheng Ji* declares:
*"Just at this moment, wondrous and rare,*
*When the moon in the sky is full at mid-autumn,*
*Swiftly gather the yang—do not let it linger!*
*Advance the fire with care, lest danger arise."*

The reference to the eighth month’s fifteenth day, when metal essence is at its peak, does not mean that only on this day can one gather the medicine. Rather, works like *Baizi Qing* and *Liao Chanhui* all specify entering the chamber at the *Zi* hour on the night of the eighth month’s fifteenth. For at this time, yin and yang are in perfect balance, the autumn air is crisp, metal essence is at its height, neither cold nor warm—most suitable for cultivation. The ancient immortals formed the embryo at this time, stealing the metal essence of heaven and earth and drawing in the pure qi of heaven and earth.

Some, failing to receive the true transmission, know not the essence of the matter. They merely absorb the moonlight on the night of the eighth month’s fifteenth—this is like dangling a net to catch the wind or grasping a rope to bind a shadow. How can such a thing be spoken of as the supreme Dao?

Moreover, there is another account. Master Wen Yuan of Chao recorded that during the Tang dynasty, the recluse of Mount Zhongyue, Qizhenzi Shi Jianwu, composed the *Lingxiang Ci*. In its preface, he writes: "Upon casually perusing the *Three Classics of Stillness*, I read that those who cultivate the Dao must enter the Three Stillnesses to refine spirit and qi, thereby extending life. The Great Stillness lasts three hundred days, the Middle Stillness two hundred, and the Lesser Stillness one hundred." Moved by utmost sincerity, he resolved to attempt the Lesser Stillness. Thus, in the third year of the Kaicheng reign (the *wuwu* year), beginning on the first day of the first month (*gengshen*), he shut his door, severed all worldly ties, and adhered strictly to the hundred-day term. Upon emerging from his chamber before the month had passed, his spirit-light shone before his eyes, a hundred spirits gathered at his ears, and his essence and vitality were unclouded—all three responses confirmed that the immortal classics and secret scriptures were not empty teachings.

Now, in the cultivation of the Great Elixir, one must proceed likewise. For only with prolonged stillness do spirit become clear and qi harmonized, the inner vessel perfected—only then can refinement begin. To remain mired in worldly entanglements, exhausting sinew and bone, taxing heart and mind, yet expect to succeed in a single attempt on the fifteenth of the eighth month is most arduous indeed.

If the slightest mote is amiss, regret and misfortune become thieves. The solstices shift their degrees, the equinoxes deviate east and west, midwinter transforms into scorching summer, midsummer reverses into hail and snow. Lead and mercury scatter, myriad anomalies erupt—all because the fire and timing are awry. As the *Wuzhen Pian* states: "Though one may recognize cinnabar and black lead, without knowing the fire’s timing, it is as idle as a vast city. Cultivation depends entirely on this power; even a hair’s divergence fails to produce the Elixir."

The so-called *fire* is not the imagined flame between heart and kidney, but the body’s innate true fire. The so-called *timing* is not the measured moments of the clock, but the body’s natural qi rhythms. Fire’s nature flares with wind and hides with earth; its use must neither exceed nor fall short. One must gently fan it with *xun*-wind, fiercely conceal it with *kun*-earth, ensuring moderation without excess or deficiency. Then, great and small remain unharmed, both realms preserved, and in an instant, the Elixir’s head appears. This is what is meant by "adjusting and applying."

If even a hair’s breadth errs, the solstices shift, the equinoxes stray, midwinter becomes scorching summer, midsummer turns to hail, lead and mercury flee, and anomalies proliferate.

The filial adept’s intent moves the August Ultimate. As the *Fuming Pian* says: "One man and one woman draw upon heaven and earth; three daughters and three sons unite beginning and end." For Qian begets three sons, Kun bears three daughters—together called the Six Children. Now, Qian as father and Kun as mother occupy above and below, while the Six Children move between them, ascending and descending, ever following their parents—hence the name "filial child." The August Ultimate is the central, correct position, the convergence of the eight directions. When the filial adept’s intent moves it, tigers roar, dragons emerge from their caves, phoenixes dance, and the Elixir enters the golden city.

But if one’s actions are rebellious and discordant with the Ultimate, the dark pearl scatters, the numinous mercury flees, and the Six Children become not filial heirs but rebels against the Elixir Way.

Thus may one invite misfortune or summon blessing, bring about peace or incite war—these four outcomes arise from the heart’s resolve. As the *Wuzhen Pian* states: "Fire is born of wood, yet its edge is hidden; without skillful drilling, do not force it. Disaster springs from this—harming oneself. One must master and subdue it, seeking the Metal Elder. With the medicine present and the fire’s timing applied, metal is pressed by fire, surging to the Li Palace, transforming into water, which in turn restrains fire—thus fire avoids the peril of flaring upward. Without the medicine, the fire’s timing drives empty yang to attack, precisely what burns the body. Herein lies the division between inviting misfortune and summoning blessing."

Again, it says: "Sun and moon meet once every thirty days; timing is easily changed. The method lies in divine skill—guarding the city, waging wild battles, knowing defense, and increasing fortune. When the numinous cinnabar fills the vessel, crimson, true lead arises from the Kan Palace, turbid and unmoving. To capture and restrain the true mercury of the Li Palace, one must use martial fire, fiercely refining and forging until it soars upward. Upon reaching the Li Palace and uniting with true mercury, one must then guard the city and bathe—no further fire may be added. Herein lies the vast difference between peace and war."

Whether one invites misfortune or summons blessing, brings peace or incites war—how could this arise from anything other than the heart’s reflection? It is simply a matter of turning inward.

The movement and stillness follow constant principles, adhering to their guiding line. When utterly still, one returns to the root and recovers tranquility—this is the time of Kun. I then wait in stillness; when stillness reaches its extreme, movement arises, and yang qi subtly germinates in the Yellow Bell Palace—this is the time of Fu. I then move in response. Thus it is said: "Movement and stillness follow constant principles, adhering to their guiding line." If, when one should move, stillness is mixed in, or when one should be still, movement is interposed; if one forces growth prematurely or neglects it afterward—this is not adhering to the guiding line.

The perfected ones of antiquity moved in accord with the heavens and were still as the abyss. When it was time to move, they moved; when it was time to be still, they were still—there was always a constant method. Today’s scholars, unaware that the alchemical method’s movement and stillness follow constant principles, either cling solely to movement or solely to stillness. What they call "movement" is merely the movement of circulating qi; what they call "stillness" is merely the stillness of dhyāna-samādhi—both miss the mark entirely.

Does not Chapter 39 of the *Zhixuan* state: "Every person’s qi and blood naturally flow, nourishing and defending, with yin and yang cycling through the hundred moments—how could this depend on shutting the door to practice circulating qi? It is like placing another head atop one’s own." When has circulating qi ever been considered true movement? Does not the *Cui Xu Pian* state: "Only this Qian and Kun truly operate—there is no need to sit rigidly, speechless, thoughtless, and mindless, for the spirit will only grow dull. How could one then congeal it into an immortal embryo?" How could dhyāna-samādhi ever be considered true stillness?

The *Jindan Dacheng Ji* says: "Yang governs movement; yin governs stillness." The *Cui Xu* states: "Within movement, seek stillness; within stillness, there is action." Movement and stillness have their proper roles—how could those who merely parrot transmitted teachings presume to lightly discuss them?

**Harmonizing with the seasons and aligning with qi**
The four seasons are the spring, summer, autumn, and winter within the body. When practicing inner work, one must skillfully regulate and harmonize with their proper times, so that warmth, coolness, cold, and heat each find their place. Lü Chunyang’s poem says: "When water and fire are balanced, the medicine is formed; if yin and yang are mismatched, the elixir fails." All success lies in this regulation.

**Firmness and softness are distinct, not intermingling**
Firmness is yang; softness is yin. "Firmness and softness are distinct, not intermingling" means that when yang’s firmness is at work, the intent is wholly directed downward—it must not encroach upon the upper. When yin’s softness is at work, the intent is wholly directed upward—it must not encroach upon the lower. As Zhang Ziyang’s *Shiqiao Ge* says: "Water generates fire, fire generates water—water and fire must be ordered in sequence." This is what is meant.

**The Five Phases guard their boundaries, not recklessly expanding or contracting**
The *Wang Liangqi Tiantong Jing Song* states: "Metal, wood, earth, water, and fire—all five guard the single cinnabar field. Who says one may succeed without such guarding?" For when these five are dispersed, they become qi circulating throughout the body; when gathered, they form the treasure in the cinnabar field. Now, if they are steadfastly and single-mindedly guarded in the cinnabar field without reckless expansion or contraction, they will blend and fuse, transforming into a single qi that congeals into golden liquid.

"Expansion" refers to excess; "contraction" refers to insufficiency. The *Cui Xu Pian* says: "The numinous mercury, connected to the true, undergoes many transformations—it is only fitting to preserve and guard it, not to overdo it. For excess harms the thing, while insufficiency fails to generate it. One should only maintain it ceaselessly, as if present, guarding it with intent." With prolonged guarding, when the time arrives and qi transforms, the vessel will be full and must overflow—then the numinous spirits will naturally come.

**The Yi flows ceaselessly, bending and extending, turning and returning**
The *Yi* is the sun and moon.

The sun and moon traverse the ecliptic, circulating ceaselessly day and night. In the first half of the month, yang expands while yin contracts, the *hun* grows and the *po* wanes; in the latter half, yin expands while yang contracts, the *hun* wanes and the *po* grows. This cycle repeats endlessly, without cessation.

The human body, with the head as *qian* and the abdomen as *kun*, resembles heaven and earth. Its two qi ascend and descend just as in heaven and earth. The *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* states: "The sun and moon constantly traverse the ecliptic and the celestial equator. All immortals who cultivate this method to refine the returning elixir do so in a way no different from heaven and earth." Yet the key to emulating the sun and moon’s movements and achieving the same efficacy as heaven and earth lies in the *Ren* and *Du* meridians.

The *Ren* and *Du* meridians are the seas of yin and yang for the entire body, the pivot for the five qi and true origin. The *Ren* meridian begins below the *Zhongji* point, ascends through the pubic region, follows the inner abdomen up to *Guanyuan*, reaches the throat, and is the sea of yin meridians. The *Du* meridian begins at the *Xiaji* point, runs alongside the spine up to *Fengfu*, enters the brain, ascends to the crown, follows the forehead to the bridge of the nose, and is the sea of yang meridians.

The *Ren* meridian is so named because women rely on it for conception and nourishment. The *Du* meridian is so called because it oversees and governs the sea of meridians. Deer live long, reaching five hundred years as white deer and a thousand as blue deer, for they can unblock their *Du* meridian. Creatures like cranes, toads, and toads all live a thousand years, for they can unblock their *Ren* meridian.

The *Nanhua Zhenjing* states: "Follow the *Du* as your guiding principle, and you can preserve the body and safeguard life." If one can unblock these two meridians, all meridians will flow freely, naturally circulating throughout the body without stagnation or blockage—this is the way to attain longevity. The *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* says: "If the water of the method can ascend to the mysterious pass, one may wander freely day and night, ceaselessly revolving the wheel within. Stagnation gives rise to all illnesses; unblocked flow preserves youth—is this not the meaning?"

As the poem by Langranzi says:
*"Ascend the current straight to the Mud Pill Palace,
Unblocked joints and passages preserve youth."*
And again:
*"Constantly let qi surge through the joints,
Naturally essence fills and the Valley Spirit abides—
Are these mere empty words?"*

Chen Tuan regarded it as transforming the foul and turbid qi, while Han Xiaoyao saw it as turning the wheel without pause, achieving harmony with the mysterious within a month. If there is action, it is yet no action; if there is stillness, it is yet not stillness. From the secret transmissions of the Supreme Primordial Lord down through the generations to the present, apart from this Way, there is no other. Some call it "the center of the circle," others "the celestial revolution"—this is the true path of the body’s ascent and descent, yin and yang’s circulation.

The *Daodejing* says: *"The Valley Spirit does not die; it is called the Mysterious Female. The gate of the Mysterious Female is called the root of heaven and earth. Ceaselessly it seems to endure; in its use, it is inexhaustible."*
The *Yinfu Jing* says: *"Three returns in a day and night yield ten thousandfold."*
The *Huangting Jing* says: *"All lies within the heart, revolving the celestial scripture; day and night preserve it, and you will naturally attain long life."*
The *Duren Jing* says: *"Revolve the Dipper, traverse the Winnowing Basket, measure the Five Constants."*
The *Tiantong Jing* says: *"The Three Palaces ascend and descend, circulating endlessly."*
The *Shangqing Yuzhen Taixi Jue* says: *"From the Mud Pill Palace above to the Gate of Life below, the Two Radiant Ones follow each other, reviving the aged and infirm."*
Liu Xugu’s *Yi Zhuan* says: *"Dwell the spirit in the upper mystery, gather qi in the lower female; revolve and circulate without end."*
Bai Ziqing’s *Donglou Fayu* says: *"From the Celestial Valley above to the yin extremity below, the Two Radiant Ones meet and merge into one."*
Cui Gong’s *Ruyao Jing* says: *"Return to the root’s aperture, the Gate of Life’s pass; thread the tailbone, penetrate the Mud Pill Palace."*
Xu Jingyang’s *Sanyao Ge* says: *"The Three Fields revolve within, flowing ceaselessly; when black hair and dark eyes return, it is the time."*
Wu Zongxuan’s *Xuexian Ge* says: *"Kan and Li reverse, overturning heaven and earth; sun and moon both revolve."*
The *Zhixuan Pian* says: *"A single horse follows heaven’s transformations; six dragons drive the sun’s endless cycle."*
Lü Chunyang’s poem says:
*"If one wishes to learn long life,
First let the pivot revolve day and night."*
His *Jingzhong Yin* says:
*"Ride the axle to ascend the celestial pole,
Day and night like a turning wheel."*
The *Huánjīn Piān* says: *"Silently revolve qian and kun into one great harmony; let sun and moon flow on either side."*
The *Yuzhi Shu* says: *"Eastward turn, westward flow—none can fathom it; earth ascends, heaven descends—who can know?"*
The *Lingyuan Dadao Ge* says: *"Harmonizing within, one becomes true; exhaling and inhaling without—this is never complete."*
Zhang Hongmeng’s *Huanyuan Pian* says: *"In the twelve hours, let midnight lead; ceaselessly revolve and refine without pause."*
The *Wuzhen Pian* says: *"The wondrous workings of the Five Agents rely on qian and kun; qian and kun revolve, and the Five Agents divide."*
The *Fuming Pian* says: *"Patrol the twelve positions, the barefoot fierce general."*
Liu Gaoshang’s *Fayu* says: *"Spirit preserves the generative qi; qi, not from mouth or nose, descends as yin, ascends as yang, flowing in harmony with heaven and earth."*
Liu Xugu’s *Huandan Pian* says: *"Descend and ascend, revolve the hub; left turn, right cover, align the pivot."*
The *Chongzheng Pian* says: *"Revolving with skill, day and night unbroken; the subtle turning of the celestial sphere."*
The *Hunyuan Baozhang* says: *"Self-strengthening without cease, in harmony with qian’s movement; within a six-foot body, embrace the nine heavens."*
The *Cuixu Pian* says: *"By day, revolve the numinous banner; by night, draw forth the fiery mushroom. In refinement and augmentation, be ever vigilant."*
The *Huanyuan Pian* says: *"Who can grasp the great meaning of this work? It lies with the Yellow Dame."*
The *Shangqing Ji* says: *"Day and night, the river chariot never pauses; silently harmonizing with the great creation, it revolves in unison."*
The *Quanzhen Ji* says: *"Qi and blood circulate without leakage."*
The *Jianwu Ji* says: *"Revolve the sun and moon’s wheel."*
The *Shuiyun Ji* says: *"Constantly till the field of clarity; in the three stages of fixed guarding, dwell in non-action’s abode."*
Liu Changsheng’s *Xianyao Ji* says: *"In stillness, movement, sitting, and lying, revolve Kan to welcome Li."*
The *Mingdao Ji* says: *"At ease and self-content, the Way of long life—day and night, silently turn the Dharma wheel."*
Wang Yuyang’s *Guang Ji* says: *"Revolve the inner elixir with fire timing; carry the sun and moon, driving the jade wheel."*
The *Taigu Ji* says: *"Know the celestial revolution’s degrees, and the joints’ steaming and fuming will flow everywhere."*

All these refer to this same Way. Some call it "somersaulting," others "turning the mind-wheel." One says: "Model heaven’s pivot, and immortality spans ten thousand eons." Another says: "Harmonizing within preserves long life." A thousand scriptures and ten thousand discourses, without exception, concur—citing the ancient to verify the present, their agreement is as precise as tallying tokens.

This is neither mere qi circulation, nor visualization, nor the "flying golden essence behind the elbow," nor the "revolving of the mind through the spine." It is simply the Way of preserving the great harmony through breath, guarding the natural and true essentials.

Alas, how is it that scholars of this world ever delight in difficulty yet disdain simplicity, seek the distant yet neglect the near? Though there be those who possess the Way and wish to reveal it, who would believe them? The *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* states: "The world takes no joy in the true Dharma, preferring instead to discourse on what lies east or west." Unless one thoroughly comprehends the workings of yin and yang and sees through the transformations of creation, none will trust this.

Within the span of a single dark-and-dawn—*that is, the thirty and a half days before midnight*—in the alchemical arts, when time replaces days, the juncture of *hai* and *zi* each day is precisely this dark-and-dawn. At this moment, heaven and earth first opened; at this moment, sun and moon conjoin; at this moment, grasses and trees germinate; at this moment, the yin and yang of the human body unite. When immortals undertake the elixir at this time, inner truth and outer resonance align as perfectly as tallying tokens—neither too soon nor too late, but precisely at the center. Among the four virtues of the *Book of Changes*, this is the interval between *zhen* and *yuan*; among the twelve hexagrams, it is the end of *kun* and the beginning of *fu*—the most wondrous point for heaven, earth, and humanity.

Chaos and the primordial vastness—this is the time before the division of the one qi. The union of female and male—this is when yin and yang mingle within it, not yet parted. When they have not yet parted, spirit congeals and qi gathers, blending into oneness. Within, one is unaware of one’s own body; without, one knows not the cosmos. With the Dao, one merges in darkness; ten thousand thoughts all vanish. Vast and boundless, it cannot be named—perforce called the Great One containing true qi, or the primordial unity of precelestial qi. The *Cuixu Pian* says: "The great medicine must rely on spirit, qi, and essence, gathering them in one place to form the cinnabar head. It is simply the precelestial qi refined into yellow sprouts, blooming as jade flowers." For the immortals’ cultivation, there is no other method but to gather the precelestial unity of qi as the mother of the golden elixir. Practice diligently, and in days you may ride alongside Zhong and Lü. The *Fuming Pian* states: "Gather the qi of the undivided Two Modes, seize the essence of the first mating of dragon and tiger. Flash into the yellow chamber and forge it into the supreme treasure." And again: "Gathering requires utmost sincerity; discern the ugly and the fair. It is most difficult to grasp the subtle thread, easy to lose. The cold spring fears only that scholars cling to effortful doing, failing to comprehend the precelestial of the great Dao, mired in gathering yet unable to find its pulse." The *Chongzheng Pian* says: "In the ten-thousand-fathom icy abyss, beneath the sleeping dragon’s chin, the pearl glows red. Guard it closely, disturb it not, and only then may you gather it. Follow the hours and days, rely on spiritual work." For gathering is gathering without gathering; taking is taking without taking. In the midst of stillness and stability, there is nothing to be done through action.

Of old, the Yellow Emperor lost his mysterious pearl and sent Knowledge to seek it; sent Li Zhu to seek it; sent Chi Gou to seek it—none found it. Only Xiangwang obtained it. Xiangwang means "formless and traceless." Only when form is absent and traces vanish can the precelestial unity of qi be attained. The *Jirang Ji*’s *Xiantian Yin* says: "A piece of the precelestial is called the Great Void. When nothing is done, the true richness appears." And again: "If you ask about the precelestial, there is not a single word. Only after the postcelestial must one apply effort." This is indeed so. Before the Supreme Ultimate divided, before yin and yang parted—this is the precelestial of heaven and earth. In terms of the elixir arts, it is the time of utter stillness, returning to the root and reverting to tranquility. The *Hunyuan Baozhang* says: "Utterly still, unmoving, yet responsive when stirred—one glimpses the workings of yin and yang. Truly, when utterly still and unmoving, the heart communes with heaven, and the transformations of creation may be seized." The *Cuixu Pian* states: "Seek not the transformations of creation within the kidneys; rather, seek the precelestial within the heart." This is most profoundly and clearly expressed. The *Jirang Ji*’s *Silü Yin* says: "Before thoughts arise, the spirit of the mysterious female is unknown." The *Huánjīn Pian* says: "Where the spirit of the mysterious female is unseen, dragon and tiger quietly seek each other." The great Way of the precelestial must be attained through utmost emptiness and profound stillness—no trace of thought may intrude.

At the moment of utter stillness and unmoving, when ten thousand thoughts are extinguished, rivers and seas fall silent, mountains and peaks conceal their mists, sun and moon pause their radiance, the jade pivot ceases turning, the eight vessels return to the source, and breath itself vanishes. One sinks deeply into the profound and obscure, utterly unaware of heaven as canopy or earth as chariot, nor of the world’s people or one’s own body. After a little while, the three palaces fill with qi, the mechanism stirs, and the mysterious female sings. With a single sword, one cleaves open chaos; with two hands, one rips apart the primordial vastness—this is called "nothing giving birth to being." The poem of Master Ningxuan says: "Not in the dust of toil, not in the mountains—one must seek precisely the profound and obscure." Is this not true?

Nowadays, people know not the ancestor of the great Dao. Some point to true lead as the precelestial; some to the "heavenly one generating water" as the precelestial; some to the single gleam between the two kidneys as the precelestial; some to the navel as the precelestial; some to the ancestral breath of the nose as the precelestial; some to the movement of qi as the precelestial. These are all fragmentary views, plucking branches and leaves—they are not the precelestial of the great Dao. The *Book of Changes* says: "Precelestial, yet heaven does not oppose it; postcelestial, yet one follows heaven’s timing." "Precelestial, yet heaven does not oppose it"—this is the pure spontaneity of heavenly principle, which even heaven cannot contravene. "Postcelestial, yet one follows heaven’s timing"—when the time of heaven arrives, one should comply with it. Who but the most profoundly penetrating could partake of this?

Moistening fluids and nourishing essences flow and circulate, transforming and communicating. The *Huandan Pian* says: "For ten thousand miles, yin settles as spring’s qi conjoins; through nine skies, clarity pervades as dew’s essence congeals." Ah! This is the true scene of yin and yang’s mutual arousal—precisely at this time. Spirit and essence extend in all four directions, flowing without limit. Above, they reach heaven; below, they coil upon the earth. Dimly, like mountain mist; hazily, like water’s vapor; gently, like winter snow gradually congealing; deeply, like fermented liquid slowly clearing—this is the intertwining of heaven and earth within the body, the union of male and female within the body. When the time arrives and qi transforms, stirred into communication, suddenly the fire-wheel scorches the earth’s veins, startlingly the divine spring gushes from the mountain’s peak. Between heaven and earth, all is moistened and greatly enriched.

The *Jirang Ji*’s *Huanghu Yin* says: "In the indistinct, yin and yang first transform; in the intertwining, heaven and earth first revolve. Within, a little good scenery—how can effort enter words?" Unless one personally attains true realization, who would believe in such a real scene?

The spirits of heaven and earth are beyond measure; when yin and yang intersect, the divine and demonic conceal themselves—this is the moment of heaven and earth’s spiritual power. When dragon and tiger intertwine, the *gui*-spirits remain unaware—this is the moment of our body’s spiritual power. Uniting the body’s spiritual power with that of heaven and earth, the inner truth externally responds: within stillness, the *xun*-gate suddenly opens, and both sides ascend in unison, merging as one.

The *Huanyuan Pian* states: "Only in the formless and imageless realm lies the root of eternal life without death." Truly, only by forgetting form and image can one witness it. If it could be attained through deliberate contemplation, calculation, or measurement, it would no longer be called spiritual power.

**Utilizing and Concealing the Body, Hiding the Form**
At the new moon, sun and moon conjoin in the north, their radiance hidden from sight. In the elixir method, the time is shifted to midnight, at the cusp of *kun* and *fu*. Cross-legged and seated like an unmoving mountain, mouth sealed and breath stilled like a hibernating winter serpent—this is "utilizing and concealing the body, hiding the form." At this moment, containing light in silence, one inwardly reflects. With each exhalation and inhalation, long and unbroken, the qi meanders back to the life-root. Over time, one feels profound and guarded, as if standing at the edge of an unfathomable abyss. This is the time when spirit and qi return to their root, body and mind revert to their mandate, and golden liquid coagulates.

The *Cuixu Pian* says: "When qi enters the cinnabar field to nourish the white crow, only then is it called the sprouting of the yellow sprout." If the mind-monkey is restless and the intent-horse gallops outward, spirit and qi scatter externally—how could one hope to congeal the returning elixir?

**The Fire Phases and Lunar Cycles**
It begins in the northeast, in the region of *Ji* and *Dou*, then turns rightward. The wheel of fire disgorges its bud, and the hidden pool reveals its image; radiant essence scatters above *Mao* and *Bi*. *Zhen* emerges as the first yang, the inception of yang qi. In the *Qian* hexagram, the *ninth-one* is the "hidden dragon," for yang is established by three and yin by eight, thus communication occurs. On the third day, *Zhen* stirs; on the eighth day, *Dui* moves. The *ninth-two* is the "visible dragon," harmonious and luminous; the virtues of three and five are complete, and the *Qian* body is formed. The *ninth-three* is "vigilant at dusk," its divine tally waxing and waning, gradually returning to its origin. *Xun* continues its lineage, firmly governing and sustaining. The *ninth-four* is "perhaps leaping," its path perilous; *Gen* controls advance and retreat, not overstepping its time. On the twenty-third day, it guards the *xiaxu* period. The *ninth-five* is the "flying dragon in the heavens," exalted and joyous; the *sixth-five* of *Kun* receives and binds, completing the cycle, nurturing all beings as the eternal mother. The *ninth-six* is the "exhausted dragon," its virtue contending in the wilds.

The northeast, the region of *Ji* and *Dou*, is where the fire phase begins. Above *Mao* and *Bi*, *Zhen* emerges as the first yang—*Zhen* is also the trigram where the fire phase commences. The *Cuixu Pian* states: "There is a path where mother and child part ways; its wonder lies in *Wei*, *Ji*, *Niu*, *Dou*, and *Nü*." This is what it refers to.

This chapter cites the six precelestial trigrams and the six lines of *Qian* to symbolize the waxing and waning of the moon, interspersed with the twenty-eight lunar lodges where the moon resides—all are metaphors. If scholars lack the oral transmission and rashly read this, few will avoid bewilderment of both mind and eye.

The fire phases of elixir refinement are no different from the moon’s waxing and waning. On the third day, the moon begins to brighten, starting in the northeast at *Ji* and *Dou*, then turning rightward until evening, when the wheel disgorges its bud above *Mao* and *Bi* in the southwest. Its image resembles *Zhen*; in terms of the *Qian* hexagram, it corresponds to the *ninth-one*—this metaphorically describes the initial stirring of yang fire in the body. On the eighth day, the moon ascends to *shangxu*, its light half-bright; its image resembles *Dui*, corresponding to the *ninth-two* of *Qian*—this metaphorically describes the halfway point of yang fire’s work in the body. On the fifteenth day, the moon is full, its spirit abundant and light complete; its image resembles *Qian*, corresponding to the *ninth-three*—this metaphorically describes the time of yang fire’s fulfillment in the body. On the sixteenth day, the full moon wanes, and the lunar essence begins; its image resembles *Xun*, corresponding to the *ninth-four*—this metaphorically describes the beginning of yin tally’s succession in the body. On the twenty-third day, the moon descends to *xiaxu*, its light half-diminished; its image resembles *Gen*, corresponding to the *ninth-five*—this metaphorically describes the halfway point of yin tally’s work in the body. On the twenty-fifth to thirtieth days, the moon’s light vanishes; its image resembles *Kun*, corresponding to the *ninth-six*—this metaphorically describes the time of yin tally’s completion in the body.

Why does Master Wei use the six trigrams to illustrate the fire phases and pair them with the six lines of *Qian*? Because the elixir is the treasure of pure yang, and *Qian* is the hexagram of pure yang. Yet among the six trigrams, *Qian* is called the *ninth-three*, "vigilant at dusk, its divine tally waning," and *Kun* is called the *ninth-six*, "the exhausted dragon contending in the wilds"—why is this? Because guarding the city with *Qian* employs civility, while battling in the wilds with *Kun* employs martiality; both require vigilance against danger and caution against peril.

**The Use of Nine**
"Using nine, gracefully it becomes the Dao’s compass and square." When the yang numbers are exhausted, they begin anew, cycling in accord with nature and aligning with inherent virtue. Revolving and abiding by the celestial pivot, ascending and descending, flowing through the six lines—difficult to perceive, thus it has no fixed position, becoming the ancestral origin of the *Yi*.

Nine is the yang number. The *Yi* states: "Three for heaven, two for earth, relying on these numbers." It draws from the generative numbers of the five phases: heaven-one, heaven-three, heaven-five—three heavens rely on one another to form nine; earth-two, earth-four—two earths rely on one another to form six. This is why *Kun* uses six and *Qian* uses nine.

In the elixir method, "using nine" refers to the nine revolutions of the fire phase within a single period, modeled after *Qian*. "Using nine, gracefully it becomes the Dao’s compass and square; when the yang numbers are exhausted, they begin anew"—each line passes, and another arrives, without the slightest deviation. The *Fuming Pian* says: "Within the numbers, nothing is lost; the fire phases must not lag." This is what it refers to.

**Emotion and Nature**
Emotion is metal; nature is wood.

Within one thing, division creates two—separated as east and west. Yet if one grasps the mechanism of the Dipper’s handle and sets it in motion, then wood-nature loves metal, and metal-emotion yearns for wood, no longer divided. Thus it is said: "Push emotion to unite with nature, revolving together in accord with the turning jade pivot—ascending and descending, rising and falling."

The ceaseless flow through the six lines is difficult to observe, hence "without fixed position" becomes the ancestral principle of the *Yi*. This flow permeates the body: heaven hides within it, and earth hides within it—like the six lines’ ceaseless transformation. The "six lines" refer to the six lines of the *Qian* hexagram. Extend this principle, and it expands into six hexagrams, sixty hexagrams—its meaning remains one.

The *Huánjīn Piān* says: "At nine revolutions, the mysterious mechanism reaches its apex; open the furnace, release the fire, and the lid flies off." This refers to the nine revolutions of the fire phase, beginning at *yín* and ending at *xū*—the secret of the fire timing within the furnace. Within it lie the wonders of withdrawal and addition, advance and retreat, the subtleties of bathing and conjoining. Thus it is called the ultimate mystery.

If one does not know this, even with true medicine in hand, how can the elixir be formed? This is why Master Wei dared not lightly disclose it, merely borrowing the *Qian* hexagram’s "using nine" to hint at its mechanism. From this perspective, the immortals’ returning elixir is the *Yi* within the body; the nine revolutions of the fire phase are the *Qian* within the body. Yet the *Qian* within the body has no visible lines, no observable images or numbers—welcomed, its beginning is unseen; followed, its end is unseen. How then could it have a form to observe?

At the new moon’s dawn, *Fù* arrives: yang qi begins to flow, entering and exiting without haste. Erect a marker—subtle and firm. When the Yellow Bell aligns with *zǐ*, signs emerge, spreading warmth and gentle steam, attaining constancy. *Fù* is the hexagram of one yang. In terms of pitch, it corresponds to the Yellow Bell; in a day, it is midnight (*zǐ*); in a month, the first to the third day’s half; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *zǐ*. At this time, yang qi first flows, like the initial stirring of yang fire within the body. The fire qi is most subtle—neither indulgent nor restrained, neither hasty nor sluggish—allowing gentle warmth to spread within the crucible.

The *Chóngzhèng Piān* says: "The Yellow Bell is *zǐ*, the first arising of yang, the initial reception of qi. The number has not yet increased; the fire should be warm, not scorching. One fears the dragon’s weakness and the tiger’s overbearing might." In the first line’s movement, yang first flows—one can only raise it lightly, silently, not yet applying force. Thus it is said: "Entering and exiting without haste." Yet it must not be too soft; one must stir the crown gate’s mechanism, gently lifting it. Thus it is said: "Erect a marker—subtle and firm." Soon, the fire’s power blazes, driving out true lead to the region of the Winnowing Basket and Dipper. Then the river chariot dares not linger, halting its movement to ascend Kunlun’s peak.

The *Jīndān Dàchéng Jí* says:
*The Fù hexagram rises with the hidden dragon; at wù-sì, adjust slightly, do not attack.
At nine-two, the dragon appears in the Lín hexagram, mastering spiritual penetration.
From here, the furnace’s order reddens step by step.
When the Fáng hexagram arrives, fierce fire burns dry, borrowing the xùn wind.
Refining the yellow sprout and white snow, the marvelous work returns to the Kūn Dao—
Here the fire phase’s secrets are exhausted.*

When tending the furnace, apply the firing sequence to open the path—light gradually brightens, growing day by day. *Chǒu*’s Great Frame forms, its rhythm rising and falling. *Lín* is the hexagram of two yangs. In terms of pitch, it corresponds to the Great Frame; in a day, it is cockcrow (*chǒu*); in a month, the third day’s half to the fifth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *chǒu*. At this time, yang qi gradually advances, like the body’s yang fire gradually flowing smoothly, the yellow path gradually brightening. Thus it is said: "Light brightens, gradually advancing, growing day by day."

*Tài* is the hexagram of three yangs, where firm and yielding flourish together, yin and yang conjoin—small departs, great arrives, converging at *yín*’s movement to follow the season. In terms of pitch, it corresponds to the Great Cluster; in a day, it is dawn (*yín*); in a month, the sixth to the eighth day’s half; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *yín*. At this time, yang qi emerges from the earth, like the body’s three yangs ascending—gradually rising, gradually lifting. One must swiftly drive the river chariot back into the crucible. Thus it is said: "Moving to follow the season." At this stage of the fire phase’s movement, one must not pause.

Yet there is *yín* of a day, and *yín* of a moment. As the *Zǐ* poem says:
*"Diligently swallow saliva a thousand times,
Long remember to guard the spirit at the fifth watch."*
This speaks of *yín* of a day.

The *Jīndān Dàchéng Jí* says:
*"When the three yangs meet the Tài hexagram,
One may advance the fire’s method and spiritual work."*
This speaks of *yín* of a moment.

The *yín* of a day naturally follows the celestial stems and branches as its measure—many in the world know this. But the *yín* of a moment is the body’s fire phase secret. Ancient and modern alchemical texts dare not speak of it plainly—truly, among thousands or tens of thousands, only one or two know it. Profound! Profound!

Gradually progressing through *Dàzhuàng*, the yang energy aligns with the *mǎo* gate, where elm pods fall and return to their root—*xíng* and *dé* counterbalance as day and night first divide.

*Dàzhuàng* is the hexagram of four yang lines, corresponding to the *Jiázhōng* pitch. In a day, it marks dawn at *mǎo*; in a month, the eighth to the tenth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *mǎo*. At this time, yin assists yang, qi gathers, and beings emerge. This illustrates the body’s yang fire at its midpoint, where the qi phase is evenly balanced—hence the saying, “*xíng* and *dé* counterbalance as day and night first divide.” Yet while all things flourish in spring, why do elm pods fall now? Because within yang, yin resides.

*Guài* is the hexagram where yin retreats as yang ascends, washing and shaking off the dust of old perches. *Guài* is the hexagram of five yang lines, corresponding to the *Gūxǐ* pitch. In a day, it marks the *chén* hour of the morning meal; in a month, the eleventh to the thirteenth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *chén*. Now yang qi is abundant, pressing near the celestial heights—illustrating the body’s yang fire rising. Thus it speaks of “yang ascending forward,” and also of “washing and shaking off the dust of old perches,” for when the great roc prepares to migrate to the celestial pond, it strikes the water to ascend, its momentum surging forth.

*Qián* is the hexagram of vigorous strength, its brilliance spreading to the four neighbors—yang culminates at *sì* and interacts. *Qián* is the hexagram of six yang lines, corresponding to the *Zhōnglǚ* pitch. In a day, it marks the *sì* hour of midday; in a month, the thirteenth to the fifteenth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *sì*. Now yang qi is at its peak, pervading all under heaven—illustrating the body’s yang fire reaching fullness, where the elixir’s radiance appears. The mountain spirit’s effluence divides into four streams, flowing down to nourish the land, its influence encompassing all—hence the saying, “spreading to the four neighbors.” It also speaks of “yang culminating at *sì* and interacting,” for when the yang fire’s number ends, the yin tally begins.

*Gòu* marks the inception of yin’s thread, treading frost first at the well’s cold spring. *Wǔ* is *Ruíbīn*, where the guest submits to yin, and yin becomes the host. *Gòu* is the hexagram of one yin line, corresponding to the *Ruíbīn* pitch. In a day, it marks noon at *wǔ*; in a month, the sixteenth to the eighteenth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *wǔ*. Now yin qi begins to arise—illustrating the body’s yin tally taking root. Once the numinous elixir enters the mouth, it returns to the cold spring. One must gently guide it back to the cinnabar field, without haste or urgency—hence the saying, “treading frost first at the well’s cold spring,” and also “the guest submits to yin, and yin becomes the host,” for when one yin governs, the many yangs become guests. “Guest” means reverence, caution against peril and vigilance against danger.

*Dùn* is the hexagram of withdrawing from the world, gathering one’s essence, cherishing virtue, and awaiting the time in obscurity. *Dùn* is the hexagram of two yin lines, corresponding to the *Línzhōng* pitch. In a day, it marks the *wèi* hour of the afternoon; in a month, the eighteenth to the twentieth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *wèi*. Now yin qi gradually lengthens—illustrating the body’s yin tally departing the *wǔ* position, withdrawing and descending, like a worthy retreating to seclusion in mountain valleys—hence the saying, “cherishing virtue, awaiting the time in obscurity.”

*Pǐ* is the hexagram of blockage, where nothing sprouts, yin advances and yang recedes, names and reputations are marred. *Pǐ* is the hexagram of three yin lines, corresponding to the *Yízé* pitch. In a day, it marks the *shēn* hour of dusk; in a month, the twenty-first to the twenty-third day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *shēn*. Now yang qi gradually wanes—illustrating the body’s yin tally descending further, like the three yins’ austere season when grasses and trees wither—hence the saying, “yin advances and yang recedes, names and reputations are marred.”

*Guān* is the hexagram of observing the scales, discerning the autumnal mood, where the young and tender are stored, the old and withered revive—shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout anew, sprouting forth under cover. *Guān* is the hexagram of four yin lines, corresponding to the *Nánlǚ* pitch. In a day, it marks the *yǒu* hour of sunset; in a month, the twenty-third to the twenty-fifth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *yǒu*. Now yin assists yang’s work, and all things contract to fruition—illustrating the body’s yin tally passing its midpoint, descending into the cinnabar field, like trees withdrawing blossoms to bear fruit—hence the saying, “storing the young and tender.” Yet while all things wither in autumn, why do shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout now? Because within yin, yang resides.

*Bō* is the hexagram of decay, where limbs rot and forms dissolve, qi transforms and exhausts, the utmost spirit is lost. *Bō* is the hexagram of five yin lines, corresponding to the *Wúshè* pitch. In a day, it marks the *xū* hour of twilight; in a month, the twenty-sixth to the twenty-eighth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *xū*. Now yang qi declines and perishes, fruits on branches rot and fall to the earth—illustrating the body’s yin tally nearing its end, where spiritual work has no place to act—hence the saying, “qi transforms and exhausts, the utmost spirit is lost.” Fire is born at *yín*, flourishes at *wǔ*, and is buried at *xū*—*xū* is the time of enclosure. The fire phase of a moment concludes here, as the *Fùmìng* chapter says: “East and west, movement and stillness, unite at dawn and dusk.” The fire phase of a day also rests here, as Lü Chunyang’s poem says: “Day after day, following him out and sinking again.” Trivial side gates and minor arts know only one aspect but not the other—how could they ever fully comprehend this?

When the Way is exhausted, it returns to *Kūn*’s origin, ever compliant with earth’s patterns, bearing heaven’s mandate to spread the profound and distant, bridging the subtle and obscure. It nurtures the seeds of yin and yang’s source, vast and indistinct, without discernible beginning. Those who lose the track first wander astray; later, they become the lord and ruler.

*Kūn* is the hexagram of six yin lines, corresponding to the *Yìngzhōng* pitch. In a day, it marks the *hài* hour of the night’s quiet; in a month, the twenty-eighth to the thirtieth day; in a year, the month when the Dipper’s handle points to *hài*. Now pure yin governs, and all things return to their root to renew their mandate—illustrating the body’s yin tally reaching its limit, then becoming utterly still, returning to the root and restoring tranquility—hence the saying, “When the Way is exhausted, it returns to *Kūn*’s origin.” “Ever compliant with earth’s patterns, bearing heaven’s mandate to spread” echoes the earlier passage: “The metal elixir arises from the sun, receiving the solar tally at dawn”—for heaven, earth, sun, and moon are one.

The moon receives the sun’s light, yet the sun is not diminished thereby—thus the moon’s light is the sun’s light. Heaven’s *qi* descends to earth, and all living things within the earth are born of heaven’s *qi*. The human body models heaven above and mirrors earth below; the interplay of yin and yang within it differs not from that of heaven and earth.

The *Returning to the Origin* chapter states: “With spirit returning to *qi*, the inner elixir’s Dao naturally forms.” If one can turn inward, gather spirit, and merge it into the *qi*-cavern, then spirit abides, life-*qi* congeals into liquid. When the celestial mechanism stirs, the red lotus holds its bud, dewdrops gather in jade hues, and as they descend into the Flower Pool, drop by drop, the cinnabar field coalesces—forming the elixir’s pivot.

“Vast and distant, faintly linked” refers to the juncture between *hài* and *zǐ*, the threshold where yin and yang exchange. At the moment when six yin lines reach their limit and the first yang has yet to arise, all is still and silent, as in the primordial chaos before heaven and earth divided. It is at this hour that the immortal forges the elixir: sealing the senses, lowering the eyelids, and directing the spirit’s light downward into the *kǎn* palace. At first, all is dim and obscure, like moonlight steeped in a frigid pool. Then spirit and *qi* unite, faintly communicating across barriers—akin to a lodestone drawing iron.

“Corresponding to pitch, nurturing the seed of yin and yang’s origin” describes the elixir-making process as mirroring the time when pure *Kūn* governs in the *hài* month. Then all things return to their root, sealing themselves in winter’s hibernation. Though winter rules concealment, the year’s generative power is in truth conceived within it—merely hidden, its traces unseen by men. Thus the ancients named this pure yin month the “yang month,” for at the *xiǎoxuě* solar term, yang *qi* already stirs beneath the six yin lines. Accumulating until the winter solstice, it fills one yang stroke, transforming into the *Fù* hexagram.

The elixir’s Dao follows the same pattern: when night’s *qi* is not yet spent, one gathers spirit, collects *qi*, and sits upright for a brief while. Soon spirit and *qi* return to their root, and from nothing, something arises—gradually congealing, accumulating into a single droplet of golden essence.

The *Emerald Canopy* chapter states: “Golden essence is the medicine of the *Kūn* palace.” *Kūn* lies in the southwest, the river’s source; the toad’s glow shines all day upon the western river—this is the medicine’s root. For the first yang does not arise in *Fù* but in *Kūn*. Though *Kūn* is utmost yin, within yin yang is born—thus it is the river-source that produces the medicine.

“Vast and indistinct, its beginning unknown” describes the moment when body and mind return to their mandate, spirit enters the vastness, and one becomes one with the Great Void. After long stillness, when the heart’s flower blooms, the three palaces fill with *qi*—all feels hazy and indistinct, its cause unfathomable.

For within the hazy and indistinct, there is substance; within the deep and obscure, there is essence—this is the crux of cultivation. The *Book of Jade Fungus* states: “These four lines have been recited by countless millions through the ages, yet how many truly grasp their meaning? Without direct experience, none can know.”

Thus, to depict its depth is like gazing into an abyss; to speak of its indistinctness is like waking from a daydream—this is the true scene, not mere analogy. Scholars who have not undergone this experience fail to recognize that the deep and indistinct, the hazy and vague, are the true landscapes of their own bodies. Too often, they dismiss them as empty rhetoric—what a pity!

Know this: the deep and obscure is the stillness before movement, the time before heaven and earth divided within one’s body. The hazy and indistinct is the moment of resonance and communication, the time when heaven and earth are about to separate within one’s body. The marvel of elixir-making lies precisely here—how can this be dismissed as empty rhetoric?

The *Red Dragon Great Elixir Formula* states: “None understand the indistinct; only I know the dim. Truly, few grasp this.” “First losing the track, later becoming lord and ruler” describes the initial state of profound obscurity, deeply entering into...

Within the profound obscurity, in but a fleeting moment, yin reaches its extreme and yang is born; stillness reaches its limit and the mechanism stirs. Then the gate of the face bursts open, the void shatters, and a radiance of wind and light breaks through the desolation—transforming power returns to one’s grasp.

Master Wei’s chapter clearly expounds the source of elixir-production and exhaustively discusses the secrets of existence and non-existence, host and guest, before and after—so thorough that nothing more can be added. The *Returning Gold Treatise* states: “Vast and distant, one but scoops the moon from water; distinct and clear, one only gathers the flower in the mirror.” Truly, none but those deeply versed in the Dao can recognize this.

“No level ground but slopes”—such is the natural transformation of the Way. Flourishing and decline, waxing and waning, all follow one another. From *kun* it ends, from *fu* it begins, like tracing an unbroken ring. Emperors and kings ride this current and endure for a thousand autumns.

“No level ground but slopes, no going but return”—this is the natural course of the Dao. In elixir-methods, stillness reaches its extreme and movement arises; movement reaches its extreme and stillness returns. One movement, one stillness, each the root of the other—this too arises from nature. Like the sun and moon in their course, one cold, one hot: when summer departs, winter comes; when winter departs, summer comes—all is natural.

The golden elixir is the *Yi* within the body. When the *Yi* is exhausted, it transforms; when it transforms, it flows. Flourishing and decline succeed one another; waxing and waning follow in turn. There has never been exhaustion that did not transform, transformation that did not flow. Yang begins at *fu* and yin ends at *kun*; end and beginning join, head and tail link—thus it is said, “From *kun* it ends, from *fu* it begins, like tracing an unbroken ring.” Master Wei discusses the firing times through the twelve *pi* hexagrams and employs the names of pitch-pipes and celestial stems in his exposition—all are metaphors.

For instance, “spokes converging” corresponds to *Taicu*, “flanking ranks” to *Jiazhong*, “washing” to *Guxi*, “center” to *Zhonglü*, “perching” to *Linzhong*, “bearing” to *Nanlü*, “wounding” to *Yize*, “perishing” to *wangshe*, “responding” to *Yingzhong*, “shaking” to *chen*, “dusk” to *wei*, “stretching” to *shen*, “storing” to *you*, “extinguishing” to *xu*, and “blocking” to *hai*—all are borrowed terms. One must not cling to the words or force interpretations that spawn unnecessary complications.

“Emperors and kings ride this current and endure for a thousand autumns” refers to the noble one who, throughout the day, remains *gan gan*, advancing with the times—truly, such a one can traverse a thousand autumns and endure.

If one wishes to nurture one’s nature and prolong one’s life, one must first carefully consider the end and then reflect on the beginning. One should ponder the original constitution of the body one received at birth—a single undifferentiated primordial essence, flowing and spreading, borne by qi from the outset.

Whether foolish or wise, all share this same nature, this same life. The noble one, knowing that nature must not be harmed, preserves and nurtures it; knowing that life must not be cut short, safeguards and extends it. Even without relying on cultivation, none fail to live in peace and meet a timely end. How much more so when one obtains the method of cultivation, steals from heaven and earth, seizes the workings of creation, refines the hun and po into one, and unites nature and life in dual cultivation—how could one not attain longevity?

To seek longevity, one must seek the state before one’s birth—how this body came to be—only then can one discuss the way of nurturing nature and prolonging life. Thus it is said: “Carefully consider the end, then reflect on the beginning.” For when a person is born, it is through the union of the parents’ qi that form is completed. Form is the foundation that receives qi; qi is the root that gives rise to form. If qi does not obtain form, there is no basis for existence; if form does not obtain qi, there is no means for completion. Hence it is said: “The body one receives at birth is originally a single undifferentiated primordial essence, flowing and spreading, borne by qi from the outset.”

Now, when immortals cultivate the elixir, they inwardly stimulate yin and yang, causing spirit and qi to unite, giving birth to being from non-being—this is fundamentally the same principle as the conception of a child in the womb. The ten months of gentle nurturing, the ceaseless true breathing day and night, are no different from the state before an infant is born. The *Song of the Great Dao’s Mystic Source* states:

*Just observe the infant in the womb—*
*How could it scheme with calculating mind?*
*It refines qi to utmost softness, spirit lingers long;*
*Incoming and outgoing, true breathing rests of itself.*

For when the infant is in the mother’s womb, when the mother exhales, it exhales; when the mother inhales, it inhales. Mouth and nose are closed, and all is conducted through the navel. Thus the navel is the root of life, the stem of qi.

Lü Chunyang’s *Song of the Mystic Female* says:

*Exhaustively seek the moment of receiving qi at birth—*
*No wonder the celestial mechanism is fully revealed.*
*If one can exhaustively seek the thought that gave rise to one’s birth,*
*Return to the source, turn the light inward, and illuminate within,*
*Gather the medicine in the southwest, at the root and stem of the kun abdomen,*
*Then one resembles the moment of receiving qi at birth—*
*What need is there for further seeking?*

Alas, scholars often cling to words and rigidly adhere to images, each insisting their own view is correct. Those who favor lofty ideas mistake the golden elixir for Chan Buddhism, taking the cultivation of nature as the mystic female and the stirring of thoughts as the birth of the first yang. They dismiss the practice of prolonging life as the “light beneath the skull.” Those of baser inclinations mistake the golden elixir for the art of bedroom cultivation, taking women as the crucible, the birth gate as the place of origin, and the absorption of essences as the medicinal powder. They mock the nurturing of nature as “dry sitting on a meditation cushion.” One group sinks into emptiness and stillness; the other falls into depravity and perversion—both cling to their errors for a lifetime without awakening. How deeply lamentable!

It is like a chicken’s egg: yin and yang, black and white, support each other, spanning but an inch at the beginning. Over time, the four limbs, five viscera, sinews, and bones take shape. After ten months, it hatches from the womb—bones weak enough to bend, flesh smooth as malt sugar. The black and white are the two qi of yin and yang.

When a person is conceived, it begins with the father’s essence and mother’s blood—the two qi of yin and yang temporarily unite to form a state of primordial chaos, like a chicken’s egg. After a hundred days, the male and female forms differentiate, and the four limbs, five viscera, sinews, and bones take shape. When the time comes, the child is born, emerging from the womb—bones weak, sinews soft, flesh tender and smooth—called an infant.

Those who refine the golden elixir cultivate the infant within their own body, and it too must undergo ten months of nurturing. The *Cui Xu Treatise* states:

*In the past, when I practiced for a year,*
*The six pulses stilled, qi returned to the root,*
*And an infant dwelled in the cinnabar field—*
*Its form and appearance were just like mine.*

Truly, in the cultivation of the sacred embryo, there has never been one who achieved swift success without ten months of effort.

Yin and yang serve as the measure where the hun and po reside. Heaven has the ecliptic as its measure—three hundred and sixty-five degrees. In its revolution, it completes one cycle each day. The sun and moon travel within it, moving up and down, alternating in their exits and entrances—this is how day and night are divided and cold and heat determined. Yet the celestial path’s subtle rotation has no fixed measure; it is through the sun and moon’s passage through the celestial stems that their course is measured. The sun and moon’s movements have no fixed abode; it is through their places of morning and evening exits and entrances that their residence is determined.

Understanding this, one then grasps what Master Wei meant by “yin and yang serve as the measure where the hun and po reside.” The ecliptic within the human body is the path where the yin tally and yang fire proceed—it is the direction where the sun’s hun and moon’s po reside. One who can observe heaven’s way, grasp heaven’s movement, recognize the course of yin and yang, and know the dwelling of the hun and po—such a one circulates the three hundred and sixty-five degrees of the celestial cycle within the span of a single breath, and the sun and moon exit and enter within the subtlety of inhalation and exhalation. Exhalation is yang, inhalation is yin—this shares the same wondrous function as the celestial path. There is no need to seek it elsewhere.

The yang spirit is the sun’s hun, the yin spirit is the moon’s po—hun and po serve as each other’s dwelling. An ancient song says: *"If one recognizes the sun’s hun and moon’s po, that one is a true immortal."* What is called the sun’s hun and moon’s po here is not the manifest, visible sun’s hun and moon’s po of heaven and earth, but rather the concealed, formless sun’s hun and moon’s po within our own bodies. Without the instruction of a true master, how could one recognize them?

Now, the sun is the Great Yang, its light unfolding by day, hence called *hun*; the moon is the Great Yin, its radiance contained by night, hence called *po*. The sun’s hun belongs to yang and is rightly termed the *yang spirit*, while the moon’s po belongs to yin and may properly be called *yin essence*—how could it be spoken of as *spirit*? Yet both are now called *spirit* because they are ceaselessly transforming, never abiding, circulating through the six directions, coming and going without fixed place or constant position.

The hun is the wood of the eastern direction; the po is the metal of the western direction. Metal and wood are originally of one body—when the eastern neighbor moves, it is immediately the western household. They alternate between east and west, each serving as the other’s dwelling, hence it is said they *mutually serve as chambers*.

Nature presides within, establishing the inner citadel; emotion governs without, ever fortifying the outer walls. When the walls are complete, all beings find peace. At this time, emotion harmonizes with heaven and earth.

*Nature* (性) means stillness; *presiding within* refers to sitting upright, emptying the heart, and turning the gaze inward. *Establishing the inner citadel* means seizing the golden essence to lay the foundation of life. *Emotion* (情) means movement; *governing without* refers first to the pivot of the mechanism, which moves day and night. *Ever fortifying the outer walls* means the dragon’s fire encircling all eight directions. When the walls are complete and all beings find peace, the qi surges through the joints, penetrating naturally, essence fills the valley, and the spirit of the valley abides. At this time, *emotion harmonizes with heaven and earth* means the river chariot never pauses, day or night, silently aligning with the great creation in its movement.

Qian is active and straight, its qi spreading, essence flowing; Kun is still and receptive, converging to form the Dao’s sanctuary. Qian is heaven, governing movement, unfolding all things to reach throughout the universe—hence it is said to be *straight*. Because it is active and straight, its essence and qi flow and spread, filling the three palaces without exception. Kun is earth, governing stillness, gathering all things to be stored within the soil—hence it is said to be *receptive*. Because it is still and receptive, its essence and qi return to the original sea, forming the Dao’s sanctuary. Generally, abiding in Qian means activity, and activity means qi spreads and essence flows; abiding in Kun means stillness, and stillness means qi gathers and essence congeals.

The firm descends and retreats; the yielding transforms and nourishes. When Qian’s yang descends to aid Kun, Kun’s yielding receptivity embraces it, giving birth to the medicine. Once the fire of refinement is applied, the firm descends and retreats, while the yielding yin ascends, transforming into sweet springs that moisten the nine realms.

Nine returns, seven reverses, eight reverts, six abides. The six, seven, eight, and nine correspond to water, fire, wood, and metal. In terms of trigrams, they are Kan, Li, Zhen, and Dui; in terms of directions, east, west, south, and north; in terms of lunar lodges, Xu, Fang, Xing, and Mao; in terms of symbols, the tortoise-snake, dragon, and tiger; in terms of seasons, spring, summer, autumn, and winter; in terms of earthly branches, zi, wu, mao, and you—all are the same entities.

Now, nine is called *return*, seven *reverse*, eight *revert*, yet six alone is called *abide*—this is because the north, the position of Kan, is the native home of true lead. When true lead abides here, the upright qi of the nine metals, eight woods, and seven fires, like spokes converging at the hub, like rivers flowing to the sea, all gather here.

Wang Baoyi’s commentary on the *Jinbi Longhu Jing* states: *"The sun has three illuminations, the moon three shifts. When the sun and moon emerge in the east and shine upon the west, the upright qi of the west’s White Tiger, the metal virtue, enters the mysterious barrier and transforms into the six wu. When the sun and moon set in the west and shine upon the east, the upright qi of the east’s Green Dragon, the wood virtue, enters the mysterious barrier and transforms into the six si. When the sun and moon reside in the south and shine upon the north, the upright qi of the south’s Vermilion Bird, the fire virtue, enters the mysterious barrier, takes earthly form, and transforms into black lead, ever abiding in the profound barrier as the root of heaven, earth, and the ten thousand things."* Wang’s explanation and Master Wei’s words may be said to be of different eras yet in perfect accord.

Male white, female red—metal and fire restrain each other, and thus water is stabilized. Water is the first of the five phases.

The *Cui Xu Pian* says: *"A strange matter that makes people laugh many times—now even men..."*
