Within the body’s chamber, conception arises—one’s own essence and blood naturally unite, forming the wondrous union of husband and wife. The sacred embryo of the Golden Elixir is born of the inner resonance of yin and yang, the conjoining of spirit and qi. "Male and female," "white and red"—these are but alternate names for the husband and wife within. When yin and yang inwardly resonate, spirit and qi unite like metal and fire restraining each other. As metal and fire restrain each other and settle in the north, within the Water of Kan, spirit congeals and qi gathers, and the water naturally stabilizes. Once water is stabilized, the medicinal substances coalesce.

The *Huán Jīn Piān* says: *"When water clarifies and congeals, it is like amber."* Water is the root-source of the Great Elixir. Heaven engenders water first; its position is in the north, its hexagram is Kan, and it stands at the head of the five phases. It is the homeland where the medicinal substances of our body are produced. If one can inwardly turn the light to reflect here—exhaling ever so slightly, inhaling ever so softly, without interruption—then spirit and qi return to the root, gradually entering, gradually softening, gradually harmonizing, and gradually stabilizing. When this stabilization endures, breath ceases, and the medicinal substances will naturally coalesce.

The highest good is like water, clear and flawless. The highest good is like water, flowing where it will without contention. The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is non-action, likewise flowing where it will without contention. The *Yǔlù* of Master Ma Danyang says: *"The cultivator of the Dao, whether walking, standing, sitting, or lying down, must not for a moment let the heart stray from the Dao. When walking, place the feet on level ground; when standing, concentrate the spirit in the Great Void; when sitting, regulate the breath at the tip of the nose; when lying down, embrace the pearl below the navel. With long and unbroken practice, one becomes as if dull-witted—this alone is the true work, not the contrived effort of forced discipline."* Is this not "flowing where it will without contention"?

The *Wù Zhēn Piān* says: *"Do not merely guard the furnace and watch the fire; simply settle the spirit and rest in naturalness."* The *Shàng Qīng Jí* says: *"The immortal’s art is not complex—it is merely a single taste of dullness in the human realm."* This, too, is "flowing where it will without contention."

Water’s nature is stillness and stability; when settled, it clarifies, but when disturbed, it muddies. The marvel of the Golden Elixir arises entirely from stillness and stability. The *Huán Yuán Piān* says: *"Know the medicine and the fire, and in stability, witness the Elixir’s formation."* For there has never been one who attained it without stability.

The Dao has no form or image; the True One is difficult to depict. It transforms and scatters, each part dwelling alone. *"There is a thing, chaotic yet complete, born before heaven and earth. I know not its name, so I style it the Dao."* It has no form to seek, no image to behold—how could it be captured in a diagram?

Long ago, Guangchengzi told the Yellow Emperor: *"The essence of the supreme Dao is profound and obscure; the ultimate of the supreme Dao is dark and silent."* In that profound obscurity and dark silence, not a single thought arises, and the ten thousand concerns vanish. All is merged and boundless, like the undivided Great Ultimate; vast and formless, like the unmanifested Two Modes. Only this one thing remains, serene and solitary, like the moon reflected in a clear abyss, motionless as still water without ripples. One knows not what is lead or what is mercury—this is called the True One. When the time arrives and qi transforms and scatters, the light and pure ascend, while the heavy and turbid settle below. Thus, in the Kan Palace there is lead, and in the Li Palace there is mercury. What was once merged and boundless, vast and formless, now divides into two, each dwelling alone.

The *Fù Mìng Piān* says: *"One thing divides into two—knowing these two is to name the furnace and cauldron. The dripping of the water clock has reached the third watch."* This is what it refers to.

The great medicine of the Golden Elixir is conceived in the prior heaven and born in the posterior heaven. Its marvel lies in the moment when the Great Ultimate is about to divide yet remains undivided—when stillness has reached its extreme but has not yet given way to movement, when yang is about to return but has not yet departed from yin. Here, the mechanism of heaven and earth converges, and the place where the Elixir coalesces is known. Recognizing the secret of where to begin, one finds something within the indistinct, draws true essence from the profound, and realizes that the Great Dao arises from non-being into being. Truly, the marvel of the True One cannot be captured in a diagram.

Yet, in the end, is there no way to reveal the marvel of the True One? The yang-sui mirror does not produce fire without the sun; the fang-zhu vessel cannot yield water without the moon and stars. Though the two qi are suspended far apart, their resonance and transformation still communicate—how much more so when they are intimately present within the heart and breast, where yin and yang pair like the sun and moon, and water and fire respond as evidence?

The *Zhou Li*, in the *Autumn Offices*, states that the *Si Xuan Shi* is charged with using the fu-sui to draw bright fire from the sun and the mirror to draw bright water from the moon. The *Huainanzi* says: *"When the fang-zhu faces the moon, it exudes water."* Gao You’s commentary notes: *"The fang-zhu is the yin-sui, a large clam."* Though the earth is separated from heaven by countless thousands of miles, and the sun and moon hang in the void at an equal distance from the earth, the yang-sui, when facing the sun, obtains fire, and the fang-zhu, when facing the moon, obtains water. How swiftly their resonance and transformation communicate!

How much more so when they are intimately present within the heart and breast? Within the body, there is water and fire; within the heart, there are medicinal materials. Should one not inwardly turn the light to reflect and seek the marvel of this resonance? Moreover, on the bright night of the fifteenth of the eighth month, the *xiao* in the deep mountains forms its embryo, and the oyster in the blue sea forms its pearl. What causes their resonance and transformation to be so marvelously swift?

Born between heaven and earth as the most intelligent of the ten thousand things, humans cannot steal from heaven and earth or seize the workings of creation—they are not even the equal of the hare or the oyster. Have those who study immortality ever paid heed to this?

Generally, when yin and yang attain harmony, their resonance is natural. Master Wei used the yang-sui to draw fire and the fang-zhu to draw water as proof, urging cultivators to inwardly observe and realize being within non-being. Their resonance and transformation are just like this.

The *Huán Dān Piān* says: *"When the two qi meet, sweet rain descends; when the two spirits unite, jade nectar flows."* This is indeed so.

The mirror and the pearl are both luminous and pure, transparent inside and out, without the slightest blemish. Thus, they can gather the light of the sun and moon into a single grain of brightness. Though vast and distant, they respond immediately. Yet when settled, they gather, and gathering, they exist; when moved, they scatter, and scattering, they vanish. If one can empty the heart, concentrate the spirit, and inwardly stabilize without the slightest stray thought, then the light of one’s own sun and moon will gather without scattering. When qi stabilizes over time, wondrous effects will naturally arise.

The supreme Dao is not far off—it is ever before one’s eyes. It is like the magnet that draws iron, though obstructed, its resonance is intimate and near. Yet the world’s people are deluded and turn away, unable to seek it within.

Ning Xuanzi’s poem says:
*"Diligently, I discuss with master and disciple—
Not elsewhere, but right before one’s eyes."*
And again:
*"That one phrase received from the master’s side,
Secretly bestowed, carefully observed—
How very near it is!"*

The ear, eye, and mouth—three treasures—must be firmly sealed and not discharged. The *Yinfu Jing* states: "The nine orifices’ deviance lies in the three essentials." What are these three essentials? The ear, eye, and mouth. Here, Master Wei designates them as the "three treasures" and says they must be "firmly sealed and not discharged," urging those who cultivate and refine to restrain the ear’s and eye’s acuity, seal the throat’s and tongue’s true qi, and gather all inward without letting it leak outward—like treasuring and concealing a precious jewel.

Have you not seen the pot and steamer cooking rice? The lid is sealed tightly above, firewood burns below, and no crack—even the thinnest—exists on the outside, while within, steam swirls and gathers. In an instant, the rice is steamed and done. Now, when one enters the chamber to cultivate and refine, one must withdraw sight and return hearing, close the mouth and hold the saliva, allowing not the slightest leakage of true qi. Only then will the supreme medicine arise and the great elixir ripen. If the ear is drawn by sound, the eye pulled by form, and the "towering pavilion" (breath) flows out ceaselessly—if all three are discharged without restraint—how can the returned elixir ever be sought?

The true person abides in the deep abyss, floating and wandering, guarding the center of the compass. This true person is the primordial spirit.

The "deep abyss" is the Great Abyss—its alternate names are many. Let us now enumerate them: Mud-Pill Palace, Flowing Pearl Palace, Jade Purity Palace, Purple Purity Palace, Emerald Microcosm Palace, Grand Microcosm Palace, Supreme Unity Palace, Supreme Mystery Pass, Mystery Gate, Mystery Palace, Mystery Chamber, Mystery Valley, Mystery Field, Cinnabar Field, First Pass, Capital Pass, Heavenly Pass, Heavenly Gate, Heavenly Valley, Heavenly Field, Heavenly Heart, Heavenly Wheel, Heavenly Axis, Heavenly Source, Heavenly Pool, Heavenly Root, Heavenly Hall, Heavenly Palace, Qian Palace, Qian Household, Intermingling Palace, Li Palace, Spirit Palace, Spirit Chamber, Spirit Pass, Spirit Capital, Spirit Metropolis, Mystery Metropolis, Former Metropolis, Former Homeland, Former Mound, Former Grove, Former Palace, Purple Court, Purple Court, Purple Gold City, Purple Gold Crucible, Cinnabar Crucible, Mercury Crucible, Jade Crucible, Jade Chamber, Jade Capital, Jade Universe, Jasper Peak, First Peak, Supreme Peak, Zhurong Peak, Kunlun Summit, Kongtong Mountain, Penglai Upper Isle, Upper Capital, Upper Palace, Upper Mystery, Upper Prime, Upper Valley, Upper Earth, Cauldron, Upper Cinnabar Field. Though these names are many, their essence is one.

The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Heaven has seven stars, earth seven treasures, and man seven orifices—all authority returns to the brain." The *Tai Gu Ji* says: "When the golden elixir circulates to the Mud-Pill Cavity, the name and surname are first inscribed in the Jade Capital." The Dao of the Former Emperor’s return flows upstream through the hundred meridians to replenish the Mud-Pill, and when the brain is full, the spirit is whole; when the spirit is whole, the form is whole.

Now, when Master Wei speaks of "the true person abiding in the deep abyss, floating and wandering, guarding the center of the compass," he refers to following the true breath’s ebb and flow, allowing the true qi to rise and descend. From dawn to dusk, the primordial spirit constantly dwells in the Mud-Pill. The *Huangting Jing* says: "If you wish not to die, cultivate Kunlun." In quietude, one chants: "I cultivate Kunlun and obtain the true formula." The *Fuming Pian* states: "Return to my home and cultivate a mu of Heavenly Field." The *Huanyuan Pian* says: "Awakening to the Dao is luminously clear; in spacious leisure, sit upright and move the Heavenly Pass." This is the simplest and easiest of ways: merely stir the pass at the crown of the head, and in even, silent stillness, lift the Three Palaces. Naturally, ascent and descent occur, and the hundred bones and myriad orifices become unobstructed—like a ten-thousand-hulled boat set in motion by a single span of wood, or a thousand-jun crossbow triggered by a single inch of mechanism. Yet it expends not the slightest effort; only the deluded fail to believe it. Some visualize Lord Lao in the Mud-Pill Palace and silently pay homage—this is truly superfluous.

Turning and curving to survey, opening and closing all conform to the axis of the self; movement and stillness are inexhaustible. The *Qiong Huangji Jingshi Shu* says: "The spirit of heaven dwells in the sun; the spirit of man emerges from the eyes." How great indeed!

The human spirit emerges from the eyes—when the body is born, this essence precedes heaven and earth; when the body perishes, this essence outlasts heaven and earth. The Five Phases of water, fire, wood, metal, and earth converge here; the five viscera of liver, heart, spleen, lungs, and kidneys gather their numinous power here; the seven substances of saliva, mucus, essence, fluids, qi, blood, and marrow crystallize their brilliance here. In its vastness, it can contain heaven and earth; in its subtlety, not even a mote of dust can enter. Is this not the great treasure within this single body?

The *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* states: "Containing the light is the elixir of long life." The *Shangpin Xian* says: "Gather the twin pupils before the eyes and refine them—this alone can guard against the golden immortals." Such direct instructions are given, yet scholars may still fail to awaken. How deeply benighted they are!

For the Three Palaces rise and descend, ascending and descending without end—like a wheel in ceaseless motion. Their operation lies in the heart, as the wheel’s hub; their pivot rests in the eyes, as the axle’s linchpin. The *Jinhua Shi* says: "The immortal lad guards only the cavern’s gate, while the true person of the Three Isles eternally comes and goes." This is indeed the case.

When *li* qi flourishes within, defense is perfected; when *kan* ceases to rely on hearing, *dui* unites without speech, and words grow rare to follow the primordial chaos. These three, once secured, the body relaxes in an empty chamber.

*Li* qi flourishes within defense when the eyes are drawn inward and the light is not revealed. *Kan* ceases to rely on hearing when the ears are turned inward to listen, and hearing is not leaked. *Dui* unites without speech, words grow rare, and primordial chaos is followed when the heart’s chamber is as cold as ash, utterly silent—guarding the sacred embryo. Once these three are thus secured, the body should relax in an empty chamber.

To relax the body is to move slowly, with ease and without straining the form. To dwell in an empty chamber is to enter a quiet room—within it, nothing else should be placed, save a single incense burner, a lamp, a small table, and a couch. The seating should be neither too bright (lest it harm the *hun*) nor too dark (lest it harm the *po*). For the *hun* favors brightness, the *po* favors darkness; brightness belongs to yang, darkness to yin. Thus the *Cuixu Pian* advises: "The chamber should face wood, toward the morning sun, with *dui*’s brightness facing the evening light," and also: "Do not rest with a bright lamp or a hundred harmonies."

Entering a quiet chamber to cultivate is a matter of the greatest karmic affinity—how could it be a trivial undertaking? Have you not seen the *Wu Zhen Pian*? It says: "This Way is most divine and sacred; I fear your allotment is too meager to bear it." How could one without virtue and conduct dare covet it?

Yet the quiet chamber need not be confined to mountain forests—it may be within a city wall or a village of the Dao. Wherever one finds refuge, there is no place it cannot be. Some doubt the *Wu Zhen Pian*’s mention of relying on the powerful in bustling cities, but this refers to the time before the great elixir is formed, when one cannot endure the cold and heat of the four seasons in a single year, or the hunger and thirst of the three daily meals before true qi is complete. Thus Wu Daling found Zhang and Cheng as friends; Zhang Ziyang found Ma and Lu as patrons; Wang Chongxi relied on the wealthy Han; Li Changyuan depended on his kinsmen of Junyang. All achieved success through the fire-times of the celestial revolution, requiring unbroken effort. One must have like-minded, capable companions to provide protection, supplies, and service, shielding one from the theft of qi by hunger, cold, and hardship—only then can one focus wholeheartedly on cultivation.

The *Yin Zhenjun Jinye Huandan Ge* says: "Without the proper place, do not act recklessly." The *Wu Zhen Pian* says: "The treasure of life should not be lightly toyed with." Those who attain the true transmission of this Way will know this for themselves.

Entrust your will to emptiness and nothingness, making this your constant practice. To entrust your will to emptiness and nothingness is to keep the heart free of杂念 and the mind from wandering outward, dwelling all day in true emptiness. To make this your constant practice is to let thoughts follow one another without interruption, aligning the breath with the celestial Dao.

Some suspect this method resembles Chan practice, but they do not realize that the golden elixir arises from nothing to become something, nurturing the infant immortal—this is not the same as sitting like a block of wood or dead ash. The *Bai Ziqing Yulu* says: "The first oral instruction for refining the elixir is to gather qi and congeal spirit. Constantly maintain firmness—this is gathering qi. Thought after thought, guard silence—this is congealing spirit." If scholars only know emptiness of heart and thought but do not gather qi and congeal spirit, they fall into dead emptiness—how then can the immortal embryo be formed?

Proof shifts with the progress of the heart’s focus. With three parts effort, there is three parts proof; with ten parts effort, ten parts proof. If one practices diligently, day and night without rest, after a hundred days of effort, the numinous power manifests: the two kidneys become as if scalded, the bladder as if afire, the eyes radiate spirit-light, and the ears...

The ears hear subtle sounds, the nose detects rare fragrances, and the mouth fills with sweet fluids—this body becomes supple and flowing, its proofs shifting day by day. What is prized in maintaining a focused heart without distraction is the very fear that, should one’s grasp of principle remain incomplete, one might be ensnared by demonic realms during the liminal state between stillness and dream. The *Thirty-Ninth Chapter of the Pointer to the Mystery* states: "Do not cling to external phenomena; single-mindedly guard the coiled tiger and dragon." This is indeed the teaching. Ding Lingyang’s *Heart and Nature Secrets* says: "If, in deep stillness, one suddenly sees immortals, Buddhas, demons, spirits, towers, or radiant lights—any phenomena appearing before the eyes—one must not generate attachment or aversion." The master says: "Within the vast emptiness of one’s own nature, let the myriad transformations unfold. If the heart remains unmoved, all delusions will naturally retreat. When the heart-fire does not arise, spirit and qi converge, mother and child unite, and naturally water and fire achieve harmony. As water encounters fire, it transforms into qi, rising to permeate every orifice, naturally regulating the hundred vessels and harmonizing the four great elements. Guard this diligently, and the Dao will spontaneously align."

Wang Xi’s *Panshan Record* states: "In stillness, the practitioner may encounter manifold illusions, all conjured by the discriminating mind, arising in quietude to lure the sovereign heart. Have you not heard the ancient saying: *All phenomena are illusory*? If the mind seeks to dispel the discriminating consciousness while it yet lingers, it will manifest forms—demonic faces and spirit-heads—to disturb the sovereign heart. If the sovereign remains unmoved, seeing as if not seeing, the body merges with emptiness, leaving no trace to grasp, and the illusions will naturally dissolve." Guan Yinzi says: "When a person’s eyes suddenly perceive extraordinary things in ordinary times, it is because essence has congealed to produce them. When a sick person’s eyes suddenly perceive extraordinary things, it is because the heart harbors deficiency. If one knows that the heart can manifest being from non-being, one also knows it can manifest non-being from being. Simply do not believe in them, and they will naturally lose their numinosity." Someone asked: "When consciousness is dimmed, who can avoid belief?" I replied: "Like the snake-catcher whose heart fears not the serpent, though he dreams of snakes, he feels no terror." The Yellow Emperor said: "The Dao has no demons or spirits; it moves alone, comes alone." Observing these teachings, we understand why Master Wei’s admonition—"focus the heart without distraction"—is indeed the essential principle for cultivation.

In sleep, spirit embraces spirit; upon waking, one attends to the moments of presence and absence. Essence arises at its appointed time, and spirit knows the hundred divisions of the day. Beware of dullness and confusion; one must maintain unbroken awareness in movement and stillness, as if brooding over an egg, keeping the warmth unceasing—only then can it be called cultivation. With sincere accumulation and prolonged effort, skill becomes pure, and day and night merge into one, with no distinction between dream and waking. Even in sleep, spirit remains lucid, and when essence arises, spirit communes with heaven. Though uncalled, it awakens of itself. Xia Yunfeng said: "The natural timing is even in dreams." This is the teaching.

Now Master Wei speaks of "in sleep, spirit embracing spirit; upon waking, attending to presence and absence," urging cultivators to remain ever vigilant. The great elixir of golden cinnabar is formed through the union of spirit and qi, transforming the insubstantial into substance to gestate the sacred embryo. One must guard it with diligence for ten months, like a young woman’s first pregnancy or a small dragon newly nurturing its pearl. As spirit and qi first congeal, they are easily scattered. In sleep, one must embrace spirit closely, never sinking into dreamy confusion. Upon waking, one must fear the cooling of the fire and the weakening of the elixir’s power, thus attending to its presence and absence. Throughout the twelve hours of the day, without distinction between day and night, one must remain ever mindful. Only then will the effort become pure, the medicinal materials not dissipate, and the firing times not falter. How can one ever depart from this for even a moment?

The complexion gradually moistens, the bones and joints grow stronger.

The complexion gradually moistens and the bones and joints grow ever stronger—this is because the supreme treasure is stored within, and naturally its essence radiates outward. It is like jade in the mountains that moistens the trees, or a pearl in the deep that keeps the cliffs from withering. Only after dispelling all yin and evil can true yang be established. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says: “When the host of yin is stripped away and the elixir ripens, one leaps free from the cage to live ten thousand years.”

The human body, from top to bottom, is nothing but yin’s dross and turbid impurities. The method of immortal cultivation is to generate being from non-being, seizing the one measure of true yang from Heaven and Earth to congeal the elixir’s beginning. Then, day and night, one circulates the fire to refine away yin qi. After ten months of refinement, the embryo is complete; after three years, the work is done. Only then does the body transform into pure yang, the form alchemically transmuted into an immortal.

If one refines without ceasing, the qi will become like clouds and rain, flowing abundantly as spring moisture, trickling like melting ice from head to foot, then rising again in endless circulation. As the *Neizhi Tongxuan Mijue* says: “Work the great elixir day and night without rest, and the essence will pierce the crown as the hundred spirits gather above.” After a year of seclusion, practicing night and day without pause, the effects will naturally manifest. Harmonious qi will suffuse the entire body, billowing like mountain clouds ascending into the great void, suffusing like fine rain across the plains, brimming like spring waters filling the four marshes, flowing like river ice on the verge of melting. It circulates up and down, surging through the hundred vessels, filling the valleys within, coursing through the four limbs. The heart is full, brimming with spring’s warmth, and one’s state resembles mild intoxication.

The *Ruyao Jing* says: “Whether it be precelestial or postcelestial qi, those who attain it are ever as if drunk.” The *Lingguang Ji* says: “Inverted circulation feels like drunkenness, neither sorrowful nor joyful, inwardly preserving true reality—all are the wondrous effects of elixir work.” Ding Lingyang’s *Huiguang Ji* says: “If a single thought is unborn, then naturally within the dantian and sea of qi, the essence of great yin crosses the Weilu acupoint, passes the Jiaji double gates, ascends through Fengfu to Niwan, then descends through the Hall of Light and the bridge of the nose into the Flower Pool, where it transforms into sweet nectar. Swallowed, it irrigates the five viscera and six bowels, reaching the dantian. It flows up and down, filling the four great [elements], circulating endlessly without fail.”

If one practices only in the morning and abandons it by evening, or if one’s will is not single-minded, how can one progress in this? Reversion is the proof of the Dao; weakness is the handle of virtue. Reversion means returning repeatedly. The *Daode Jing* says: “Attain utmost emptiness, guard stillness firmly. The ten thousand things arise together, and I thereby observe their return.” The effects of elixir refinement emerge from the utmost emptiness and firm stillness, merging with Heaven and Earth. Only then does primordial qi return with the coming of the one yang. If emptiness is not utmost and stillness not firm, it will be like the mountain with nine measures of earth—one basket short of completion. How can one expect any effect?

Weakness means softness. The *Daode Jing* says: “Concentrate qi to attain softness—can you be like a newborn babe?” The root and handle of elixir refinement lie in holding fast to one’s will without violently expending one’s qi. Only by being as soft and weak as an infant can one succeed. If one’s will is not guarded and qi not nurtured, what little is gained will be lost in an avalanche—what then can serve as the root and handle?

Weed out long-accumulated impurities, and even the finest details will become harmonious and smooth. When all concerns are stilled, the hundred bones are all ordered; when defensive qi dissipates, the Dao’s qi remains. It is like a farmer who must remove weeds: cutting and clearing them to the roots, preventing regrowth, so that the good may flourish. Now, in the immortal’s refinement of the elixir, one weeds out long-accumulated impurities, drives away the demonic corpse, quiets the six senses, and empties the five aggregates within right mindfulness—clear, luminous, empty, and radiant. Only then are the five viscera cool and serene, the six bowels harmonious and at peace, the three hundred sixty joints free of obstruction, and the eighty-four thousand pores all unblocked.

The path from turbidity to clarity: when dullness lingers, brightness will come. The *Cuixu Pian* says: “When spirit and qi merge in darkness, qi returns—bones and flesh fuse, all unawares.” At this time, the three fields are full of qi, and one feels as if in a drunken dream. Can this not be called dull and turbid? Yet when scholars reach this state, they must not collapse. Know that prolonged dullness will surely give way to clarity, and prolonged turbidity to purity. When the time comes and qi transforms, the nine heavens’ message will scatter the embryo’s membrane. Then the divine water will be deep and clear, the Flower Pool still, white snow will swirl and fly across the four mountains, and the twelve-storied tower of the seven-jeweled pavilion will be visible. All will be bright and distinct, seen within the realm of the indistinct—how could it remain merely dull and turbid?

Yet worldly people favor minor arts, failing to discern the depth of the Dao. They abandon the correct path for deviant shortcuts, seeking speed but finding no passage—like the blind refusing a staff, or the deaf trying to hear music. They fish for pheasants and hares in water, or seek fish and dragons on mountaintops. They plant wheat hoping for millet, or use a compass to draw a square, exhausting their spirit and strength year after year without result.

The great Dao of the golden elixir is accomplished through accumulating essence and amassing qi—it cannot be attained in a single leap. A moment forms the embryo, a hundred days establishes the foundation, a year enters seclusion, and three years of refinement and nurturing follow a natural sequence. There is no principle by which one meets a master today and ascends as an immortal tomorrow. Yet how often do worldly scholars, deluded by hearsay, fail to discern the depth of the Dao or distinguish between true and false teachers? They seek quick results, favoring minor arts, knowing one thing but not another, grasping the form but not the function. Confined by partial views, they consider themselves sufficient, even promoting deviant teachings while slandering the true Dao. They distort authentic scriptures to gain the trust of the ignorant, recklessly teaching the deluded and misleading the misguided—such cases are many.

Some clever individuals refuse to humble themselves to seek instruction, instead poring over old texts in hopes of enlightenment—how is this different from the blind refusing a staff? The foolish and ignorant, not understanding the highest principles, force their conjectures to fit the alchemical texts—how is this different from the deaf trying to hear music? Some take the navel as the mysterious pass—this is like fishing for pheasants and hares in water. Others take the two eyes as the Flower Pool—this is like seeking fish and dragons on mountaintops. Still others practice sexual arts hoping for immortality, or sit in stubborn emptiness aspiring to longevity—are these not like planting wheat to harvest millet? Or using a compass to draw a square? All are deluded and unenlightened, wasting their efforts in vain, until their hair turns white in confusion, achieving nothing. What a pity!

To know the method of ingesting the elixir: it is most concise and unburdensome. The *Thirty-Nine Pointers to the Mysterious* states, "That which is inwardly luminous comes as the utmost truth; what enters from without is not intimate." This means the elixir resides within the body—there is no need to seek it externally. Yet Master Wei speaks of "ingesting the elixir"—does this not imply the elixir comes from without and is swallowed through the mouth? The answer is no. The elixir is born within the Kan trigram, forced out by fire, traverses the Three Palaces, descends, and enters the mouth—hence it is called "ingestion." "Most concise and unburdensome" refers to the oral instructions, which are supremely simple and easy.

If it is indeed so simple and easy, then the *Cantong Qi* need not have been written. Why then does it stretch on endlessly, filling scroll after scroll? I have also seen the *Wuzhen Pian*, which says, "The Great Elixir’s method is supremely simple and easy; even the dullest and most ignorant may practice it and instantly transcend to the realm of sages." Yet it also states, "Those who refine the golden elixir must thoroughly comprehend yin and yang and deeply penetrate the workings of creation." Why this contradiction? Ah, there is an explanation.

The dull and ignorant, free from overthinking, readily believe. Once they encounter the method, they follow it directly without hesitation. Though they lack deep understanding, they may still succeed. The wise and learned, however, are broad in knowledge and often beset by doubts. If one point remains unclear, they will seek to exhaust its depths before proceeding. Should they fail to fully grasp the origins of yin, yang, and creation, they may grow skeptical midway. If swayed by heterodox teachings that seem plausible, they may abandon the path, casting aside the shuttle and loom, and declare the true Way false. Thus, the method cannot but be explained in full.

Therefore, though Master Wei may have wished to keep his words unadorned, how could he have done so? The flowing pearl of the Great Yang ever seeks to depart from man, but once it obtains the Golden Flower, it turns and clings, transforming into a white liquid that congeals into utmost hardness. The flowing pearl of the Great Yang is the numinous mercury. The *Song of the Numinous Source of the Great Dao* says, "This substance has no fixed position; it changes with the times, following the heart’s intent. In the body, when stirred by sensation, it becomes sweat; when the nose meets wind, it becomes mucus; when the kidneys unite, it becomes essence; when the eyes grieve, it becomes tears." Thus, it ever seeks to depart from man. The Golden Flower is true lead. When mercury obtains true lead, it turns and clings, naturally neither flying nor fleeing, lingering within and transforming into golden liquid, congealing into utmost hardness.

The Golden Flower first arises, and in a moment, dissolves into water. The horse’s teeth stand in rows; yang then goes forth to harmonize with nature and temperament. True lead is born in the Kan position, first liquid, then congealing into the Yellow Sprout—hence the metaphor of the horse’s teeth standing in rows. The *Cui Xu Pian* says, "The elixir’s head is nothing but the primordial qi refined into the Yellow Sprout, giving rise to the Jade Flower." Lead is the sovereign; mercury, the minister. Lead moves first, and mercury responds—like a ruler initiating and a minister harmonizing. Lead belongs to metal; mercury, to wood. Wood’s nature loves metal; metal’s essence clings to wood. Yin and yang unite naturally.

When the time is pressing, yin is constrained and the gate is sealed. "When the time is pressing, yin" refers to the proper moment, when one must swiftly gather the medicine. "Constraining and sealing the gate" means returning with the gathered medicine to the furnace for refinement. The *Huanyuan Pian* says, "One need only align with the celestial mechanism—who knows where the elixir is formed?" What is aligning with the celestial mechanism? It is pressing the time of yin. What is the place where the elixir is formed? It is constraining and sealing the gate. One must draw near the moment when yin reaches its extreme, welcoming the first stirrings of yang to advance the fire. It must not be overdone, nor should it be insufficient—this is what is meant by "pressing the time of yin." When advancing the fire, urge the hundred vessels to return to the source, penetrate the nine passes to the very end. The sovereign fire, ministerial fire, and common fire are all gathered in the Yellow Palace and refined into the supreme treasure—this is what is meant by "constraining, storing, and sealing the gate." The *Ruyao Jing* says, "Heaven and earth are stingy with their numinous transformations. Without pressing the time of yin and constraining and sealing the gate, how could one steal their mechanism?"

The compassionate mother nurtures; the filial son repays her kindness. They interlock and swallow, chewing and devouring one another. The stern father issues commands, instructing his descendants. When true lead is born, it is conceived in the womb of the Kun mother—like a compassionate mother’s nurturing. When it flies up to the Qian Palace, captures and restrains true mercury, and unites with it to form the elixir, it then returns to the Kun mother’s womb—this is like the filial son repaying his mother’s kindness, the kind crow returning to feed its parent. Yet without the fierce fire of scorching refinement, true lead cannot take flight—this is why the stern father issues commands to spur it on. The *Yuzhi Shu* says, "Lead does not fly without fire; mercury does not congeal without lead." There are subtle precautions here—how could this be spoken of lightly?

The five phases intermingle, each in its sovereign position, relying on one another to generate. Fire’s nature melts metal; metal subdues wood, which flourishes. Metal generates water, and wood generates fire—this is the ordinary sequence of the five phases. But in the elixir’s method, wood and fire are companions, and fire in turn generates wood. Metal and water unite, and water in turn generates metal—hence it is said, "The five phases intermingle, each in its sovereign position, relying on one another to generate." "Fire’s nature melts metal" means employing the fire of the Li Palace in the south to refine the metal within the water of the Kan Palace in the north. "Metal subdues wood, which flourishes" means employing the metal within the water of the Kan Palace in the north to restrain the wood within the fire of the Li Palace in the south.

The Three and the Five unite as one—the utmost essence of heaven and earth. This can be transmitted orally but is difficult to convey in writing. The Three refers to water (one) and fire (two), combining to form three. The Five is earth. "The Three and the Five unite as one" means water, fire, and earth intermingle and fuse into a single qi. At this time, the mysterious yellow is intermingled, and the clear and turbid are not yet separated—like the primordial chaos of heaven and earth. Soon, the time arrives, and qi transforms from nothing into something. In the profound darkness, something indistinct arises; in the indistinctness, something hazy forms. It coalesces into a mass, and within it, the utmost essence of heaven and earth is conceived. Qutu Shenhe said, "If you understand the Three and the Five as one, you may become an earth-treading immortal." Its wonders must be transmitted orally, heart to heart—difficult to fully express in writing.

The child turns to the right; noon then revolves to the east. Mao and You divide the boundaries, giving rise to the names of host and guest. "The child turns to the right" means moving from the west to the Zi position, where the tiger is born within water.

The noon then revolves to the east—this means moving from the east to the noon position, where the dragon emerges from within fire. Zi and Wu are south and north; south and north are water and fire. Mao and You are east and west; east and west are metal and wood. Turning right and revolving left, one conceals and one arises, so that water and fire interact while metal and wood remain unobstructed.

The *Huanyuan Pian* states:
*"The jade rabbit’s wheel and golden rooster’s path—*
*the Way resides within the human body,*
*yet people themselves are deluded.*
*All know how to regulate water and fire,*
*but how many recognize east and west?"*

For east and west, along with Mao and You, are but alternate names for metal and wood—they are neither celestial directions nor the left and right of the human body. Yet one must not seek them outside oneself.

Alas! In recent times, many in the world don robes and feign Dao, yet when asked what metal and wood are, they fall silent as if struck dumb. Unless they encounter a true master’s direct instruction, they merely speculate in vain and never truly comprehend.

The dragon calls to the tiger, the tiger inhales the dragon’s essence—both devour each other, each coveting the other. The dragon is the Li dragon of the south; the tiger is the Kan tiger of the north. When refining

Just as the autumn cicada drinks the wind, the rhinoceros gazes at the stars, the aged clam holds the moon in its mouth, the unyielding stone harbors jade within, the butterfly’s wings open and close, the firefly’s flame brightens and dims, the cat’s pupils expand and contract, the deer’s tail moves in reverse, the *qi* breathes in and out, the turtle shoots its shadow, the windbag puffs the breeze, the water droplet draws in moisture, the lodestone attracts the needle, amber gathers the mustard seed, the well-sweep and windlass draw water, sweet flag and rice blossoms condense dew, the snake enters hibernation, the fish dwells in water, the dung beetle rolls its ball, the wasp enchants its young, the hen broods over her eggs, the rabbit carries her embryo, the ox bears its yellow, the dragon possesses its pearl, the plum pit grows its kernel, the lotus fruit sheds its stem—along with the spring wind’s mechanism in the plantain, the autumn rain’s secret in the paulownia, the night moon’s reflection in the emerald pool, and the evening clouds over the blue mountains—all are but emblems of the Golden Elixir’s method.

Zhang Ziyang said: *"One who refines the Golden Elixir must thoroughly understand yin and yang and deeply penetrate the workings of creation—how could this not be true?"*

The Five Phases overcome one another, becoming father and mother in turn. The mother contains the nourishing fluids; the father governs the bestowal of essence. In the essential method of the Elixir, nothing surpasses this.

The marvel of the Five Phases lies nowhere more profoundly than in Kan and Li. Kan is Water, where Metal and Water unite, and within Water resides Metal. Li is Fire, where Water and Fire become companions, and within Fire resides Wood. These form the Four Symbols. When Kan receives Wu Earth and Li receives Ji Earth, they complete the Five Phases. As the *Wuzhen Pian* states: *"The Zhen Dragon’s mercury emerges from the Li region; the Dui Tiger’s lead is born in the Kan direction. These two substances are both produced by the child giving birth to the mother."* It further says: *"If Kan and Li lack Wu and Ji, though they contain the Four Symbols, they cannot form the Elixir. Only because each holds true Earth within does the Golden Elixir achieve its return and restoration."* This makes the principle clear.

In the Elixir’s method, Fire refines Metal, and Metal subdues Wood. When Fire flourishes, Water tempers it; when Water surges, Earth restrains it—this is the mutual overcoming of the Five Phases. Metal generates Water, and Water is Metal’s child, yet within Water, Metal is born. Wood generates Fire, and Fire is Wood’s child, yet within Water, Wood is born—this is their alternating roles as father and mother. The father is like Heaven; the mother is like Earth. Heaven’s qi descends to Earth, and Earth receives it, accomplishing the work of generation and nurturance. Thus it is said: *"The mother contains the nourishing fluids; the father governs the bestowal of essence."* The *Chilong Dadan Jue* states: *"The marvel lies in Heaven’s intercourse with Earth, enabling return and restoration."* The *Cuixu Pian* says: *"Father’s essence and mother’s blood coalesce to form the embryo, resembling another’s form yet akin to my own. Only by recognizing the true father and mother within can one grasp the essence of the Five Phases."*

Cultivators who refine the Elixir must unite their own Heaven with their own Earth, gathering their own Five Phases within. There, they mutually consume, defend, and yet remain intimate, suddenly realizing that within the void, conception occurs, and the Sacred Embryo is formed. When essence congeals and form flows, even metal and stone do not decay. The essence of the Five Phases solidifies between Heaven and Earth, becoming metal or stone, enduring for millennia without corruption. If one turns inward to seek it, condensing the essence of one’s own Five Phases into a treasure, it will endure as long as Heaven and Earth—how could mere metal or stone compare?

**Scrutinize focus, avoid leakage, and attain the true Dao.**
"Focus" means severing worldly affairs and dedicating oneself wholly to refinement, with single-minded devotion. "Avoid leakage" means maintaining silence like a hen brooding her eggs, keeping the spirit within the body’s cavity at all times. By doing so, the work remains pure, the medicine does not dissipate, the fire’s power is uninterrupted, and the Dao is achieved. Those deluded by inferior arts believe that pressing the perineum to restrain lustful impulses and engaging in sensory union constitutes "avoiding leakage." They do not realize that though the essence may not leak, the spirit and qi have already departed, leaving only stagnant, turbid residue to accumulate in the waist, breeding strange ailments. How utterly benighted! As the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states: *"Those who do not sever lust and practice dhyāna are like one who steams sand, hoping to make rice. Even after a hundred thousand kalpas, it remains only hot sand."* How much more so for the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir, which unites both nature and life—what then should be done? The *Cuixu Pian* warns: *"If you wish to prolong life and rescue old age, sever lust and abandon heterodox paths. Only by refining the Spirit-Elixir can you preserve form and dwell forever in the world."* Let scholars ponder this carefully.

**Erect a pole and see its shadow; call into the valley and hear its echo—how can this not be wondrous? These are the supreme images of Heaven and Earth.**
Erect a pole, and its shadow appears; call into the valley, and its echo responds—there has never been a stimulus without a response. Thus, the sun and moon hang in the sky: when one seeks fire, fire is born; when one seeks water, water arrives. The resonance is swift—how can this not be supremely wondrous? Yet though all under Heaven see it, none truly understand. The *Cuixu Pian* says: *"The sun’s crow and moon’s hare, two perfect wheels—rooted in the Prior Heaven. To gather them is difficult; gaze at the moonlit night and seize the moment."* If scholars can steal Heaven and Earth’s mechanism on the night when the sun and moon face each other, uniting their own sun and moon at the center, then the inner truth will resonate with the outer, and the Elixir will naturally harmonize. Eventually, the sun and moon will be refined.

If one ingests a mere inch of *Gelsemium* or a single *Croton* seed, one will collapse instantly, unable to bend or rise. At such a time, even if King Wen cast yarrow stalks, Confucius interpreted omens, Bian Que wielded needles, or Wu Xian beat drums, none could revive the fallen or restore their movement. *Gelsemium* and *Croton* are the most toxic of herbs. Ingesting an inch of *Gelsemium* or a single *Croton* seed causes immediate collapse, and even if King Wen, Confucius, Bian Que, or Wu Xian were present to cast stalks, interpret omens, wield needles, or beat drums, they could not restore life. Yet today, people believe that poisonous herbs can swiftly kill but do not believe that the Spirit-Elixir can grant longevity. How can their lack of reflection be so extreme?

The River Maiden is numinous and most divine; at the sight of fire, she takes flight, leaving no trace of dust or shadow. The hidden dragon conceals itself, its whereabouts unknown. To subdue her, the Yellow Sprout must serve as the root. True mercury is born in Li. Li is the feminine, residing in Wu; by the division of celestial stems, Wu corresponds to the Three Rivers, hence she is called the River Maiden. Tracing her origin, she arises from emptying the heart and congealing the spirit—truly emerging from within the heart. Thus, she is called numinous mercury or divine mercury. Her nature is fierce and violent; at the sight of fire, she flees without a trace, like a hidden dragon vanishing without a clue. Without the Yellow Sprout as her root, how could she be restrained? The Yellow Sprout is true lead. When mercury obtains true lead, they capture, restrain, and unite, preventing her flight—this is why it serves as the root of the Golden Elixir.

**Things devoid of yin and yang violate Heaven and defy their origin.**
A hen lays eggs and hatches chicks, yet not all are complete—why? Because the union was not consummated, the Three and Five did not merge, and hardness and softness remained apart. The Dao is called the union of one yin and one yang; an excess of yin or yang is called illness. For example, a hen that lays eggs without a rooster will surely produce none—why? Because solitary yang does not generate, and solitary yin does not complete. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: *"Do not mistake solitary yin for yang; refining a single substance only weakens the body. Stretching and guiding are not the Dao; refining qi and consuming mist are madness. The world vainly seeks to subdue mercury and lead—when will they see the tiger and dragon descend? I urge you to thoroughly seek the place of your birth, return to the root, and restore the source—this is the King of Medicines."* If one can turn the light inward and reflect, uniting one’s own yin and yang within, then hardness and softness will pair, the Three and Five will merge as one—why seek elsewhere? The *Huanyuan Pian* says: *"Harmonize yin and yang within yourself."* The *Yuzhi Shu* states: *"Yin and yang are not to be sought in other things."* The *Huandan Pian* declares: *"Yin and yang must be gathered from one’s own truth."* How could one seek them outside the body?

The *Cuixu Pian* states: *"Better to seek a master’s peaceful method than to accumulate and consume essence and blood."* As the ancients say: *"All under heaven returns—one must study the art of non-action and purity."* For the Golden Elixir is none other than the Dao of purity and non-action.

Some, deluded by perverse teachings, believe the Golden Elixir must involve women—how gravely mistaken they are! The way of transformation in heaven and earth is natural: fire moves upward in flames, water flows downward to moisten—no master guides them to do so. The origin of all things, the governance of the cosmos, cannot be altered. The transformations of heaven and earth, the flaming of fire and moistening of water—are these the work of human effort? They arise spontaneously, of themselves.

Within our own bodies, heaven and earth exist; water and fire reside there as well. Their transformations, their flaming and moistening—are these the work of human effort? They, too, arise spontaneously, of themselves. The *Zhixuan Pian* says: *"One must know the meeting of east and west; the path lies in the central harmony of the upper and lower fields."* The human body models heaven and earth: the head is heaven, the abdomen is earth. Simply guard the spirit within, neither forgetting nor forcing; regulate the breath through the nose, neither indulging nor restraining. Naturally, there will be opening and closing, receiving and bestowing—no different from the transformative way of heaven and earth.

When the time arrives and qi stirs, the mechanism moves, the phoenix cries—then fire arises from below the navel, water is born within the crown. Its wonder unfolds without anticipation, of itself. It is not attained through laboring the spirit or exerting force. This Dao is heaven’s creation, earth’s design—fixed and unalterable.

Lord Wei feared that learners might not grasp the yin and yang within the body, the upper and lower realms—thus he drew analogies to heaven and earth’s transformative processes, to the blazing and moistening of water and fire. His explanation was indeed thorough and lucid.

Observe the moment of union between male and female: hard and soft intertwine, inseparable, attaining their rhythmic harmony—not through artifice or control. The *Quanzhen Collection* states: *"Qi harmonized, spirit settled, the breath conjoins."* The *Cuixu Pian* says: *"Like husband and wife in union, a single guard of essence and blood forms the infant."* This so-called union in the golden elixir is an inner resonance of yin and yang, a merging of spirit and qi—termed "male and female" or "husband and wife" as metaphors.

The *Huanyuan Pian* states: *"When spirit and qi return to the root, body and mind revert to life’s mandate."* Such true apertures are known by few. When spirit enters qi and qi unites with spirit, attaining their rhythmic harmony, there arises the state of hard and soft intertwined, inseparable—yet no artifice or control is employed. One merely witnesses its thusness, yet knows not how it comes to be thus. Wondrous! Wondrous indeed!

When a male is born, he lies prone; when a female is born, she lies supine—this posture is received at the very inception of qi in the womb, not merely observed at birth. Even in death, they revert to the same. This is not taught by parents; it is predetermined at the moment of union.

Chu Huagu’s *Qu Yi Shuo* states: *"Opening qi makes a male; closing qi makes a female. One opening, one closing—thus male and female are distinguished."* Li Yuxi’s commentary on the *Xinyin Jing* says: *"Male and female receive the same endowment; only the sequence of their union determines yin and yang."* Yang qi gathers at the front, so a man’s face is heavier—at birth, he must lie prone. Yin qi gathers at the back, so a woman’s back is heavier—at birth, she must lie supine. This is not only true at birth but also at death. Thus, a man who drowns lies prone; a woman who drowns lies supine. Even beasts that drown do the same. This is not taught by parents; it is fixed from the moment of receiving qi—one opening, one closing, one preceding, one following.

Now, why does Lord Wei expound the Dao of clarity and stillness and yet speak of the prone and supine postures of men and women? There is no other reason: he wishes learners to thoroughly understand the yin and yang, the upper and lower within their own bodies, and to comprehend the natural principles of creation.

The *Kan* male is the moon; the *Li* female is the sun. The sun bestows virtue—this is the husband’s Dao. The moon unfurls its light—this is the wife’s Dao. Yet if the moon is *Kan* male, then the wife becomes the husband; if the sun is *Li* female, then the husband becomes the wife. This is the inversion.

Yet how does the sun transform and the moon change without harm to their bodies? It is through the mutual support of yin and yang. The eclipses of the sun and moon occur at their conjunctions. At the full moon, the moon is eclipsed; when the moon obscures the sun, the sun is eclipsed. Thus, eclipses occur at new and full moons—yet not every conjunction brings an eclipse. When they intersect, there is an eclipse; when they do not, there is none.

Now, Lord Wei cites the sun and moon as metaphors for the elixir path, earnestly emphasizing the waxing and waning, the encroachments and obscurations. His intent is profound. Should learners not turn their light inward, seek the sun and moon within their own bodies, and inquire into the waxing and waning within themselves?

The *Shangqing Collection* states: *"Watching the Dipper’s handle revolve through the heavens, one suddenly awakens to the wondrous secret of immortality. In an instant, true yang is born in the *Kan* position, guarding the deficiency in the *Li* palace. Since ancient times, *Qian* and *Kun* have never ceased their interplay of *Li* and *Kan*, day after day. On this year’s winter solstice, plum blossoms still congeal like snow. The ancient sages closed their gates on this day, allowing no comings or goings—all for the sake of living beings. Every creature embodies the intent of generation and nurturance. At the cusp of *zi* and *hai*, creation moves silently; fire arises in water—its wonder lies in the *Xu-Wei* cavity. Now that you’ve seen through this, the golden crow flies into the toad’s cave."* Such direct revelation of heaven’s mechanism, such clear exposition of the Dao’s essentials, amply confirms Lord Wei’s words—luminous and unerring!

Male and female depend on each other, exhaling and inhaling to nourish; yin and yang intermingle, seeking one another.

Within the body, male and female, yin and yang are but two aspects of the self. When these two qi intermingle and harmonize, they naturally exhale and inhale, intertwining to form the embryo. The *Shangqing Ji* states: "Within one’s own body lies a husband and wife." To speak thus to the uninitiated would surely invite ridicule—such is the Way. The seeker must pursue it through clarity, stillness, and non-action, not through perverse interpretations.

Metal transforms into water, and the nature of water flows boundlessly. Fire transforms into earth, and water cannot course freely. Metal is born in the *Kan* palace, its qi alone. For metal transforms into water, and when the true fire of the great yang lies concealed and steams beneath, it melts and becomes water. The nature of water flows abundantly—who can resist it? Yet when the fire’s power reaches its zenith, it transforms again into earth. Fire becomes earth, and earth restrains water; when water encounters earth, it halts—thus it cannot flow.

Therefore, the male moves and emits outward, while the female remains still and conceals within. If the overflow exceeds measure, the female constrains it, the *po* restraining the *hun*, preventing excess. The *Ruyao Jing* states: "Water can flow, fire can flame"—within the body, this can be verified. Thus, in harmonizing lead and mercury to form the elixir, one must not exceed measure. Now, *Kan* as male moves and emits outward, while *Li* as female remains still and conceals within. Should the emission exceed measure, *Li* as female will constrain and restrain it, so that *hun* and *po* mutually check each other and do not fall into excess. Neither cold nor heat, advancing and retreating in timely fashion, each attains harmony, and both exhale in mutual confirmation.

When gathering the medicine, regulate the fire’s work—neither too swift nor too slow, but seeking only balance and union. The *Fuming Pian* states: "When firm and yielding meet, qi evens out—its wonder lies in avoiding harm." The *Dui* and *Zhen* trigrams hold harmony as their essence.

Cinnabar is the essence of wood, and only when united with metal do they merge. Yuan Yangzi’s *Song of the Great Dao* says: "To know cinnabar as the essence of wood, move it to the west to join with metal." For mercury’s nature is to fly and flee, most difficult to subdue; only when cast into the lead crucible and united with lead does it cease to fly or flee.

Where metal and water unite, wood and fire become companions. These four, in primordial chaos, array as dragon and tiger. The dragon is yang, its number odd; the tiger is yin, its number even. Heaven-one generates water, its position in the north, its symbol the Dark Warrior. Earth-two generates fire, its position in the south, its symbol the Vermilion Bird. Heaven-three generates wood, its position in the east, its symbol the Azure Dragon. Earth-four generates metal, its position in the west, its symbol the White Tiger. These four each dwell in their quadrant, each forming a symbol, originally unrelated.

Yet now it is said: "Where metal and water unite, wood and fire become companions." The yang dragon originally emerges from the *Li* palace, while the yin tiger returns from the *Kan* position. Since each forms a symbol, they should be collectively called the Four Symbols. Yet here, only dragon and tiger are mentioned, omitting the Vermilion Bird and Dark Warrior—why? North and south form the warp, east and west the weft.

The phrase "where metal and water unite" refers to the metal of the west-four White Tiger descending into water. "Wood and fire become companions" refers to the wood of the east-three Azure Dragon ascending into fire. Thus, metal is not in the west but unites with water in the north, and wood is not in the east but becomes fire’s companion in the south. The White Tiger transforms into the Black Tiger, the Azure Dragon into the Red Dragon.

When metal, water, wood, and fire—these four—converge as one, they are a primal chaos like the undivided Great Ultimate. When arrayed as two, *Zhen*’s dragon-mercury emerges from *Li*’s homeland, and *Dui*’s tiger-lead is born in *Kan*’s domain. Zhang Ziyang deeply grasped this secret and thus encapsulated it in a single phrase: "The Four Symbols do not depart from the Two Bodies." Later scholars, unaware of what the Four Symbols truly are, mistakenly identify them as the eyes, ears, nose, and tongue, or as the liver and lungs as dragon and tiger. With such misguided recognition, their understanding diverges further—how could they grasp the wonder that the Four Symbols do not depart from the Two Bodies?

The liver, green, is the father; the lungs, white, the mother. The heart, red, is the daughter; the spleen, yellow, the ancestor. The kidneys, black, are the child—the child is the beginning of the Five Phases. The qi of the day arises at *zi*, born in the kidneys, then transmits to the liver, heart, spleen, and lungs. Yet though the liver, heart, spleen, lungs, and kidneys are five, they are called the Three Substances—why? When metal and water unite, wood and fire become companions, and together with the central *wu-ji* earth, they form three. For the Four Symbols and Five Phases rely entirely on earth; without *wu-ji*, the elixir cannot form. This is why the Three Substances, one family, all return to *wu-ji*.

The *Cuixu Pian* states: "Liver, heart, spleen, lungs, kidneys, intestines, gallbladder—all are empty houses, old fences. Saliva, mucus, essence, fluids, qi, blood—these can only assist as stepping stones." It speaks of the golden elixir as the wondrous Dao of something born from nothing, not a thing of form or substance. Yet now Lord Wei speaks of the Five Viscera—why? It is not that the Five Viscera are used, but that they are borrowed to illustrate the Five Phases. The liver, green, and lungs, white, represent metal and wood; the heart, red, and kidneys, black, represent water and fire. Metal generates water, wood generates fire, so the liver and lungs are likened to father and mother, the heart and kidneys to child and daughter. Yet metal and wood both arise from earth, so the spleen is likened to the ancestor.

If the kidneys are the child, why are they called the beginning of the Five Phases? The kidneys belong to water, and water’s number is one. The qi of the day arises at *zi*, born in the kidneys, then transmits to the liver, heart, spleen, and lungs.

Firm and yielding alternate in ascent, traversing the divisions in turn. Dragon to the west, tiger to the east, establishing the weft at *mao* and *you*. Punishment and virtue converge, meeting in joy. Punishment governs killing and subjugation; virtue governs generation and ascent. In the second month, the elm dies as the *Kui* star descends upon *mao*. In the eighth month, wheat sprouts as the *Tian Gang* star occupies *you*. *Zi* to the south, *wu* to the north—these mutually form the warp and weft.

"Firm and yielding alternate in ascent" refers to the two qi mutually aiding and revolving. "Traversing the divisions in turn" means circulating through the celestial stems. "Dragon to the west, tiger to the east, establishing the weft at *mao* and *you*, punishment and virtue converging, meeting in joy" describes the weft in motion, *mao* and *you* joining, metal and wood uninterrupted. "Punishment governs killing and subjugation" is like the autumn qi, stern and killing, gathering all things inward. "Virtue governs generation and ascent" is like the spring qi, flourishing and generating, bringing all things forth.

"In the second month, the elm dies as the *Kui* star descends upon *mao*"—in the second month, *mao* is established, and the monthly general is *He Kui*. *He Kui* belongs to *xu*, and within *xu* lies *xin*; the elm dies in this month because the killing qi of *xin* metal descends upon *mao*. "In the eighth month, wheat sprouts as the *Tian Gang* star occupies *you*"—in the eighth month, *you* is established, and the monthly general is *Tian Gang*. *Tian Gang* belongs to *chen*, and within *chen* lies *yi*; wheat sprouts in this month because the generating qi of *yi* wood occupies *you*.

The "north-south axis" and "east-west weft" mutually serve as their guiding principles: when the sun traverses the Western Continent, it is called spring, and in the second month at *mao*, the Western Continent lies in the northeast while the Southern Continent lies in the south; when the sun traverses the Eastern Continent, it is called autumn, and in the eighth month at *you*, the Eastern Continent lies in the southwest while the Northern Continent lies in the north.

Now, the north and south form the warp of Heaven and Earth, while the east and west form their weft. When the *ding*-cauldron’s *kui* and *gang* rotate once, the dragon flees west and the tiger flees east; *zi* lies in the south and *wu* in the north—this transformation in the second and eighth months at *mao* and *you* is no different. Thus, the *Wuzhen Pian* speaks of "the months of the hare and rooster," for when their time arrives, punishment and virtue descend upon the gate—this is the image of the elixir.

"Image" means "to resemble this." Master Wei set forth this image to instruct others, and its principle is entirely self-evident. Yet why do those who cling to the text and adhere rigidly to the image often confine their search to the page, never turning inward to seek within themselves? Thus, they lead one another astray without end, unaware that what Master Wei called "the dragon in the west, the tiger in the east, *zi* in the south, and *wu* in the north" is nothing other than the Heaven and Earth within our own bodies, reversed.

If one can awaken to the marvel of Heaven and Earth reversed within the self, then the reason the east and west establish the weft, and the north and south mutually serve as the guiding warp, can all be silently comprehended in their workings. What need is there to seek outwardly in vain?

The number one through nine completes and begins anew. This "one through nine" is the same as the arrangement of "nine above, one below." Its completion and renewal mean moving from one to nine, then from nine back to one, circulating endlessly between above and below. "Containing the Origin in *Xu* and *Wei*, sowing essence at *Zi*": *Xu* and *Wei* refer to the sun’s arrival at these lunar mansions, where night after night the primordial *qi* takes form. "Sowing essence at *Zi*" means that at the *Zi* hour, *qi* reaches the *Weilu* Pass, and true essence is born here.

The marvel of the Golden Elixir lies in containing the Origin in the precelestial and sowing essence in the postcelestial. What is the precelestial? It is the state of profound stillness, the time before the Supreme Ultimate divides—subtle and indistinct. What is the postcelestial? It is the state of responsive movement, the time after the Supreme Ultimate divides—vague and elusive. The precelestial is but a single *qi*; only in the postcelestial does it transform into true essence.

The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Half a catty of true mercury, half a catty of lead, produced before the Supreme Ultimate in emptiness and nonbeing. One must hasten to the *Zi* hour to gather them, refine them into golden liquid, and return them to the Elixir Field." This is indeed correct. For *Xu* and *Wei* lie between *Hai* and *Zi*, the moment when yin reaches its extreme and yang begins to stir. The *Zi* hour is where the first yang moves. Beyond this point, the path of mother and child divides in the womb. The mystery lies in the *Wei*, *Ji*, *Niu*, *Dou*, and *Nü* lunar mansions. Without profound understanding of the yin and yang within one’s body and deep penetration of Heaven and Earth’s creative transformations, who could grasp this?

*"Guan guan go the ospreys, on the islet in the river. The modest, retiring, virtuous young lady: for our prince a good mate."* The male does not dwell alone, nor the female in solitude. The dark warrior and the coiled serpent support each other, illustrating how the female and male must mutually depend. If two women share a chamber, their beauty may be exquisite, and even if Su Qin were to mediate or Zhang Yi to propose, deploying eloquent words and persuasive arguments to urge harmony, they could never become husband and wife. Their hair would rot and their teeth decay before they ever knew union. Just as medicinal substances must be of the same kind and their proportions precise, if the ingredients are not of the proper species or their measurements disordered, even if the Yellow Emperor were to tend the furnace, Tai Yi to hold the fire, the Eight Lords to pound and refine, or Huainan to blend and harmonize—even if they built a towering altar with jade steps, offered unicorn flesh and phoenix jerky, held registers and knelt in prayer, or entreated the spirits with solemn vows after fasting and ablutions—any hopes would be in vain. It would be like using glue to mend a broken cauldron or sal ammoniac to treat a festering sore, like adding ice to dispel cold or boiling water to remove scalds, like making a flying *kui* dance or a writhing snake perform—only to see things grow more perverse.

The Great Way of the Golden Elixir is simply the interplay of one yin and one yang. This principle applies to human affairs and the patterns of all things. Now, Master Wei draws on the *Guan Ju* ode’s imagery of the virtuous maiden matched with the prince to illustrate the union of male and female within the body, and on the dark warrior and coiled serpent to illustrate the mutual dependence of female and male within. This may be called a broad analogy with meticulous comparisons, leaving nothing unaddressed.

Moreover, when the *Dui* maiden and *Gen* youth respond to each other above and below, it is called *Xian*. When the *Li* and *Dui* maidens dwell together but their aspirations differ, it is called *Kui*. This clarifies that yin and yang must complement each other and cannot be one-sided.

As for the work of the furnace and fire, yin and yang must also be balanced. If the medicinal substances are not of the proper kind or their proportions disordered, even if the Yellow Emperor were to tend the furnace, Tai Yi to hold the fire, the Eight Lords to pound and refine, or Huainan to blend and harmonize—even if they built an altar of gold and jade, offered unicorn and phoenix, fasted and bathed, held registers and knelt in prayer, or presented blue petitions and red memorials to the spirits above and below—all would be a waste of time and effort, laboring in vain. It would be like using glue to mend a broken cauldron, sal ammoniac to treat a festering sore, ice to dispel cold, or boiling water to remove scalds, like making a flying *kui* dance or a writhing snake perform—only to see things grow more perverse.

Some may misinterpret the saying "the male does not dwell alone, nor the female in solitude" to mean that one must use a woman in elixir refinement, thus falling into the error of "solitary yang." They then cite this passage to justify their perverse theories, while mocking the true Way as impractical and pedantic. Such people clearly overestimate their own understanding.

*Explication of the Zhouyi Cantong Qi*, Scroll Middle
*Section: Masters, Daoist Works, Explication of the Zhouyi Cantong Qi*

From the *Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*, *Explication of the Zhouyi Cantong Qi* by Yu Yan of the Song Dynasty, Scroll Lower:

The ancient sages and worthies embraced the Mysterious and held fast to the True. "Seeing nothing, hearing nothing, embracing the spirit in stillness"—this was the embrace of the Mysterious and True by Guangchengzi. "Concentrating qi to attain suppleness, able to be like an infant"—this was the embrace of the Mysterious and True by Laozi. Then there were Nüwa, who smelted the five-colored stones to mend the heavens; Ranshi, who attained the center of the ring and followed its completion; and the cinnabar methods of Lie Yukou, the clerk of Qiyuan. Let no scholar say that only those with innate immortal bones may seek the Way. With resolve, none is beyond attainment. What were the sages but men? What am I but a man? Those who act may do likewise.

The aspirant of the Dao, if truly able to rouse a courageous heart, exert diligent effort, and practice unceasingly day and night, will find that when the time comes, qi transforms of itself, and as water flows, channels form of themselves. What then is there to fear in the failure of stubborn stones to soften or iron pestles to become needles?

Yet one may study without encounter, encounter without attainment, or even attain the true formula yet fail to preserve it. To work at dawn and desire results by dusk, to sit in practice and expect effects by standing—this is to seek the profit of a ladle before the strength of a bushel is firm, or to incur the cost of a thousand stones while the foundation remains shallow, only to suffer the alternating assaults of frost and ice. In such cases, even if Guangchengzi or Laozi were one’s teacher, and Liezi or Zhuangzi one’s companions, what could they do?

Alas! The *Gold and Jade Collection* of Ma Danyang states: "Refining qi is one’s livelihood, nurturing spirit one’s daily practice. Keep dragon and tiger harmonized, lest horse and monkey play." It also says: "Observe the great Dao of heaven’s movements, silently attain the union of Qian and Kun. The oral transmission of the original master holds the greatest merit in non-action." For the ancients who refined the elixir, not a single thought arose, and all dharmas were forgotten. In utter clarity and stillness, they followed only the Dao. Within quiescent concentration, they embraced the qi of harmonious emptiness. Their exhalations were faint, their inhalations continuous, rising to the Mud Pill and descending to the Gate of Life, circulating without cease. Spirit and qi never parted for even a moment. When the inner elixir neared completion, primordial qi naturally abided in the cinnabar field, governing as one with heaven and earth.

Formerly, the Yellow Emperor retired to the Hall of Great Courtyard for three months, practicing inner contemplation—this was the very Dao. This Dao is most simple and most easy. Within the twelve hours of a day, if the heart constantly guides qi, qi unites with spirit, and form endures forever, revolving with the sun and moon, turning with the River of Heaven. Endless turning, boundless longevity.

The *Mysterious Chapter* states: "If only each breath attends to the other, the jade liquid will flow, transforming the entire bodily frame." Such is its meaning. If one exhales without the spirit’s governance, a single breath is incomplete; if one inhales without the spirit’s governance, a single breath is likewise incomplete. The key lies in heart and breath constantly depending on each other, spirit and qi constantly guarding each other, thoughts continuously linked, forged into one. Only then are form and spirit both wondrous, uniting with the Dao in truth.

The *Song of the Great Dao of the Numinous Source* says: "Spirit is nature, qi is life. When spirit does not stray outward, qi naturally settles." The *Anthology of Immortal Joys* states: "When qi and spirit meet, nature abides and life is stabilized." Truly, they must not be separated even for an instant.

Ordinary people are not so. Though qi breathes within, the spirit is ever ensnared by external affairs. From youth to maturity, from maturity to old age, not a single breath rests within the body. Thus, spirit and qi each follow their own course without mutual guardianship. Eventually, the dwelling becomes empty, walls crumble, and the master cannot sustain himself. Is this the doing of heaven and earth’s destruction, or the harm of demonic spirits? No—it is the loss of the Dao and self-departure.

The *Zhong-Lü Transmission of the Dao* by Shi Qizhen states: "What is exhaled is one’s own primordial qi, issuing from within. What is inhaled is heaven and earth’s true qi, entering from without. If one’s root is firm and primordial qi undiminished, then even in breathing, one may seize heaven and earth’s true qi. But if the root is unsteady, essence exhausted, and qi weakened—above, primordial qi leaks away; below, the original palace is unfortified. The true qi of heaven and earth, inhaled, follows the exhalation outward, and the body’s primordial qi ceases to belong to oneself, instead being seized by heaven and earth." Thus, immortals often say: "Refinement must be timely. Once the crucible is ruined, there is no remedy."

Su Ziyou, in his later years, inquired of the immortal nun Zheng about the art of nourishing life. She replied: "Your vessel is ruined. The Dao is difficult to attain." For the medicinal ingredients, early cultivation is precious. If one practices only in old age, essence will be depleted, qi exhausted, lead withered, and mercury scarce. Even with effort, one may only prolong life in peace—nothing more.

The *Emerald Writ* states: "It is all clear in a few words: the paths of youth and age differ in ease and difficulty." This is its meaning.

Some may ask: Lü Chunyang began to hear the Dao at fifty; Ma Ziran and Liu Langran both attained the Dao after sixty-four. Why is this? The answer: "A ruined house is easy to repair; withered medicine is not hard to revive. Only know the method of return. Gold and treasure may pile like mountains—yet what of one’s daily cultivation?"

Refining the Nine Cauldrons, one’s traces vanish into seclusion. The Nine Cauldrons refer to the nine revolutions of the fire phase. When the fire phase’s number is complete, transformations occur, and traces disappear. One roams freely between heaven and earth, riding the void. As Lü Chunyang’s *Song of the Kiln’s Ruin* says: "Nine years of fire phase have passed. Suddenly, the Heavenly Gate atop the head breaks open. The true person appears, and great spiritual powers manifest. From this, heavenly immortals come to offer congratulations." Such is its meaning.

Containing essence and nurturing spirit, one communicates with the virtue of the Three Origins. The Three Origins are the three fields: upper, middle, and lower. With long containment and deep effort, spirit and essence are stored within, harmonious qi fills the entire body, and the hundred bones and myriad orifices are all pervaded. Naturally, it is like the turning of the River of Heaven. The *Precious Chapter of Primordial Unity* states: "With years of practice, no effort is needed. The fire wheel and water axle turn of themselves." Such is it.

What is meant by "containing essence and nurturing spirit"? Externally, one does not toil the bodily frame; internally, one does not labor the heart and will. For if spirit is overused, it tires; if essence is overused, it depletes. Day after day, it is whittled away until one rots with the grasses and trees—is this not deeply lamentable?

Thus, those who refine the elixir empty the heart, forget the form, guard the One, and embrace the numinous. Only then can they secure essence, preserve qi, and perfect spirit. When the three fields are full of essence and the five viscera brim with qi, only then is the elixir complete.
