The *Transformation Book* of Tan Jingsheng states: "Sorrow brings tears like rain; bitterness brings mucus like rain. Rage forms goiters; anger forms abscesses. Where the heart desires, qi follows—nothing is not nurtured. If one errs here, one must err there." Therefore, the great person restrains sorrow and bitterness, cautions against rage and anger, attains the gate of vast qi, and gathers the root. Knowing the bag of original spirit, one veils its light. Like the clam’s inner guarding, like the stone’s inner concealment—this is the chamber of pearls and jade. Truly, it is the ultimate discourse.

When essence overflows the interstices of skin and sinews, bones become firm. The interstices lie between skin and flesh. Through cultivation, when essence overflows the interstices, blood transforms entirely into marrow-fat. The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Now the whole body is white blood, and one feels no cold through the four seasons." Truly, no practice surpasses filling the brain, for the brain is the sea of marrow. When the brain’s marrow is full, pure yang flows and overflows, filling all marrow. Only then are bones solid and sinews firm, forever free from the worries of cold and heat.

All perverse influences are dispelled, and upright qi abides. That by which humans cultivate and attain longevity is their ability to steal the upright qi of Heaven and Earth. That by which humans steal the upright qi of Heaven and Earth is their breath. Exhalation reaches the root; inhalation reaches the stem—thus they steal the upright qi of Heaven and Earth and return it to the cinnabar field. Human breath is like the breath of Heaven and Earth. The *Huangji Jingshi Shu* states: "After the winter solstice is exhalation; after the summer solstice is inhalation." This is the yearly breath of Heaven and Earth. Zhang Guanwu’s commentary says: "After the winter solstice, yang grows and yin wanes, unfolding the myriad things—hence exhalation. After the summer solstice, yin grows and yang wanes, gathering the myriad things—hence inhalation." If speaking of a single day, then after midnight is exhalation, and after noon is inhalation. One year of Heaven is but a single breath of humankind. Thus, the number of one cosmic cycle—129,600 years—is but a single year in the great transformation. Now, in speaking of the Dao of the elixir: in one day, there are 13,500 exhalations and 13,500 inhalations. One exhalation and one inhalation make a single breath. In the space of one breath, one subtly seizes the workings of 13,500 years of celestial motion. In one year of 360 days, there are 4,860,000 breaths, subtly seizing 4,860,000 years of celestial motion. Thereby, the foul and turbid body is entirely exchanged, transformed into a body of pure yang. First, the qi is changed; next, the blood; next, the vessels; next, the flesh; next, the marrow; next, the sinews; next, the bones; next, the hair; next, the form. After nine years and nine transformations, yin is exhausted and yang is pure, and one’s years equal those of Heaven and Earth—this is the Dao of longevity and transcendence.

Now, when Master Wei says, "All perverse influences are dispelled, and upright qi abides," he means that by circulating the fire day and night, one refines away all yin qi, transforming it into pure yang, so that upright qi abides and one attains longevity. The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "The body permeated with golden light, the marrow fragrant; golden sinews and jade bones—all pure yang. Refine until the red blood flows white, yin qi dissolved, and the body is naturally sound." For pure yang is the immortal, pure yin the demon, and half yin and half yang the human. If one can refine yang to dissolve yin, one may become an immortal of pure yang. If even a trace of yin qi remains unrefined, one cannot be called pure yang.

Through long and gradual accumulation, the form is transformed, and one becomes an immortal. Great indeed!

Alas! The method of the Dao of the Elixir is modeled on Heaven. How arduous! The ceaseless practice of elixir-work. Why say so? The *Yin Fu Jing* states: "Observe the Way of Heaven, grasp the movement of Heaven—this is all." That by which Heaven is long and enduring is its ceaseless movement day and night. If one can observe the Way of Heaven and turn inward to seek it within oneself, emulating Heaven’s ceaseless movement day and night, then the Way of long life and enduring vision truly lies herein. Apart from this, there is no other Way.

Now, the ceaseless movement day and night is the Way of Heaven. That a person, with their humble six-foot frame, can emulate the Way of Heaven and harmonize their movement with that of Heaven—how great is this Way! The *Yi Jing* says: "Heaven’s movement is strong; the noble person ceaselessly strengthens themselves." The Master’s intent in composing the hexagrams was to make those who know the Way emulate the strength of Heaven’s movement. That by which Heaven constantly moves without ceasing is its strength. If one is strong, one can move without end. If the noble person wishes their movement to be unceasing, they should emulate this strength and fortify their resolve—only then can it be so.

Yet ceaselessness is what people find difficult. Heaven’s movement continues day and night without rest; if a person emulates Heaven’s ceaseless movement, how difficult is this! Nevertheless, great affairs in the world must begin with the small, and difficult tasks must begin with the easy. A tree of great girth grows from a tiny sprout; a nine-story terrace rises from heaped earth; a journey of a thousand miles begins with a single step. Thus, the sage plans for difficulty while it is still easy and achieves greatness while it is still small—all lies in the accumulation of long and gradual effort.

The *Huang Ting Jing* states: "Immortal and Daoist are not divine; through accumulating essence and amassing qi, they attain perfection." Thus, from the moment of forming the embryo in a brief instant, after a hundred days the work becomes numinous; after ten months the embryo is complete; after one year there is small attainment; after three years, great attainment. Only after nine years, when the work is complete and all human affairs are exhausted, can one leave the world behind, stand alone, grow feathers, and ascend as an immortal. There has never been one who attained this without long and gradual accumulation. If one claims that without such accumulation one can suddenly ascend as an immortal, it is like digging a well nine *ren* deep with a single shovel or climbing a city wall a hundred million *zhang* high in a single step—how could this be possible?

The *Tong Xuan Zhen Jing* says: "Though the lame turtle rests not a step, it travels a thousand miles; though earth is heaped without cease, a mountain is formed. By the river, one who desires fish would do better to return and weave a net." Thus, in learning the Dao, one need not fear failure—only lack of diligence. One need not fear lack of diligence—only lack of enduring resolve. For enduring resolve is the most difficult.

Wu Zongxuan’s *Xuan Gang Lun* states: "Among a thousand who know the Dao, one resolves to follow it; among a thousand who resolve to follow it, one is single-minded; among a thousand who are single-minded, one is diligent and enduring. Thus, though many study, few succeed." If those who know can resolve, those who resolve can sever worldly ties, those who sever worldly ties can be single-minded, and those who are single-minded can be diligent and enduring, there is none who studies and does not attain. Yet as the world declines and customs deteriorate, and the distance from the sages grows ever greater, students often approach the path of stillness with restless hearts, seeking quick results and immediate responses, desiring near outcomes from distant efforts. None can persist to the end.

Having resolved, their resolve is not firm; believing in the Dao, their faith is not steadfast. They change course from morning to evening, begin with diligence but falter midway, delighting in the moment and wearying of perseverance. Yet they wish to match the years of Heaven and Earth—is this not foolish?

The *Nei Guan Jing* says: "Knowing the Dao is easy; believing in it is difficult. Believing in the Dao is easy; practicing it is difficult. Practicing the Dao is easy; attaining it is difficult. Attaining the Dao is easy; preserving it is difficult." If it were not difficult, the streets would be filled with immortals—what would be extraordinary about that?

Lü Chunyang’s poem says:
*"Three acres of cinnabar field, unsown—
To sow them, one must borrow the Red Dragon’s plow.
I once taught others to sow this seed,
But none understood its cultivation—how could the Dao grow?"*

Is the Dao distant from people? No—people distance themselves from the Dao.

People merely see the *Wu Zhen Pian*’s line: "The radiant red elixir forms in a day," and take the ancient immortals’ words as true and worthy of hearing. Yet when it speaks of nine years or three years, they dismiss it as mere procrastination, mocking the idea of long and gradual accumulation. They fail to understand that Master Ziyang’s poem borrows the words of Han Xiaoyao to enlighten those in the world who, unaware of the marvel of seeing effects in a single day, merely toil for three or nine years. It does not mean one can ascend as an immortal in a day without long and gradual effort.

Let us examine this further. The *Shang Qing Ji* says:
*"Teach me to practice for a brief moment—
Bones grow cold, the heart’s flower forms into a single red grain."*
It also says:
*"In the mid-autumn night of the Kai Xi era, I burned incense, knelt, and received the oral transmission.
In just two or three days of practice, Heaven and Earth, sun and moon became soft as silk."*

Yet it also says:
*"Even if one’s work reveals mercury and lead,
Without knowing the firing times, it is all in vain.
It all depends on the fire of the Zhou Tian—
Only after ten months is the sacred embryo complete.
Though the head of the elixir forms, it will dissipate in the end.
One must know the firing times to solidify it."*

Thus, while the minor effects of the golden elixir may be seen in a brief moment, its great work certainly does not end in a single day.

Moreover, let us consider the sayings of the ancients. Wang Yixuan says:
*"Nine transformations in one year—
Lifespan may equal Heaven and Earth."*
And:
*"After the fire has circulated for a thousand days,
The furnace opens to reveal the eight jades."*

Han Xiaoyao says:
*"After ten months, it is complete, resplendent between the Two Modes."*
And:
*"Three years emulate Heaven’s movement—
Forever free, the golden body is perfected."*

Wang and Han were both true immortals of the Tang who attained the Dao—would they speak falsely?

Lü Chunyang says:
*"A thousand days of work, never idle—
The river chariot carries its load up Kun Mountain."*
And:
*"Only when the celestial tally descends to the Jade Capital
Do three thousand days of accumulated work begin."*

Liu Haichan says:
*"The furnace seals the eight passes, completing the nine and five—
The medicine connects the seven returns—this takes three years."*
And:
*"After nine transformations, the work is complete in a thousand days—
Already, names are listed among the immortals."*

Dongbin and Haichan are among the most eminent of the immortals—would they speak falsely?

Not only this. Chen Chaoyuan says:
*"Nurturing the embryo requires ten months;
Refining the elixir takes three years."*
And:
*"After nine years of return without error,
Refine pure yang to become the perfect truth."*

Wang Liangqi says:
*"The great general follows Heaven’s law, refining for a thousand days—
Then the false is removed, and only the true remains."*
And:
*"The magic mushroom fills the three cinnabar fields—
A thousand days of work extends ten thousand years."*

Xue Zixian says:
*"The four images are contained, returning to Wu and Si—
Diligently for ten months, the infant is born."*
And:
*"Ascension must wait until three years—
Tenderly nurtured, with much affection."*

Liu Xugu says:
*"The countenance may be fixed by three dwellings;
The embryo’s breath succeeds in one cycle."*
And:
*"The great work nears completion in three thousand days—
The wondrous use is flawless through the twelve hours."*

Chen Momo says:
*"The firing times gather and form in a hundred days;
The work is refined—this takes three years."*

Li Changyuan says:
*"Do not say the elixir is complete in a day—
In the end, one must devote several years of work."*

Chen Cuixu says:
*"A moment’s work refines and reveals maturity—
Yet one must pass three years."*
And:
*"A thousand mornings of firing times, knowing the seasons—
The sprout is certain, and mercury dries of itself."*

Would all these masters have spoken falsely to deceive later generations?

Qiu Changchun’s *Pan Xi Ji* says:
*"Though blessings are light and demonic obstacles heavy,
Press on—when the work is complete, it will surely be whole.
Let our fellowship strive!"*

Alas for later generations—those who love the Dao, following in the wake of others’ elegance, tracing the brushstrokes of ancient texts, compiling them into illustrated volumes to instruct posterity. They expose the branches while concealing the root, adopting cryptic names like "various stones" to obscure the truth. Scholars who obtain these works treasure them in caskets all their lives; sons inherit their fathers’ pursuits, grandsons follow their ancestors’ paths, passing them down through generations—yet remain deluded, never awakening to the truth. Thus, officials abandon their posts, farmers neglect their plows, merchants forsake their goods, and men of aspiration fall into poverty. I grieve deeply for them.

Here, I record these words—concise in language, simple in thought, sparing in detail. Each clause is laid out clearly, its substance verifiable, its measures and weights precise. Following their sequence, one may discern the pattern; the text’s gates and apertures lie open. Let the wise ponder and reflect, testing its meaning with their own insight.

The *Zhi Xuan*’s thirty-ninth chapter says:
*"Seeking immortality without recognizing true lead and mercury,
One idly reads ten thousand scrolls of alchemical texts—
For the lead and mercury spoken of in alchemical texts are but metaphors.
The learner must extend and expand upon them,
Lest one cling to the letter, mire in the image,
And abandon one’s own body to seek it without."*

Indeed, the sages of old pitied later generations—those who loved the Dao yet failed to grasp its teachings. Thus, they followed the elegance of others, traced the brushstrokes of ancient texts, and compiled them into illustrated volumes to guide them, truly intending to provide seekers with a path to trace the stream back to its source.

Yet in their writings, they often concealed the root, refusing to speak plainly, instead adopting names like "the five metals and eight stones" to expose only the branches. Those who later obtained these books, failing to fathom their essence, merely treasured them in caskets all their lives. Generation after generation, they remained deluded, exhausting cinnabar and mercury to no avail. Thus, officials abandoned their posts, farmers left their fields untended, merchants lost their livelihoods, and men of aspiration had no means to sustain themselves. How lamentable!

Master Wei, grieving over this, thus composed and transmitted the *Cantong Qi* to the world. Within it, measures and weights are precise, not a single word is idly spoken. Following its sequence, not a single character is superfluous; with one glance at the branches, the substance is revealed. Truly, its words are concise and easy to ponder, its matters simple and uncluttered.

Some may see its three chapters and suspect redundancy, unaware that the three chapters are but one chapter—one chapter but a single sentence.

Within its single-sentence oral formula, the teachings are dispersed across three chapters—thus the words are deliberately disordered, the gates and apertures concealed, lest the whole be carelessly revealed in one piece. Those of wisdom, if they examine and ponder it, cross-referencing with their own insight, will surely awaken to its truth.

No image is greater than Heaven and Earth, whose dark ravine spans tens of thousands of *li*. The greatness of the Dao cannot be captured in form or description; if one must describe it, then only Heaven and Earth suffice. Heaven and Earth are the greatest of all images, and within them lies the dark ravine—a celestial gulf stretching from the Tail and Winnowing Basket stars to the boundary of the Willow Star, cleaving the celestial vault in a span of countless *li*. Those who refine the elixir, if they truly model themselves upon Heaven and emulate Earth, turning inward to seek within their own bodies, will discover that within themselves lies a flask of Heaven. Only then will they understand that what Master Wei called the dark ravine is also present within them, and there is no need to seek it in the sky. What is this dark ravine? It is the Celestial River.

When the River Drum nears the Star Marker, all the people are struck with terror. The River Drum is a star beside the Celestial River, positioned between the Dipper and Ox stars. The Star Marker is the *chou* position of the celestial vault. When the River Drum aligns with the Star Marker, it drives back the Tail Cavity, linking the empty flames and herding them into the Celestial Thoroughfare, surging straight upward. At the very moment of breaking through the pass and finding the path, how could the people within the body not tremble in alarm? The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Above the Winding River, the Golden Crow flies; the Moon Maiden rejoices with the Dipper and Ox"—this refers precisely to the River Drum nearing the Star Marker. It also says: "Mountains and rivers, the great earth, erupt in fierce fire; within, the ten thousand images stir with wind and thunder"—this speaks of all the people being struck with terror.

If the sundial’s shadow recklessly advances or retreats, nine years of misfortune will follow. The sundial’s shadow refers to the firing times; advancing and retreating refer to progression and withdrawal. Nine years refers to the nine revolutions. If we compress nine years into a smaller scale, one month per cycle constitutes one revolution—the *Cui Xu Pian* speaks of "nine revolutions of effort, nine months in use." If we compress it further, within a single moment there are minor nine revolutions—the *Jin Dan Da Cheng Ji* states: "The nine revolutions of effort occur in an instant." If the sundial’s shadow recklessly advances or retreats, nine years of misfortune will follow: only if the firing times’ progression and withdrawal are not off by even a hair’s breadth can one steadily ride the Yellow Mare through the nine revolutions and remain free of calamity. Otherwise, during the nine revolutions, the Purple River Chariot will overturn, and misfortune will swiftly arrive. The *Wu Zhen Pian* states: "It all depends on the power of cultivation; if off by even a hair’s breadth, the elixir will not form." One cannot be careless.

When the August One gazes upon it, the King retreats to the rear. The *Tai Shang Su Ling Jing* states: "The human body contains the Three Ones: the Upper One is the Heavenly Emperor of the body; the Middle One is the Cinnabar Emperor of the Crimson Palace; the Lower One is the Primordial King of the Yellow Court." Now, the Upper Heavenly Emperor is the Mud Pill Monarch of the Niwan; the Middle Cinnabar Emperor is the Heavenly Son of the Crimson Palace; the Lower Primordial King is the Primordial Yang Monarch of the Cinnabar Field. When Master Wei speaks of the August One gazing upon it, he refers to directing the spirit-fire to illuminate and enter the Kan trigram, driving out the true yang within Kan. When the King retreats to the rear, this means the true yang, pressed by the fire, emerges from its position in Kan. At this point, if one mobilizes the River Chariot, the true yang will ascend and soar, transforming the true yin of the Li Palace.

The gate-bolt has its rises and falls; the celestial revolution thus proceeds. The shape of Heaven is like a pellet, revolving day and night without cease. Its north and south poles—one high, one low—form the gate-bolt. The human body is the same: the Celestial Pass is above, the Earthly Axis below. If one can turn the Celestial Pass and rotate the Earthly Axis, aligning above and below, then with each breath, a full celestial revolution is completed.

The rivers and streams never run dry; their waters flow into the sea. The sea is where all rivers converge. The waters of the rivers and streams flow into the sea without ever running dry because the great mountains and rivers are interconnected through cavities, circulating endlessly. The human body is the same: when the qi flows unimpeded, the waters of the eight meridians all reverse their course and rush into the Primordial Sea.

The female and male of Heaven and Earth linger between *zi* and *wu*. *Wu* is the center of Heaven; *zi* is the center of Earth. *Zi* and *wu* are the places where yin and yang intersect, where water and fire meet. When the sun and moon reach this point, they must linger. Today, when the sun is at its zenith, it is called "pausing at *wu*," which is the meaning of lingering. In the Dao of the elixir, ascent and descent, rising and falling, also linger between *zi* and *wu*—this follows the same path as Heaven and Earth, with no difference.

*Yin* and *shen* are the ancestors of yin and yang, the gate of all things’ exit and entry. When the Dipper points to *yin*, spring arrives under Heaven, and the yang qi begins to flourish, bringing forth the ten thousand things. When the Dipper points to *shen*, autumn arrives under Heaven, and the yin qi begins to restrain, gathering in the ten thousand things. Thus, *yin* and *shen* are the ancestors of yin and yang, the gate of all things’ exit and entry. In the Dao of the elixir, exiting at *yin* and entering at *shen*, cycling endlessly, is this not the same as Heaven and Earth?

Following the Dipper to summon the *Yao* star, grasping the balance to determine the primordial cycle. The *Shu Ji* of the *Tian Guan Shu* states: "The handle of the Northern Dipper’s seven stars drags the Dragon’s Horn; the balance of the Southern Dipper’s scoop rests on the head of Orion. At dusk, the handle establishes the cycle; at midnight, the balance establishes it; at dawn, the scoop establishes it." It also states: "The Dipper is the Emperor’s chariot, revolving at the center, governing the four directions, distinguishing yin and yang, establishing the four seasons, balancing the five phases, shifting the solar terms, and determining all cycles—all depend on the Dipper." The *Dou Suo Yin* states: "The *Chun Qiu Yun Dou Ji* says: the Dipper’s first star is the Celestial Pivot, the second is the Jade Swivel, the third is the Jade Armillary, the fourth is the Balance, the fifth is the Crossbar, the sixth is the Open Yang, and the seventh is the Twinkling Brilliance. The first through fourth form the scoop; the fifth through seventh form the handle." When Master Wei speaks of following the Dipper to summon the *Yao* star and grasping the balance to determine the primordial cycle, he refers to the celestial pivot of one’s own body pointing from *zi* and cycling through the twelve branches.

The Dipper resides at the center of Heaven just as the heart resides at the center of the human body. Thus, Heaven uses the Dipper as its mechanism, and humans use the heart as theirs. In the elixir method, the heart directs the firing times just as Heaven uses the Dipper to govern the twelve branches. The *Cui Xu Pian* states: "Seizing the Heavenly mechanism’s wonder, at midnight observe the Dipper’s handle—a few drops of pearly dew, who moves them to the rice flower’s head?" This is precisely what it means.

Ascending and steaming upon the Steamer Mountain, flames blazing below; the White Tiger sings in the lead, the Azure Dragon harmonizes behind. The Steamer Mountain is Kunlun Mountain. When the medicine ascends, the golden furnace blazes, the jade crucible boils, the tiger roars first, and the dragon sings after—like a husband leading and his wife following. The *Jin Dan Da Cheng Ji* states: "When the dragon sings and the tiger roars in the deep of night, swiftly drive the River Chariot without pause. In an instant, carry it to the Niwan’s peak, advance the fire, and refine it in the jade furnace until it resembles snow." This clarifies the meaning.

The Vermilion Bird soars and plays, its five-colored plumage dazzling—yet it encounters the spread net, pressed down and unable to rise. Its cries are mournful and bitter, like an infant longing for its mother. Overturned into the seething cauldron, its feathers and wings are crushed and broken.

The Vermilion Bird is fire. When overturned and driven within the crucible, it chases and urges the Five Agents, forming the five colors and soaring above. If enveloped by the net, wind and clouds fill the crucible, and the cries resemble those of an infant. Once caught in the net and pressed down, unable to take flight, it gathers its body and folds its wings, crouching within the crucible. The *Chilong Dadan Jue* states: "The Vermilion Bird loves to fly high, yet the Toad seizes it. Though it cries as the Alchemical Maiden, its weeping is no more than an infant’s. Its radiant power flourishes, but its hidden strength gradually wanes." This is precisely the meaning. The *Yuanyangzi Dadao Ge* says: "The Azure Dragon pursues the Tiger, the Tiger follows the Dragon. The Red Bird mates, their voices harmonious. Regulating qi and managing fire, cinnabar enters the abdomen from the Li Palace, and the body naturally ascends." This, too, clarifies the meaning.

Before half the clepsydra has passed, the dragon’s scales and mane rise, and the five colors gleam and shimmer, transforming without constancy. The crucible boils turbulently, surging violently without cease. Layer upon layer, interlocking like canine teeth, its form resembles midwinter ice. The railing drips with stalactites, towering and jumbled, heaped and propped against one another.

"Before half the clepsydra has passed, the dragon’s scales and mane rise" refers to the moment of refinement: in less than a moment, qi subtly traverses the Three Passes, yellow clouds form ranks, white sheep gather in herds, and golden coins, golden flowers, and golden scales arrive in profusion. "The five colors gleam and shimmer, transforming without constancy" describes how the wind sways the jeweled tree, its light filling the eyes, and rain strikes the jade flowers, snow covering one’s robes—crimson clouds and purple mists manifest in endless variation. "The crucible boils turbulently, surging violently without cease" means that when the fire in the Cinnabar Field blazes and the wind in the Mud Pill Palace arises, the Three Palaces fill with qi, like the moment when steamed rice in a rice steamer is fully cooked, and boiling water surges within the pot. "Layer upon layer, interlocking like canine teeth, its form resembles midwinter ice; the railing drips with stalactites, towering and jumbled, heaped and propped against one another" describes how, with each inhalation and exhalation, the elixir gradually congeals and coalesces, pouring forth the Yellow Sprout from the earth, naturally forming buds and sprouting blossoms. The *Cuixu Pian* states: "Though the toil lasts but ten months, gradually gathering and congealing, if the scholar can inwardly refine with devotion, day and night without weariness, the elixir’s form will change with each passing hour, and the fire’s work will not err from night to night. As in the scenes above, each will be personally witnessed."

When yin and yang attain their union, they dwell in tranquil harmony.
The *Wu Zhen Pian* says: "When yin and yang unite in harmony, the two eights naturally pair. If the true breath flows continuously without interruption, yin and yang will attain their union. By emptying the mind and concentrating the spirit in pure unity, yin and yang will naturally dwell in harmony."

The Azure Dragon resides in House Six, the spring blossoms of the Zhen Palace in the east at Mao. The White Tiger dwells in Pleiades Seven, the autumn awns of the Dui Palace in the west at You. The Vermilion Bird is in Spread Two, the true yang of the Li Palace in the south at Wu. When all three come to pay court, their household becomes kin. Originally but two things, in the end they become three, five, and three-five—all gathering at Danger One, converging in the place of governance. The days of refinement follow the method above.

The twenty-eight lunar lodges of the celestial circuit: the seven lodges of the east are called the Azure Dragon; the seven lodges of the west are called the White Tiger. Among the twelve celestial divisions, the middle one of the three eastern divisions is called *Great Fire*; the middle one of the three western divisions is called *Great Beam*. *Great Fire* lies at the center of the three eastern divisions, in the celestial position of Chen, corresponding to Mao, belonging to the trigram Zhen, to spring, and to wood. House Five Degrees resides within *Great Fire*, hence it is said: "The Azure Dragon resides in House Six, the spring blossoms of the Zhen Palace in the east at Mao." *Great Beam* lies at the center of the three western divisions, in the celestial position of Xu, corresponding to You, belonging to the trigram Dui, to autumn, and to metal. Pleiades Seven Degrees resides within *Great Beam*, hence it is said: "The White Tiger dwells in Pleiades Seven, the autumn awns of the Dui Palace in the west at You." "The Vermilion Bird is in Spread Two, the true yang of the Li Palace in the south at Wu" refers to Spread, a lodge of the southern quadrant, whose symbol is the Vermilion Bird. Though Spread spans eighteen degrees, only two are mentioned here, for the celestial circle of 365 degrees is bisected between the northern lodges Void and Danger, and Danger’s first degree aligns precisely with Spread Two in the south. "When all three come to pay court, their household becomes kin" means that the true qi of the three directions—Azure Dragon, White Tiger, and Vermilion Bird—all converge at the Dark Warrior’s position. House Six and Pleiades Seven correspond to the completion numbers of water and fire, while Spread Two and Danger One correspond to the generative numbers of water and fire, like kinfolk drawing near. "Originally but two things, in the end they become three, five, and three-five—all gathering at Danger One" means that at their root, they are merely water and fire. When these two are moved within the crucible, they arrange into three and five, that is, House Six, Pleiades Seven, and Spread Two. When the three families meet and converge at Danger One, the infant is formed. "The days of refinement follow the method above" means that in cultivating the Great Elixir, one must proceed according to the method above, awaiting the moment when the solitary yang arises to advance the fire, for the marvelous work begins at Void and Danger.

First white, then yellow, with red permeating both inside and out—this is called the First Crucible, its form like a grain of millet.

"First white, then yellow" describes how it initially resembles jade liquid flying through the void, then gradually appears as flowing gold filling the hut. "Red permeating both inside and out" describes how the numinous light and divine flame burn through heaven and earth, with wind, thunder, clouds, and mist filling the mountains and rivers. "Called the First Crucible" refers to the first of the nine cycles of firing. "Its form like a grain of millet" means that when the elixir head has just formed, it is no larger than a millet grain.

It is the work of spontaneity, not of deviance or falsehood.

The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir has often been regarded by ancient immortals as spontaneous. If it is truly spontaneous, what need is there for methods or oral instructions? One need only entrust it to spontaneity—why rely on a teacher’s transmission? Yet this is not so. The method of the elixir is most simple and most easy; its divine mechanism and marvelous workings require no contrivance, no thought. Thus it is called spontaneous. However, one must withdraw the gaze and return the hearing, concentrating the spirit within. With each breath, long and unbroken, neither hurried nor slow, without interruption, the spirit returns to the qi, and the qi unites with the spirit, intertwining to form the embryo. It is not merely a matter of entrusting everything to spontaneity.

The *Scripture of the Golden and Azure Dragon-Tiger* states: "The essential point of spontaneity lies in first preserving and then forgetting. First preserving and then forgetting means first preserving the spirit within the qi-cavern, and afterward forgetting it together with the qi. Practicing thus, the spirit naturally congeals, the qi naturally gathers, and the breath naturally stabilizes—this is not the same as settling the mind into quiescence like withered wood or dead ashes. For while settling the mind into quiescence like withered wood or dead ashes may serve as a means to enter the Dao, it is not sufficient for cultivating truth."

The *Zhi Xuan Pian* says: "The merit of spontaneity is partial; spontaneity arises from what is not spontaneous." This is precisely what is meant. Yet when the time arrives and the qi transforms, when the mechanism stirs and the elixir resonates, within spontaneity there lies the secret of refining and advancing the fire.

The *Awakening to Reality* states: "At first, one engages in effort, which people readily notice; by the time one reaches non-doing, the multitude knows it. They see non-doing as the essential marvel, but how could they know that effort is the foundation? If a scholar, unaware that the elixir method involves effort, immediately seeks non-doing and spontaneity, how is this different from one who neither plows nor weeds yet sits waiting for a bountiful harvest?"

When the qi of mountains and marshes steams upward, it forms clouds and becomes rain. All know that timely rain descends from heaven, and clouds emerge from mountains and rivers, yet they do not realize that the rain in the sky is the transformed qi of mountains and marshes. For the qi of mountains and marshes steams upward into the heavens, where it transforms into clouds; thus, yin and yang harmonize and produce rain. Once formed, the rain returns to the earth. The workings of the golden elixir within our bodies differ not the slightest from this. Master Wei’s use of this analogy to instruct later learners is truly ingenious in its comparisons.

"Mud dries and turns to dust; fire extinguishes and becomes earth." Mud is earth near water, heavy and sluggish by nature, residing below. When exposed to the sun and dried, its earthly nature is exhausted, and it cracks, transforming into dust. Fire is an insubstantial and luminous element; when it clings to wood, it produces smoke and flame. When the smoke dissipates and the flame cools, the nature of fire is extinguished, and the embers turn to ash and earth. Only by understanding this can one discuss the Dao of yin and yang’s reversal and return.

"When coptis is dyed, it becomes yellow; when indigo is used, it turns green. Boiling hides and leather makes glue; fermenting yeast transforms into wine. Though the materials differ, the application of effort is the same—without the proper seed, skill is in vain."

The *Awakening to Reality* states: "A thousand efforts are in vain if not for the proper material; only true lead aligns with the sagely mechanism." When true mercury obtains true lead, qi and material mutually resonate, wondrously uniting and congealing—like a husband and wife finding their match, hence called "shared material." When materials are the same, as with coptis dyeing yellow, indigo dyeing green, hides becoming glue, or yeast becoming wine, no effort is wasted, and truth naturally manifests. If one abandons this and seeks elsewhere, it is not the proper seed—labor is spent in vain.

This wondrous art is precise and not deceptive; its teachings have been transmitted for a hundred million generations, luminous and verifiable, shining like the stars across the Han sky, resplendent as the source of rivers and seas. Among the myriad secondary methods and minor arts in the world—numbering no less than three thousand six hundred—only this is the celestial treasure, the Great Elixir of the Ninefold Reverted Flow, the wondrous art of non-doing and purity. Its method is most simple and most easy; even the foolish man or woman, upon obtaining and practicing it, may transcend the ordinary and enter sagehood. Thus, the celestial precepts are most solemn, forbidding its reckless transmission to the unworthy. Those who study it in this world, without karmic affinity and resonance, will not easily encounter it.

Now, Master Wei did not conceal this celestial treasure but composed these three texts. Every word is detailed and precise, every character true and exact, shining like the stars across the Han sky, resplendent as the source of rivers and seas—truly the bright mirror for learners of immortality across a hundred million generations.

The *Zhi Xuan Pian* states: "How many scriptures there are, each line true, transmitted only to those of lofty attainment." The *Wu Jie Pian* says: "It is not that the great Dao and immortals are stingy, but that the common crowd lacks sincerity. Some believe the blind teachings of false masters and mistakenly claim that immortals deceive. Would immortals deceive? The ancient immortals left elixir texts in the world to guide later learners. Their discussions are luminous and verifiable; those who have not yet encountered them may explore them here, and those who have awakened may use them for confirmation. All who attained the Dao in antiquity did so in this manner."

Liu Haichan said: "Roaming freely through grottoes and palaces, I sought out all the immortal scriptures. Fortunately, I did not violate my vow and ultimately fulfilled this wish." Yang Xubai said: "I traversed famous mountains and wondrous realms, visiting every constellation without fail. I delved into secret formulas and divine methods, profound and abstruse, and have long delighted in them." Chen Momo said: "I perused countless texts, consulted lofty masters, and wandered like clouds and water. Reflecting on what I gained, it all aligned with the sagely teachings. There has never been one who clung to personal views without seeking the counsel of fellow Daoists, or who adhered solely to a master’s teachings without reading elixir texts."

How excellent is the saying of Shi Qizhen: "The superior person of antiquity first broadly studied elixir texts, then extensively consulted fellow Daoists. When discussing the Dao, their consultations yielded no contradictions; when contemplating the Dao, their teachers were never reckless. How can later learners claim that oral instructions are not found in texts and thus neglect their study?"

If one says, "I possess my master’s secret transmission, whose discourse on medicinal substances and fire phases is absent from ancient and modern elixir texts and unmentioned in the *Cantong Qi*," then such a person is deluded.

Enough! Reflect deeply, ensuring thoroughness; repeatedly examine the text, a thousand cycles above and below. After ten thousand readings, the radiance will be splendid, and the divine illumination may reveal itself. When instructing others, the heart’s spirit may suddenly awaken. Seek the thread, trace its origins, and you will surely find the gate and doorway.

"Reading a book a hundred times, its meaning becomes clear." If even a hundred readings suffice, how much more so a thousand or ten thousand? Thus, reciting it ten thousand times, the wondrous principle will naturally clarify. Even without receiving a master’s oral instructions, over time one will surely awaken to it. Such awakenings often occur in the deep of night or during quiet sitting. For when one concentrates the mind, savors the text deeply, and repeatedly recites it, accumulating over time, it may suddenly burst forth, and one will naturally comprehend—this is what is meant by "the heart’s spirit suddenly awakening when instructing others."

Master Guanzi said: "Think and think again, ponder deeply. If thinking does not lead to understanding, the spirit will make it clear—not by the spirit’s power, but by the extremity of sincerity." This saying is apt.

In recent times, Zhang Ziyang transmitted the *Awakening to Reality* to Minister Ma Chuhou of Fufeng, urging him: "I hope you will disseminate this text, for there will be those who, upon reading it, will awaken to its intent." Thus, in his preface, he wrote: "The *Awakening to Reality* exhaustively expounds the subtle principles of the great elixir’s medicinal substances and fire phases. If those of good fortune, possessing the bones of immortality, encounter it, their wisdom and discernment will naturally clarify, allowing them to seek understanding through the text." His earnest exhortations to later learners share the same intent as Master Wei’s—this is what is meant by "the spirit instructs, and the heart awakens." After all, such a principle truly exists.

Had this not been the case, why would these two masters have troubled themselves to compose such empty words, thereby grossly deceiving later generations? The Dao of Heaven shows no partiality, yet it is invariably transmitted to the worthy.

Tan Jingsheng entrusted his *Book of Transformations* to Song Qiqiu, who murdered Jingsheng and usurped the text, renaming it as his own. Soon after, he met an ignoble end—this was a transmission to the unworthy, bringing calamity to both. In contrast, Wu Shiyun transmitted the Dao to Xu Jingsun, who then received the supreme Dao from the Divine Mother Chen, and later passed it on to Wu. All of them attained the Dao and achieved perfection—this was a transmission to the worthy, bringing fortune to both. For the Dao of Heaven is impartial, ever bestowed upon the good. If the recipient is unworthy, the Dao does not flow in vain. How, then, could there be no distinction of fortune or misfortune in the transmission between the worthy and the unworthy?

Alas! The ways of the world have long since declined, growing ever more degenerate. The good are no longer seen in my time; if I could but meet one of steadfast virtue, it would suffice.

**Song of the Cauldron**

Its circumference is three *cun* and five *fen*, its diameter one *cun*—
This is the great treasure-cauldron of my body.
The Three Powers reside within it,
The Five Phases revolve within it.
Lead and mercury, with earth at their center—
The *Yinfu Jing* says, "Here lies the vessel from which all phenomena arise."
This is the very thing.
All the wondrous subtleties of the golden elixir emerge from this alone.
To claim it lies outside one’s body in another is not the Way of purity.

The mouth spans four and eight *cun*—
Four *cun* and eight *fen*: the Four Images and Eight Trigrams are all contained within.
The two *cun* of the lips mark the boundary between the two primal forces, above and below.

The cauldron is one *chi* and two *cun* in length,
Corresponding to the twelve months of the year and the fire phases of the celestial cycle.
Its body and belly must be evenly proportioned, neither too thick nor too thin, lest it tilt to one side.

When seated, align the abdomen upright, let warmth descend—
Sit with the eyes facing the nose, the nose facing the navel.
The body must be level and straight, not leaning or slanting.
The eyes should be half-veiled, not fully closed—
Fully closed, and one falls into the black mountain’s demon cave.
The *qi* flows through the nose, connecting the orifices;
Do not breathe coarsely, for coarse breath stokes the fire,
And when the fire blazes, the medicine scatters.

Yin above, yang below—
Water and fire in mutual balance.
The *Huanyuan Pian* says:
"Fire in the Sāgara Sea,
Waves on Kunlun Mountain—
Who can grasp the great intent of their operation?
Only the Yellow Dame, in her subtlety."
The Yellow Dame’s work ensures that water moistens below and fire flames above—
How else could they achieve balance?

The beginning and end are martial, the middle is civil—
The beginning and end are the dark and new moons,
When yin reaches its extreme and yang begins to stir—hence the martial fire.
The middle is the full moon,
When yang reaches its peak and yin begins to stir—hence the civil fire.
Yet these terms—dark moon, new moon, full moon—are but metaphors;
One must not seek their meaning solely on paper.

It begins with seventy, ends with thirty, and two hundred and six—all must be harmonized.
"Thirty" refers to the three decades.
Seventy and thirty sum to one hundred, which, with two hundred and six, makes three hundred and six—
Corresponding to the three hundred and sixty days of the annual celestial cycle.
When cultivation reaches one hundred days, the sacred embryo first quickens.
Once quickened, for the next two hundred and sixty days,
One must skillfully harmonize the *qi* and fire phases,
Ensuring the warmth never ceases—
Then the elixir’s work naturally completes.
The *Cuixu Pian* says:
"In nurturing, be ever steadfast;
Let the gentle wind blow constantly toward the *Kan* trigram.
Whether walking, sitting, lying, or eating, remain thus—
Fear only that the fire grows cold and the elixir’s power lags.
There is no other hardship."

Yin fire is white, the yellow sprout is lead—
From *zi* to *si* is the phase of yang fire;
From *wu* to *hai* is the phase of yin fire.
*You* resides in the west, and the west corresponds to metal—hence "yin fire is white."
The *Wuzhen Pian* says: "The toad’s light shines all day upon the Western River."
Lead is born from earth, silver from lead;
When silver is refined from lead, it forms the yellow sprout—
This is true lead.
The *Jinbi Longhu Jing* says: "Refine lead to seek the yellow color."

The two sevens gather to assist the human—
The two sevens are the seven lunar lodges of the Eastern Green Dragon
And the seven lunar lodges of the Western White Tiger.
The human is the central position.
When the two sevens gather to assist the human,
It is the coiling of the dragon and the mating of the tiger, converging at the center.
Then, as if the suddenness of the Southern Sea met the fleetingness of the Northern Sea
In the land of primordial chaos—
This is how the elixir is refined.
The *Wuzhen Pian* says:
"The three families meet to form the infant,"
And again:
"The Five Phases must all enter the center."
Is this not what it means?

Nourish the brain, stabilize it, and ascend to the mysterious—
The brain is the upper field, the palace where the original spirit resides.
If one can grasp the original spirit and lodge it in its proper abode,
True *qi* naturally ascends, and true breath naturally stabilizes.
This is what is meant by:
"When one orifice opens, a hundred orifices all open;
When the great pass is unblocked, a hundred passes all unblock."
When forging the elixir, the embryo enters the mouth;
After the work is complete, the embryo emerges from the shell—
Neither departs from this.
The *Jingzhong Yin* says:
"I cultivate Kunlun and obtain the true formula;
Daily I practice without cease.
One day, when the work is complete, no one will know—
On all sides, night-shining gates will appear."
This is the result of long cultivation, when the work is ample—
Not the achievement of a single morning or evening.

The child abides in the center and is peacefully preserved—
When the infant dwells within the womb, nurtured by the devoted care of the Earth Mother,
It is peacefully preserved.

The comings and goings do not leave the gate—
This gate is the Mysterious Female’s gate.
The comings and goings are the ebb and flow of breath.
When the ebb and flow do not depart from the Mysterious Female’s gate,
yin and yang qi are naturally full and the spirit naturally flows.
Gradually, the great nature becomes pure:
In movement and stillness, sitting and lying down,
it is continuous as if preserved—
Day by day, it gradually congeals and gathers.
Once the fetal qi has congealed, the infant appears in form,
and the nature becomes ever more refined.

Returning to the One, restoring the original source—
The Great Elixir’s Way is simply embracing the origin and guarding the One.
At the beginning, it lies in yin and yang and the Five Phases;
at the end, it arrives at primordial chaos and the Infinite.
This is called returning to the One and restoring the source.

Love and reverence like lord and minister—
Living in seclusion for a year, rising early and retiring late without slack,
loving and revering it as a minister serves his lord.
After one full cycle, the toil is great.
The *Huánjīn Piān* says:
*"Joyful for a hundred thousand kalpas,
yet toiling for one or two years—
Since ancient times, none has entered seclusion for a year
and ascended to immortality in an instant."*
The *Língyuán Dàdào Gē* says:
*"In later years, when the work is complete, one roams freely;
At first, cultivation must be diligent—
Amid diligence, one must not be idle,
idly preserving the original spirit."*
How profound its words! How profound!

Guard closely, lest you fall into confusion—
The *Cuìxū Piān* says:
*"By day, wield the numinous banner;
By night, pluck the mushroom of fire.
In practice, be ever vigilant against danger—
Without a fellow cultivator to correct you,
how can you know if the furnace’s fire-timing is amiss?"*
For the Way is an inch higher, and the demon an inch taller.
In every moment of the hundred divisions of the day,
beware of confusion—
The cultivator must ever remain alert.

The path is distant, profoundly mysterious—
Moving within the Mysterious Female,
in a single day one travels eighty-four thousand miles—
Is this not a distant path?
Vast beyond measure, minute beyond limit,
welcoming it, one sees not its head;
following it, one sees not its tail—
Is this not profoundly mysterious?
One who attains this can command creation,
invert yin and yang,
and unite the Qian and Kun of one’s own body.

The *dāoguī* touches, the po-soul is guarded—
The *dāoguī* is the elixir’s head;
the po-soul, the dragon and tiger.
When transported to the peak of Kunlun,
it transforms into jade nectar flowing into the mouth—
Then the wind is calm, the waves still,
the tiger subdued, the dragon tamed.

Attaining longevity, dwelling in the immortal village—
After the elixir is complete,
one is free and unbound,
soaring in the high Dao,
indifferent to mountains or markets—
all may serve as a place of retreat.
Yet Master Wei does not speak of dwelling in the city or court,
but of the immortal village—
for in the end, the mountains are the quiet affinity.

Those who delight in the Way seek its root—
The root is the root of Heaven and Earth,
the foundation of the Golden Elixir.
The *Cuìxū Piān* says:
*"Once you recognize the elixir’s foundation,
whether releasing or gathering, it is always this—
Without a perfected one to point the way,
who could know it?"*
If one who delights in the Way can seek and find this,
how fortunate!

Examine the Five Phases, determine the precise measures—
When the Five Phases flow in sequence, they give life to humans;
when reversed, they serve the elixir.
The method must be carefully examined.
If the fire count is excessive, it scorches;
if the water measure is too great, it overflows—
The balance of weight must be precisely determined.

Contemplate deeply—no need for discussion;
Conceal it deeply, guard it, do not transmit it—
The *Wùzhēn Piān* says:
*"Lately, the golden light shines through the body,
yet I do not speak of this to ordinary people."*
Quietly reflect on this principle:
The Way is the secret of a thousand sages.
Those who attain it keep it in their breast,
self-sufficient in its use—
Why then lightly reveal it,
carelessly leaking it,
eagerly speaking of it to others?
Though for one’s own sake this may suffice,
what of the world and later generations?
This is why the *Cāntóngqì* was composed.

Riding a white crane, driving a dragon-scale chariot,
Roaming the Great Void, visiting the Immortal Lord,
Receiving the talismanic register, styled a Perfected One—
After the embryo is complete and the work accomplished,
one must regulate the spirit and emerge from the shell.
Now one may ride a white crane or mount a fiery dragon,
transcending the three realms’ difficult paths,
ascending to the Primordial Heaven of the Highest Origin—
From this, one attains carefree joy,
equal in years to Heaven,
styled a Perfected One.
Yet all phenomena are empty and illusory—
What white crane or dragon-scale is there?
If you say he is a Buddha, you yourself become a demon—
What Immortal Lord is there?

Know that the white crane and dragon-scale
are transformations of one’s own numinous power,
and the Immortal Lord is one’s own Three Pure Ones—
Why gaze upward in vain?
Those who do not grasp this principle
spend their days in delusion, awaiting a heavenly decree.
Some are seized by black tigers,
swallowed by giant pythons—
Is this not lamentable?

The Song people dug a well and gained a man’s strength,
and it was passed down that a man emerged from the earth.
Xu Jingyang’s entire household attained the Dao,
and later generations believed they lifted roof tiles into the clouds—
How excessive is the world’s love of strange tales!
Yet this may be spoken to the wise—
it is difficult to discuss with the vulgar.
To the wise, one speaks of conduct that accords with virtue;
to the foolish, one reverses the teaching, saying, *"I presume."*
Where can I find one of perfect discernment
to discuss these matters to their fullest?

Alas!

The *Cantong Qi* presents its outlines in broad strokes, yet it cannot maintain purity and unity. Its explanations overflow and scatter, leaving subtle details incomplete and its scope too broad, its meaning only vaguely discernible. Now, I have once more compiled and recorded this text to fill its omissions, refine its obscurities, and draw together its scattered threads so that its intent may be unified and its purpose unobstructed. Thus, I compose this again, designating the Three in Mutual Accordance. In this, the essence and nature of the *Great Change* are fully expressed: *can* refers to the Three, *tong* to their mutual accord, and *qi* to their accordance. This book borrows from the *Great Change* to expound the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, and it further accords with the workings of the furnace and fire. The transformations of yin and yang in these three are nearly indistinguishable.

Master Wei pitied later students for their lack of understanding, and so he composed this single work to set forth the outlines of the Great Dao. Yet, fearing the leakage of profound secrets, he dared not fully reveal it in one continuous exposition. Instead, he resorted to indirect allusions and circuitous metaphors, allowing his explanations to overflow and scatter. Concerned that its scope was too broad and its meaning too vague to fully convey the subtleties, he appended the *Song of the Cauldron* at the end. Whatever omissions existed in the text of the main chapters, he filled in this song; whatever obscurities lay in its principles, he refined in this song. Thus, the three chapters’ words are drawn together, their intent unified, so that later readers may find a direct path without straying from its purpose.

The *Cui Xu Pian* states:
*The essential Dao is nurtured in the midst of nothingness,
Within it, a true and certain core is formed.
Simplicity and wonder hold the celestial mechanism,
Yet scattered across alchemical texts, it refuses to be revealed.*

If this is so, then why did Master Wei tirelessly expound his teachings, revisiting them again and again? His intent was no different. Otherwise, the essentials of the Great Elixir could be conveyed in a single phrase or half a sentence—why labor over such exhaustive detail?

The *Great Change* embodies the nature and essence of things, each in its proper measure. The teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi delve into their mysteries and can be applied. The workings of the furnace and fire have a true basis. These three Ways emerge from a single source, sharing the same path. The Dao is present everywhere, and every head is aligned with it. The Three Sages each followed their proper measure: the *Yi* was composed through observation, the mysteries of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi were explored to create the elixir, and the furnace and fire stole their mechanism to forge the art of refinement. Whether expressed in words, cultivated in the body, or embedded in objects, all arise from observing the heavens and examining the earth, from understanding the laws of yin and yang’s union, and from drawing upon distant principles and applying them near at hand to grasp the transformations and permutations of creation. Thus, these Three Ways exist. Though their paths differ, they converge in the same place—this is why they are called the Three in Mutual Accordance.

Branches, stems, flowers, and leaves—all fruits hang and spread forth, yet their origin lies in the root. They do not lose their fundamental nature. What I have spoken here is sincere and without error. The Dao is the root of the ten thousand things, just as the root is to a tree. When it sprouts into branches and stems, the *Yi* of the Three Sages emerges. When it scatters into flowers and leaves, the techniques of the furnace and fire arise. When it ripens into fruit and returns to the root, the Yellow Emperor and Laozi do not lose their fundamental nature, refining the golden liquid and inner elixir to nurture the sacred embryo. Master Wei spoke of these three as mutually accordant, establishing them side by side—not out of a fondness for excessive solemnity, but to enable students to thoroughly comprehend yin and yang and deeply penetrate the workings of creation. His words arose from sincerity, and thus they are precise and without error.

I, a rustic from the state of Kuai, a withered scholar dwelling in a secluded valley, cling to simplicity and plainness, finding no joy in power or glory. I dwell in obscurity, indifferent to profit or fame, holding fast to tranquility and contentment, seeking peace in quietude. Thus, I have composed this text.

Master Wei was born in the Eastern Han, named Boyang, styled Yun Ya Zi. He hailed from Shangyu in Guiji. Now, referring to him as from the state of Kuai is a veiled expression. He was the son of a noble family, inheriting its legacy of cap and sash, yet he alone did not serve in office. Instead, he cultivated the true, lived in quiet obscurity, nurtured his will in emptiness and nothingness, and was broadly learned in literature and the apocrypha. Serene and unadorned, he held fast to the fundamental, following only the Dao. To him, official robes and carriages were but chaff and husks.

Though none can trace the lineage of his master’s transmission, he obtained the oral instructions of the Great Elixir. Thereupon, he distilled the *Zhouyi* to compose this *Cantong qi* in three chapters. Fearing his words might yet fall short, he further composed the *Song of the Tripod* to supplement omissions, refine obscurities, and deepen the subtleties of the three chapters. He secretly imparted it to Xu Jingxiu of Qingzhou, who, concealing his name, wrote a commentary. By the reign of Emperor Huan, it was transmitted to Chunyu Shutong of the same commandery, whence it spread through the world.

Alas! This book elucidates the essentials of the Dao and reveals the workings of heaven—its grasp of truth is so precise, its exposition so comprehensive. Such mastery cannot arise from mere knowledge but must spring from lived realization. Yet Master Wei still humbled himself as a rustic, a withered tree—such was the depth of his modesty.

The *Song* expounds the *Yijing*, preserving the legacy of the Three Sages. Its intent is singular, its principles unified, ever striving to align with nature, radiate spirit, and foster transformation, that harmony may flow through the four seas and peace endure as a model for ten thousand generations. It offers a sequence to govern, a path to nurture life: the golden light of inner cultivation arises naturally, virtue thickens, roots return to origin, and the Way draws near to the heart, never leaving the self. Embracing the One without abandonment ensures enduring existence. Paired with dietary arts—yin and yang in harmonious union—lead and mercury are subdued. The dual yellows are first swept away, and the remaining stones, though once prized, may all be discarded. Through careful application and refinement, they become either yellow or white, forming substances cherished by the world. The alchemical furnace is truly wondrous.

Fu Xi drew the eight trigrams and doubled them; King Wen arranged the hexagrams and observed their images, appending judgments; Confucius further praised them with the Ten Wings. Thus, they sought to align the principles of life and destiny, weaving the Dao of heaven and earth into a whole. Hence, as a calendar, it offers a clear path to follow, a law for all ages; as a guide to governance, its application is effortless, a model for a hundred kings. The Dao of the *Yijing* is vast indeed.

The Yellow Emperor observed the Way of heaven and mastered its workings; Laozi, thick in virtue, was likened to an unblemished child. The *Yinfu jing* in over three hundred characters and the *Daode jing* in over five thousand words—though their discussions unfold and return—all reveal the marvels of creation’s spontaneity. Thus, those who cultivate the Elixir must take the Yellow Emperor and Laozi as their foundation. Their teachings are supreme.

As for the arts of dietary ingestion, when the marvel of yin-yang harmony is achieved to subdue lead and mercury, the dual yellows are first swept away, and the remaining stones, though once prized, may all be discarded. Through careful application and refinement, they become either yellow or white, forming substances cherished by the world. The alchemical furnace is truly extraordinary.

The *Yijing*, the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi—each in its own way.

The alchemical furnace, too—three branches arrayed, stems interconnected, emerging from different names yet all through a single gate. This is called the Three Phases. Not merely an accumulation of parallel phrases or harmonious couplets, this text verily contains its truth. Were I to fabricate falsehoods, I would incur the fault of superfluity. The *Cantong qi* offers but a glimpse of its essence, its words few yet its meaning vast. Later generations ought to follow Wei Boyang, composing the *Dingqi Ge* to append to the three chapters. Its characters, bright and resplendent, gleam with clarity—how could this be mere embellishment of yellow and white, or the pairing of rhymes for aesthetic delight? Rather, it holds the most profound truth within.

To claim these are deceptive words meant to delude posterity would be something even those with slight understanding of the Dao would refuse to do. How could Wei Boyang have done so? Moreover, heaven’s judgment is luminous and clear; blessings and misfortunes are never far. Why risk deceiving later generations and the world with falsehoods, thereby incurring the sin of deceiving heaven? The *Dingqi Ge* was composed precisely to fill the omissions and illuminate the abstruse depths of the three chapters. Though its verses are but three characters long, appearing brief, their meaning is vast and all-encompassing, lacking nothing. Truly, it is plain and lucid, ready to be put into practice. If later scholars do not place their trust in this, where else shall they turn?

*"Entrust the seasons, avoid harm,
Rely on the mountains’ steadfastness,
Wander the boundless expanse,
Neighbor to the spirits.
Transform the form into immortality,
Sink into silence without sound.
Once in a hundred generations,
Roam the human realm,
Spreading feathered wings—
East and west, south and north,
Scorched by drought, beset by floods,
Water and famine divide the land,
Branches and leaves wither, yellow,
Losing their splendor.
Each bears the other’s burden—
Secure, long life."*

This is Wei Boyang’s three-character hidden message:
*"Entrust" (委) and "spirits" () bear each other—Wei (魏).
"Once in a hundred" (百) is "white" (白), and "white" (白) with "person" (人) bear each other—Bo (伯).
"Scorched by drought" (汤遭旱) leaves no water, an ordeal of "yang" (阳); "beset" (阨) is "阝," and "阝" with "yang" (阳) bear each other—Yang (阳).*
Wei Boyang’s intent is most subtle indeed.

During the Yuanfeng era, Lü Jifu, prefect of Shanzhou, encountered Lü Dongbin at Tianqing Abbey, who took up a brush and inscribed two poems. One read:
*"This rustic was once a guest of Tiantai,
Dwelling south of Stone Bridge.
Father and son, just the two of us,
Loving song but not clapping."*
Lü Jifu’s son-in-law, Yu Zhong, interpreted it thus: *"Tiantai guest" is "guest" (宾); "Stone Bridge" is "cave" (洞); "two mouths" is Lü (吕); "loving song but not clapping" means reciting poetry. The one reciting this poem—could it not be Lü Dongbin?*
Thus do ancient immortals speak to the world: their sole aim is to elucidate the Dao and guide later generations, never seeking to glorify their own names.

**Eulogy and Preface**
The *Cantong qi* is crude in diction yet vast in Dao, subtle in speech yet profound in meaning. It arrays the Five Emperors to establish governance, pairing them with the Three Sovereigns to institute order. Though ruler and minister differ in rank, above and below lack fixed standards. Its precepts on governance do not attain great peace; its methods of dietary ingestion cannot confer longevity; learning to nurture nature does not extend lifespan. Yet when it dissects yin and yang, harmonizing their weights; when it aligns the waxing and waning of the sun and moon; when the Eight Trigrams form images; when male and female enact transformation; when hardness and softness, movement and stillness are distinguished like rice and salt—using the *Yijing* as its witness, its intent is most resolute.

Thus, annotations were established to transmit it to later worthies—only those who comprehend the great image may attain longevity, strengthen the self, and benefit the body. For this Dao, one must devote particular attention.

This piece is titled "Eulogy and Preface," being later eulogistic and prefatory words on Master Wei’s book. It is also said: "Annotations were established to transmit it to later worthies," which is clearly not part of Master Wei’s original text. Zhu Xi remarked: "Some call it a postscript, others a eulogy by Master Wei." Examining its content and intent, it appears to be a postscript to the annotations. Peng’s preface states: "Master Wei secretly showed it to Xu, an Attendant in Qingzhou, instructing him to annotate it. Xu concealed his name and composed the annotations. By the time of Emperor Huan, it was again transmitted to Chunyu Shutong of the same commandery, whence it circulated in the world." This may well be Xu’s own words, though the annotations themselves no longer survive.

Peng Zhenyi said: "According to various Daoist texts, the three chapters of the *True Contract* were each composed by Master Wei, Xu the Attendant, and Chunyu Shutong." This claim is gravely mistaken. Moreover, Master Wei himself states in this text: "Now I have again compiled and supplemented omissions." Thus, it is evident that the work was composed by Master Wei alone. Furthermore, Liu Zhigu, the Tang dynasty Perfected One of Shu, in his *Discourse on the Mysterious Pivot of Sun and Moon*, presented to the Mysterious Lineage, also thoroughly addressed this. The accounts of Xu’s annotations and Chunyu’s transmission thus raise no further doubts.

I have repeatedly pondered this matter, sighing that the ages are too distant to verify what is true or false—all remains unknowable. One evening, in a state of quiescent concentration, it was as if someone whispered in my ear: "Wei Boyang composed the *Cantong qi*, while Xu the Attendant annotated it. The bamboo slips were disordered, hence the mixture of four-character and five-character lines, as well as prose." Startled awake, I reflected on this explanation. The upper chapter contains "Qian, Kun, Kan, Li, Zhun, Meng"; the middle chapter repeats "Qian, Kun, Kan, Li, Zhun, Meng." The upper chapter has "seven, eight, nine, six"; the middle chapter repeats "seven, eight, nine, six." The upper chapter states, "The solar terms set the measure"; the middle chapter says, "Carefully observe the solar terms." The upper chapter reads, "Zhen receives geng in the west"; the middle chapter reads, "Above Mao and Bi, Zhen emerges as zheng." Between them, "wu-ji" and "hun-tun" appear thrice, "three-five" and "dark-moon, new-moon" appear four times. With such repetition in meaning, the claim that three individuals each composed one chapter may not be entirely baseless. Yet with text and annotations intermingled, it remains unclear which is the original and which the commentary.

I wished to separate the four-character lines, five-character lines, and prose into three distinct sections, so that text and annotations would not be confused, thereby aiding later scholars in their study. However, once the book was completed, it could not be altered. Thus, I record here what I have heard, appending it to the end of the scroll, awaiting the discernment of future sages.

*Elucidations on the Zhouyi Cantong qi*, Scroll Three
<*Zi* Section, Daoist Category, *Elucidations on the Zhouyi Cantong qi*>
*Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Resolving Doubts on the Zhouyi Cantong qi Elucidations*
By Yu Yan of the Song Dynasty, Upper Chapter

A certain edition reads *kuang* (匡) as *yuan* (垣), while Chen Baoyi’s annotated edition alters it to *weiguo* (围郭) to avoid taboo. "Turning the hub and aligning the axle" means that in cultivating the elixir, one must rectify the heart, just as turning the hub requires aligning the axle. The axle lies within the hub to penetrate its spokes, yet it also extends beyond the hub to converge with it. One edition mistakenly renders *zhou* (轴, axle) as *fu* (辐, spoke).

The "four trigrams of the female and male" recapitulate the preceding line—"Qian and Kun as gateways, Kan and Li as the enclosing frame"—summarizing these four trigrams. They represent the cauldron, the vessel, and the medicinal substances; the subsequent "sixty trigrams" denote the firing times. As the middle chapter states, "These four are the primordial chaos," echoing the earlier line, "Qian’s firmness and Kun’s yielding, Kan and Li at the forefront." Later, it says, "The sixty trigrams are deployed as a chariot," completing the sixty-four trigrams—all of which serve the Dao of our elixir. This is why it is called the *Zhouyi Cantong qi*. One edition incorrectly identifies the "four trigrams of the female and male" as Zhen, Dui, Xun, and Gen.

"As bellows and pipes, congealing the spirit": Chen Huizhen’s annotated edition renders this as *hu* (互, mutual). "Like a charioteer holding the reins and bit, with standards and guiding cords, aligning with the tracks and staying centered to govern externally" means that, just as a charioteer seated within the carriage controls the horse’s advance and retreat to conform to the guiding cords, so too does this parallel the middle chapter’s "the dragon and horse are harnessed, the enlightened ruler governs the times." An older edition reads, "Like a craftsman holding the reins and bit with standards," but this is incorrect. Earlier scholars, following the error, annotated it as "like a craftsman adhering to the inked cord and compass, or a charioteer following the bit and tracks," which is mistaken.

"Combined to make sixty" means that two trigrams are used daily, and in a month of thirty days, they combine to form sixty trigrams. One edition incorrectly reads "combined to make sixty-four." "Employ them in sequence": one edition mistakenly renders *yi* (依, follow) as *ru* (如, like).

Having not yet reached *mei-shuang* refers to the passage below concerning "from the last day of the lunar month to the dawn of the new moon." One edition mistakenly renders *mei* (昧) as *hui* (晦). The reason is that *ji-wei* (既未) denotes the last day of the lunar month, while *mei-shuang* (昧爽) denotes the first day of the new moon. If interpreted as "from *ji-wei* to *hui-shuang*," it would mean "from the last day of the month to the last dawn of the month," which is a gross error in textual meaning.

In spring and summer, one relies on the inner body, proceeding from *zi* to *chen* and *si*; in autumn and winter, one accords with external application, from *wu* through *xu* and *hai*. This is because when speaking of two trigrams together, the first pertains to spring and summer, from *zi* to *si*, where yang ascends from below; the latter pertains to autumn and winter, from *wu* to *hai*, where yin descends from above. If speaking of only one trigram, a single trigram has but six lines, and when matched with the twelve hours, it falls short by half. Thus, in daily practice, two trigrams must be spoken of together.

"Attaining its sequence": one edition mistakenly renders *xu* (序, sequence) as *li* (理, principle).

"Transformations within": Zhu Xi’s annotated edition renders *bianhua* (变化) as *shengjiang* (升降, ascent and descent). Another edition reads, "ascent and descent, transformations within."

"Enveloping": This adopts the language of the *Wenzi* and *Tongxuan Zhenjing*. An older edition incorrectly reads *baonang* (包囊, wrapping).

"Using the formless to regulate the formed" refers to the *zhi* (制) of creation, not the *zhi* of control. The method of the Great Elixir creates existence from non-existence, hence "using the formless to regulate the formed." Some explain it as the *zhi* of control, elucidating that the formless is spirit, mercury, or *li*, while the formed is qi, lead, or *kan*—this is but one interpretation, yet it does not align with Master Wei’s original intent. I have repeatedly examined this and found that, owing to the *Longhu Jing*’s statement of "mutual regulation of being and non-being, the Vermilion Bird blazing in the void," later scholars, without scrutinizing the context, invariably interpret *zhi* as the *zhi* of creation rather than control.

Again, in the middle chapter, it states, "The firm and yielding are decided," meaning the firm and yielding do not encroach upon one another. Thus, it continues, "The Five Phases guard their boundaries, neither recklessly expanding nor contracting," which differs from the later chapter’s meaning of "the firm and yielding parting ways." Some, clinging to the *Longhu Jing*’s statement of "the firm and yielding contending in balance without encroachment, the blame does not lie with fire but with the censure of earth," mistakenly apply the meaning of "the firm and yielding parting ways" here. Today, it is commonly held that Wei Boyang composed the *Cantong Qi* based on the *Longhu Jing*, and thus could not but follow its doctrines. Yet they do not realize that the *Longhu Jing* merely paraphrases the *Cantong Qi* and was actually written after Master Wei’s work. Zhu Xi remarked, "Later scholars, seeing that Wei Boyang’s transmission included a *Longhu Shangjing*, fabricated this text, generally modeling it on the *Cantong Qi*, though with some misinterpretations." I once, not having grasped this, dared not readily accept Zhu Xi’s view. Later, upon repeated reflection and comparative study of the *Cantong Qi*, I came to see the inconsistencies in the *Longhu Jing* and realized it truly was a paraphrase of the *Cantong Qi*.

Master Wei’s composition of the *Cantong Qi* borrows the hexagrams of the *Zhouyi* to elucidate the secrets of elixir-making, not to expound upon the *Longhu Jing*. Had he indeed expanded upon the *Longhu Jing*, it should have been called the *Longhu Cantong Qi*, not the *Zhouyi Cantong Qi*.

Nevertheless, the *Longhu Jing* is also a fine text. Cai Jitong deeply admired its words, such as, "The Primordial Sovereign first refines mercury; the spirit chamber embraces emptiness," and, "The essentials of spontaneity: first preserve, then let go"—truly profound insights. Yet it is not a work predating Master Wei.

"Implements and vessels": Li Baosu’s annotated edition reads *zhe* (者, those) as *jie* (皆, all). "As evidence": one edition mistakenly renders *zheng* (征) as *zheng* (证).

The phrase "相当一本" should read "当作合," not the reverse. "始终" in one edition is mistakenly rendered as "终始." "皆禀中宫" in one version reads "禀作在," while another has "中宫所禀," both incorrect. "之时" in one edition is altered to "之际." "统黄化" in one version reads "化黄包."

In Zhu Xi’s *Kaoyi*, "鄞鄂" is written as "根鄂," where "鄂" means "calyx." As the *Book of Songs* states, "鄂不韡韡," this is the correct form of "鄂." Some versions render it as "垠堮," "圻堮," or "釿锷," all incorrect. "称元皇" in one edition reads "称作当." "元年" in one version is mistakenly written as "元." "乃芽滋" appears as "芽乃生" in one edition and "乃牙滋" in another.

Regarding the line "复卦建始初," some suspected redundancy with "关睢建始初" and changed "初" to "萌." Zhu Xi noted this as a reckless alteration by those ignorant of ancient rhymes. Similarly, "九六亦相当" was suspected of repeating "刚柔相当" and thus "当" was changed to "应," both incorrect.

"日月无双明" means that when the sun rises, the moon sets, and when the moon rises, the sun sets—day and night alternate in their illumination. One edition mistakenly renders "无" as "," but in ancient texts, "无" was used where modern texts use "無," as in the *Yijing*’s "无妄无咎." Another version reads "焕炳而成双."

"兔者吐生光" appears as "兔魄吐精光" in one edition and "兔魄吐生光" in another. "东方丧其明" was miswritten as "东北丧其明" in an old edition, likely due to the *Yijing*’s phrase "东北丧朋," perpetuating the error through unexamined habit.

Examining the *Yijing*’s *Kun* hexagram: "西南得朋, 东北丧朋" refers to the southwest, where *Dui* (west) and *Li* (south) are yin hexagrams, and *Xun* is also yin—thus "得朋" (gaining companions). The northeast, however, contains *Zhen*, *Gen*, *Kan*, and *Qian*, all yang hexagrams—hence "丧朋" (losing companions). Yet Master Wei’s statement, "坤乙三十日东方丧其明," refers to the thirtieth day’s dawn, when the moon sets in the east at the *yi* position, not to the *Kun* hexagram’s "东北丧朋." How is this clarified?

The western regions *geng* and *xin*, the southern *bing* and *ding*, and the eastern *jia* and *yi* are the six nodes within a month where the moon appears at dusk and sets at dawn—what Wang Baoyi called the "three shifts of the moon." On the third day, the moon appears at dusk in the west at *geng*; by the sixteenth, it sets at dawn in the west at *xin*. On the eighth day, it appears at dusk in the south at *ding*; by the twenty-third, it sets at dawn in the south at *bing*. On the fifteenth day, it appears at dusk in the east at *jia*; by the thirtieth, it sets at dawn in the east at *yi*. The principle is thus evident—how could *yi* be in the northeast?

Since ancient times, *jia* and *yi* have both been placed in the east, not the northeast.
