If *yi* were in the northeast, why not also call the fifteenth day, when the celestial stems are full, "盛满甲东北"? *Jia* is closer to the northeast yet is not called so—how much less *yi*, which precedes *jia* and leans toward the southeast? Clearly, it is not in the northeast.

"九六亦相当" was explained above under "复卦建始初." "易象索灭藏" refers to the thirtieth night, when the symbols of the sun and moon both sink into the north. One edition mistakenly renders "易象" as "阳."

"象彼仲冬节至以晓后生" was misplaced in the postscript of an old edition. Chen Baoyi moved it before "子当右转," both incorrect. This passage rhymes with two "伤" sounds and, when connected to the earlier "易象索灭藏," also rhymes with two "藏" sounds. Ancient writers often used repeated rhymes, but later readers, not understanding this, recklessly altered the text, disrupting its coherence. Examples include "关睢建始初" with "复卦建始初" and "刚柔相当" with "九六亦相当"—all repeated rhymes. How could such repetitions be hastily dismissed as errors?

"商旅" in one edition reads "贾作商." "浩广" in one version is written as "广作旷."

"眇难睹" in one edition reads "睹作觌." "符征" in one edition reads "征作证." "节令" in one edition reads "节作时." "中稽于人心" in one edition reads "心作情." "依卦变" in one edition reads "循卦节." "循彖辞" in one edition reads "循作因彖作象," which is incorrect.

"乾坤用施行天地然后治" in one edition reads "治作理," while another reads "天下然后治." These two lines appear to quote the *Qian* hexagram’s "Qian yuan yong jiu, tianxia zhi" (When Qian’s primal power is activated through the Nine, all under heaven is well-ordered). However, considering the preceding "Kan and Li are the two functions of Qian and Kun," this suggests Kan and Li serve as the functions of Qian and Kun—not the "yong jiu" (Nine) or "yong liu" (Six). As in the earlier passage "In the east, its brightness is lost," this does not refer to the Kun hexagram’s "In the northeast, one loses one’s companions." Master Wei’s composition of this work largely employs figurative language, and one need not cling rigidly to literal interpretations. "可不慎乎" in one edition reads "可得不慎乎," which is incorrect.

"魁杓" in one edition reads "杓作柄." "誃离即别离也" in one edition reads "移离," while another reads "坎离." "仰俯" in other editions reads "俯仰"; Zhu Xi noted that "仰俯" is the correct reading to preserve the rhyme, and this version follows his emendation. "统录" in one edition reads "统作总." "日含五行精" in one edition reads "含作合," which is incorrect. "诘过贻主" in one edition reads "结过移主." "辰极处正" in one edition reads "处作受," while another reads "正作政." "布政" in one edition reads "政德," which is incorrect. "终修" in one edition reads "修作循," while another reads "中美." "以掩蔽" in one edition reads "以作已."

The presence and absence likewise depend on each other, referring to the mutual reliance of the water and fire *qi* to complete the workings of creation. In some editions, "有无" appears as "金炁" or "吟," both of which are incorrect. "进退分布" in one edition reads "进退作退而," which is incorrect. "采之白" in one edition reads "采作望," while another reads "揺." "白里" in one edition reads "包裹." "相扶" in one edition reads "扶作拘." "相连" in one edition reads "连作通."

"This is not the method of visualizing the Five Viscera" means that this Dao is not the practice of meditating upon and storing thoughts within the five viscera. If one takes the two characters "是非" to mean "right and wrong," one has misunderstood. "次日辰" in one edition reads "次作以," which is incorrect. "累土" in one edition reads "周." "而意悦" in one edition reads "意悦喜."

"To tread through fire without scorching, to enter water without getting wet" describes the state after the Dao is accomplished and the Dharma-body is realized. If one were to cast the flesh-and-blood body into water or fire, how could it merely be a matter of scorching or wetting? "移居" in one edition reads "居作名," which is incorrect. "功满" in one edition reads "满作成," which is incorrect.

"偃月" — given that the following text reads "汞日则偃," it is likely that "铅" was mistakenly transmitted as "鼎." "作鼎炉" in older editions reads "作法," which is incorrect. "亘亦八" in one edition reads "亦如之." "亏明" in one edition reads "明作伤." "金本从日生" — at the new moon, the moon receives the light of the sun, for the moon’s radiance is born from the sun. At the beginning of each month, at dawn, the moon conjoins with the sun. Here, "金火" refers to the sun and moon. In older editions, "日" appears as "月," which is incorrect. If one were to say that "金本从月生," it would be as if saying "月本从月生," which makes no sense.

"数称五" in one edition reads "数作号." "含育" in one edition reads "贪欲." "伫息" refers to the unification of concentrated stillness and the breath, merging the two into one, like the union of husband and wife. In one edition, "息" appears as "思," while in another, it appears as "恩," both of which are incorrect. "执平" in one edition reads "执作气."

"Soil travels through the four seasons, guarding its boundaries and adhering to its rules" — in all editions except Zheng Xu’s, this line appears after "寿命得长久," but in Zheng Xu’s edition, it is placed after "本性共宗祖." "Gold takes cinnabar as its master, harmonizing with mercury" is a direct allusion to the transformation of ordinary cinnabar and mercury, serving as a metaphor for the alchemical process. It does not rely on external objects for comparison or juxtapose internal elements with one another.

"以黍" in one edition reads "黍作粟," which is incorrect. "其卵" in one edition reads "卵作子," which is incorrect. "泥汞" in one edition reads "汞作澒." "炼飞" in one edition reads "飞作治," while another reads "持." "鼓铸" in one edition reads "铸作下," which is incorrect. "杂性不同" in one edition reads "作种."

"Fortunate chance ultimately fails to meet the sage who alone knows it" — the Zhu Xi edition lacks these ten characters. "Engendering doubt" in one edition reads "sitting in doubt." "First sketched the Eight Trigrams, emulating Heaven and Earth" — one edition reads "first sketched the Eight Trigrams, imaging and emulating Heaven and Earth," while another reads "sketched the Eight Trigrams to emulate Heaven’s diagram"; both are incorrect. "Following this" in one edition reads "structuring the body." "All sages" refers to sages without rank; Confucius is one such. One edition reads "sages and" instead, which is incorrect. "Three lords" in one edition reads "three sages." "Making it a model" in one edition reads "making it sincere." "Establishing this book for the world" — this refers to the *Cantong Qi*, as the great Dao of the Golden Elixir is formless and traceless, leaving nothing for later generations to emulate. Thus, this book was established for the world, just as the middle chapter’s "establishing this record" shares the same meaning. Older editions read "establishing the *Book of Poetry* for the world," which is incorrect. "Privately considering" in one edition reads "awaiting." "Presumptuously pondering" in one edition reads "composing this foolishness," which is incorrect.

Gold accounts for fifteen, and the water number is likewise — this refers to the earlier chapter’s "upper *kan* trigram’s *dui* number is eight, and the lower *kan* trigram’s *gen* is also eight." For the upper *kan* is half a *jin* of metal, and the lower *kan* is half a *jin* of water; when the two *kan* are observed together, they form the number of thirty days in a month, though this differs from the meaning of "metal within water." One edition reads "gold accounts for fifteen" as "the gold number is fifteen." Its earth does not enter the two but unites with them; the three substances mutually contain and receive one another, transforming as if by spirit. These three substances are the three elements: metal, water, and earth. Metal and water unite with earth to become one, hence "three substances mutually containing and receiving." If fire were added, it would become five substances. Some interpret the three substances as metal, fire, and wood, and the two as water and fire — such redundancy is far from the ultimate discourse. One edition reads "its earth" as "its three," "the two" as "fire two," and "again as water two," all of which are incorrect. "Pounding and refining" in one edition reads "casting and smelting." "Achieving perfection and hardness" in one edition reads "arriving at" instead of "achieving."

Beneath, the blazing fire spreads forth, while the roars of dragon and tiger grow ever more urgent—this mirrors the later passage’s "ascending and roasting in the steamer-mountain, where the blazing fire is set below." The White Tiger leads the way, and the Azure Dragon follows in harmony, both conveying the same meaning. Older editions render "dragon and tiger" as "day and night," which is incorrect.

At the outset, the text must be cultivable—one edition reads "at the very beginning, the text must be cultivable." "Personally witness" is rendered in one edition as "mutually intimate," and in another as "must be intimate." "To ingest it" appears in one edition as "to grind and lift," in another as "to grind and knead," and in yet another as "to lift and grind"—all incorrect. "One pellet" is written in one edition as "one origin," and in another as "one grain"—both incorrect. The *daogui* refers merely to the tip of a knife’s corner, signifying its small quantity. If one interprets the knife as metal and the *gui* as the two earths, the craft may be clever, yet it strays from Master Wei’s original intent.

"Comparatively concise" appears in one edition as "simplified." "Radiant" is rendered in one edition as "flourishing"—incorrect. "Gradual erosion" is written in one edition as "violent erosion." "Manifesting dragon and tiger" appears in one edition as "inscribing dragon and tiger." "Auspicious Yellow Sprout" is written in one edition as "added Yellow Sprout."

**Middle Section**
In concert with the workings of creation—one edition reads "must" instead of "in concert," and another reads "at the beginning." "Primal foundation" appears in one edition as "mysterious foundation," and in another as "primal mold." The sixty hexagrams are employed, spreading forth as a chariot—one edition reads "encircling" instead of "employed," which is incorrect. "Without deviation" appears in one edition as "without embankment." "Toppling the precarious state" is written in one edition as "toppling the state’s precariousness." "Hexagram days" appears in one edition as "hexagram months." "Each of the sixty hexagrams has its own days" is written in one edition as "the remaining sixty hexagrams"—incorrect in meaning. "Establishing punishments" is written in an older edition as "standing punishments." "Celestial stems and earthly branches" appears in one edition as "celestial stems and nights." "With focused intent" is written in one edition as "treacherous rebels," and in another as "scattering in all directions." "One’s own mouth" appears in one edition as "the cauldron." "Its arrival" is written in one edition as "the center." "Hard and soft are decisively severed, never intermingling"—the earlier section speaks of the insubstantial controlling the substantial, which has already been explained below.

Between the dark of the moon and the new moon, the tally aligns and moves through the center to begin in the northeast, the region of *Ji* and *Dou*. For the dark of the moon and the new moon occur between *hai* and *zi*, when the sun and moon conceal themselves; the region of *Ji* and *Dou* lies between *gen* and *yin*, where the sun and moon begin their revolution. This is used to illustrate the subtlety of the elixir method, which follows the same path as heaven and earth, sun and moon. Some, however, directly take the period between *gen* and *yin* as the time between the dark and new moon, when the first yang arises and the medicine is gathered—yet this is far from the mark.

Turning to the right and revolving, the wheel of fire vomits forth its sprouts: when the yang fire revolves from the waist through *Dou* and *Ji* upward to the *Xuan* Pass, its radiance shines beyond the curtains. One edition reads "turning to the left" instead of "turning to the right." Above *Mao* and *Bi*, *Zhen* emerges as the omen—this refers to the moon’s first appearance in the west, its form like *Zhen*, used to illustrate the fire phases within the body. If one were to discuss the moon’s rising position according to the directions of the *Xiantian* Eight Trigrams, then *Zhen* of the *Xiantian* is not in the west, nor is the fire-phase *Zhen* in the west. Failing to recognize its true locus, one merely sees the contradictions in the explanations.

"Harmonious peace" appears in one edition as "initial peace." "Its unity" appears in one edition as "yin unity."

Six Five of *Kun* denotes the thirty-day period—this is not referring to the sixth line of the *Kun* hexagram. When the yang numbers have reached their term, they then recommence. Following the emotions to align with nature, they turn and unite. All editions place this under "the compass and square of the Dao," except for Master Zhu’s edition, which places it under "the mother of the world"—this is incorrect.

"Circulating through the six lines"—one edition reads *liu* as *zhang*—incorrect.
"Difficult to"—one edition reads *yi* as *ke*.
"Flourishing"—one edition reads *zhang* as *heng*.
"Generating radiance"—one edition reads *sheng* as *zheng*—incorrect.
"Gradually advancing"—one edition reads *jian* as *jin*.
"Completing *Tai*"—one edition reads *cheng* as *cheng* (to bear).
"Moving"—one edition reads *er* as *yi*.
"Elm pods"—one edition reads *jia* as *ye*—incorrect.
"Vibrating and seeking"—one edition reads *suo* as *jing*.

Yang ends in the center of *Si*, interacting—this refers to the fourth month, which belongs to *Ji*, corresponding to the *zhonglü* pitch. Master Xiashan, Zhan Gu, in his annotated edition, writes *zhong* as *zhonglü*—one edition writes *zhong* as *wu*—incorrect. *Wu* serves as the guest; one edition reads it as the host.

"Withdrawing from the world and relinquishing position"—one edition reads *qu wei* as *qu shi wei*—incorrect.
"Its essence"—one edition reads *qi* as *zhen*.
Yin expands while yang contracts, damaging name and reputation—this refers to the seventh month, which belongs to *Shen*, corresponding to the *yize* pitch. *Shen* is expansion, *yi* is injury. An old edition writes "damaging" as "submerging yang"; one edition writes "submerging" as "extolling"—both incorrect.
"Dissipating and vanishing"—one edition reads *mie* as *xi*—incorrect.
"Utmost spirit"—one edition reads *zhi* as *qi*; one edition reads *shen* as *kun*.

"Decline and flourishing"—one edition reads *shuai* as *geng*—incorrect.
"Serving and attending"—one edition reads *cheng* as *yong*.
"For a thousand autumns"—one edition reads *qiu* as *zai*.
"Utterly none"—one edition reads *wu* as *yuan*—incorrect.
"Flowing and spreading"—one edition reads *liu* as *yun*.
"Like a chicken’s egg, to the flesh smooth as maltose"—an old edition misplaces this phrase, likely having mistakenly transposed it for the sake of rhyme; it has now been corrected. "Like"—one edition reads *xiang* as *zhuang*. "Supporting"—one edition reads *fu* as *fu* (talisman). "Complete"—one edition reads *ju* as *ju* (both). "As maltose"—an old edition reads *yi* as *qian*; Master Zhu’s edition corrects it to *yi*.
*Jin* and *e* were explained in the preceding section.
"Constantly"—one edition reads as *zhu gu*; one edition reads as *zhu wan*; one edition reads as *zhu yuan*.
"At this time, emotions harmonize with Heaven and Earth"—one edition reads *yuan chu zhi shi you hu qian kun*.
"Constrained, then water settles"—one edition reads *ze shui ding huo wu xing zhi chu*. "The Dao has no form or image"—an old edition reads *wu* as *zhi*, for in antiquity the character *wu* (無) was written similarly to *zhi* (之), and in cursive script they resemble each other, leading to the error. One edition reads *xiang* as *zhuang*.
"The True One is difficult to depict"—one edition reads *yi* as *qi*.
"Utmost suspension"—one edition reads as *xuan qie*—incorrect.

The ear, eye, and mouth are the Three Treasures—one edition reads "the treasures of the ear, eye, and self," which is incorrect. "Exalting"—one edition reads *tong* for *yang*. "Seeing and observing"—one edition reads *ting* for *lan*. "Accord with the primordial chaos"—one edition reads "accord *with* the primordial." "Thought after thought"—an old edition reads "no thought." "Verification shifts of itself"—an old edition reads "verification is difficult to shift," which is incorrect. "*Jin* with"—one edition reads "with *jin*." "Penetrating the boundless"—one edition reads *run* for *dong*, which is incorrect. "Within the valley"—one edition reads "Central Palace"; one edition reads "within the vessel," both of which are incorrect. "Relying on the staff"—one edition reads "leaning on the staff." "Submerged in water"—one edition reads *tou* for *mo*. "Obtaining millet"—one edition reads the second *huo* for the first. "No merit seen"—one edition reads "not" as "none." "Utmost simplicity"—one edition reads *shi* for *zhi*, which is incorrect, for in cursive script *zhi* (至) and *shi* (事) resemble each other.

"Then they interlock, swallow, chew, and devour one another"—the old edition, Peng Zhenyi’s annotation, and the Southern Song Liu Yongnian edition all place these lines under "the loving mother nurtures, the filial son repays kindness." Master Zhu states: "Now, upon examination, these two lines naturally belong to the following passage, 'all mutually covet and merge,' where the four lines form a continuous sequence of meaning and should not be here." This humble commentator observes: this chapter uses the affection between mother and child as a metaphor, for the phrase "then they interlock and swallow" signifies the young crow repaying its mother, and thus it properly belongs here. Now, following the old edition, it is moved to its correct position.

"Three and five become one"—this refers to the preceding section’s *zi-wu* numbers combining: three *wu* and five *ji* numbers. It differs from the *Three-Five Unity* in the *Wuzhen Pian*. One edition reads *yu* for *wei*, which is incorrect.

"The child should turn right"—this means the tiger is born within water. "Noon revolves eastward"—this means the dragon emerges from within fire. *Zi* and *wu* are water and fire. "The child should turn right" refers to the conjunction of metal and water, turning right to reach *zi*. "Noon revolves eastward" refers to wood and fire as companions, revolving eastward to reach *wu*. The Dao of returning cinnabar follows the same pattern as the celestial Dao of left-revolving and right-turning. If one says *zi* turns right to reach *you* and *wu* revolves eastward to reach *mao*, this would be right-revolving and left-turning, opposing the celestial Dao. "Eastward revolving"—one edition reads *zuo* for *dong*. "Host and guest"—one edition reads *ding* for *ke*. "Coveting and merging"—one edition reads *bian* for *bing*; one edition reads *rong*, both of which are incorrect.

Each attains its nature—one edition reads *you* for *de* and *gong* for *xing*. "Ascertaining"—one edition reads *you* for *shen*. "Attains the correct Dao"—one edition reads *wei* for *cheng*. "The father bestows and grants"—one edition reads *sheng* for *zhu*, which is incorrect. "The hen"—one edition reads *fei* for *pin*. "The Dao of transformation"—one edition reads *gong* for *dao*, and another reads *jing*, both of which are incorrect. "At the inception of receiving qi"—one edition reads *yuan* for *zhi*. "Daily transformation, monthly change"—this borrows from Zhuangzi’s phrasing; one edition reads *ri shou yue hua*, which is incorrect. Another suspects *ri shou yue hua* contradicts principle and thus alters it to *yue shou ri hua*, which is also incorrect. "The nature of water is all-encompassing"—one edition reads *he rong* for *zhou zhang*. "Wood and fire as companions"—this means the dragon emerges from within fire, with three in the east and two in the south together forming five. "The conjunction of metal and water"—this means the tiger is born toward water, with one in the north and four in the west completing it. Zhang Ziyang’s statement that the four images do not depart from the two bodies precisely refers to this. Some later interpretations, placing *li* of the Earlier Heaven sequence after *zhen* and *dui* of the Later Heaven to elucidate this, not only miss Master Wei’s original intent but also contradict the great process of inner transformation. One edition reads *jin mu* for *jin shui* and *shui huo* for *mu huo*, which is gravely mistaken. "Arranged"—one edition reads *he* for *lie*, and another reads *yin*. "The heart is red, thus female"—one edition omits these four characters.

"At *zi*, the Five Phases begin"—one edition omits these four characters; another reads *ju* for *zi*. "Establishing the weft"—one edition reads *wei* as *position*. "*Xu* and *Wei*"—one edition reads *embracing the true*. "Grasping fire"—one edition reads *descending to sit*. "Harmonizing"—one edition reads *grasping fire*. "Indulging vain hopes"—one edition reads *hoping for expectations*.

**Lower Section**
Only the sages and worthies of old embraced the mysterious and held fast to the true, deeply pitying later generations who loved the Dao. Following the currents of influence, they inscribed ancient texts and composed diagrams and scriptures to enlighten posterity, revealing branches while concealing roots. This means that the sages of antiquity, having attained the Dao and embraced the mysterious while holding fast to the true, pitied those of later generations who loved the Dao yet could not grasp its teachings. Thus, they followed the currents of influence, citing antiquity to validate the present, composing diagrams and scriptures to instruct them. Yet they dared not expound it too directly, so they employed the designations of the Five Metals and Eight Stones to elucidate it. The so-called composition of diagrams and scriptures under the guise of various stones refers to the sages of old acting thus—not to those of later generations who loved the Dao. In this chapter, Peng Zhen and Chen Baoyi both misunderstood this; only Master Zhu’s commentary correctly grasped its meaning.

"Three Origins"—one edition reads *light* for *origin*, which is incorrect. "Essence overflows"—one edition reads *fluids*, which is incorrect.

"Transforming form"—one edition reads *transform* as *change*, which is incorrect. "Overturning and distorting numerous texts" means to repeatedly confuse and disorder their words rather than daring to leak them directly. One edition reads *distort* as *conceal*, which is incorrect. "Transmitted through the ages"—one edition reads *transmit* as *raise*. "Not serving"—one edition reads *serving* as *thus*. "Thus producing disordered words"—one edition reads *producing* as *not*, which is incorrect. "Mysterious channel"—one edition reads *channel* as *distant*. "Retreating from behind"—an old edition reads *behind* as *reform*, which is incorrect.

"Bolts and bars rise and fall"—*the heavens in their circuit* then rush forth. In old editions, both characters for *the heavens in their circuit* were written as *zhou-hai*; Master Zhu suspected *zhou-hai* lacked coherent meaning and thus emended it to *harm qi*, which is also incorrect. The character *harm* resembles *zhou*, while *hai* is quite close to *heaven*.

"The Yangtze and Yellow Rivers never run dry"—*water flows into the sea*. An old edition reads *not* for *into*, which is incorrect. The error of *not* for *into* was explained in detail under "the Dao has no form" in the middle section. One edition reads *Yellow River* as *Huai River*. "Ending and returning to the beginning"—one edition reads *returning to the beginning and ending*; another reads *returning to renew the beginning*. "Spread forth"—one edition reads *forth* as *establish*. "White Tiger"—one edition reads *Tiger* as *alum*, which is incorrect. "Azure Dragon"—one edition reads *Dragon* as *fluid*, which is incorrect. "Pressing and halting"—one edition reads *halting* as *into*. "Dragon’s scales, armor, and mane"—one edition reads *Dragon* as *fish* and *armor* as *frivolous*, which is incorrect. "Joining and linking"—one edition reads *linking* as *jumbled*. "Guarding one another"—one edition reads *one another* as *and thus."

The Chamber-Six—one edition reads *Six* as *Seven*, which is incorrect. *Upright Yang*—one edition reads *Yang* as *Yin*, which is incorrect. "The Three and Five converge with Danger as one, all gathering in one place"—this refers to the qi of Chamber-Six, Strung-Seven, Extended-Two, and Three Directions merging with the North’s Danger-One, all assembling and returning to a single locus. At its core lies the workings of creation: the Southern Extended and Northern Danger correspond to the moon, while the Eastern Chamber and Western Pleiades correspond to the sun. Danger-One combined with Chamber-Six forms the generative and completive numbers of Water; Extended-Two combined with Strung-Seven forms the generative and completive numbers of Fire. Chamber-Six in the East and Extended-Two in the South—Wood and Fire as companions—combine Six and Two to make Eight. Pleiades-Seven in the West and Danger-One in the North—Metal and Water in union—combine Seven and One to make Eight, corresponding to the weight of two eights as one jin. This is what is meant by "rooted in but two things." One edition reads *converge with Danger as one* as *converge to become one*; another reads *as one* as *with one*—both are incorrect. Another edition reads *one place* as *two places*, which is also incorrect. One must recognize that in Master Wei’s composition of this work, not a single phrase fails to accord with the workings of creation. If one were to suppose that "the Three and Five with the One all gather in two places," where then would the workings of creation reside? Moreover, the notion of "two places" is especially egregious. Even if one were to insist on calling it "two places," one should speak of *division* rather than *gathering*; if speaking of *gathering*, one should speak of *one place* rather than *two places*.

*Qu-fu*—Jiaxia Mountain’s commentary reads *Fu-qu*. "Crimson hue penetrating inner and outer"—one edition reads *crimson-black penetrating inner and outer*. *Millet grain*—one edition reads *millet* as *rice*, which is incorrect. "If the qi of mountains and marshes steams forth"—one edition reads *the qi of mountains and marshes mutually steams forth*, which is incorrect. *For a hundred million generations*—one edition reads *generations* as *eras*. *And may be examined*—one edition reads *and* as *of itself*. *Heart-spirit*—one edition reads *heart* as *defense*. *Suddenly self-awakens*—one edition reads *suddenly* as *abruptly*. *Receiving the celestial charts*—one edition reads *charts* as *records of celestial charts*, which is incorrect.

*Tripod Vessel Song*: "Diameter of one fen"—an old edition reads *diameter* as *ten*. "Length of one chi two"—one edition reads *one chi two* as *two chi*, which is incorrect.

The abdomen’s *qi*-alignment—Zhu Xi notes that *qi* (齐) is the ancient form of *navel* (脐); one edition reads *five qi-alignments* (五齐), which is incorrect. "Head and tail are *wu*, the center is *wen*"—this refers to the new and full moons as head and tail, and the full moon as the center. Head and tail are thus one realm, and the center is likewise one realm. If one claims that *si* (巳) is yang and *zi* (子) is the tail, *wu* (午) is yin and *hai* (亥) is the head, with *si* and *wu* facing each other as the center, then there would be two heads and tails and two centers. Another interpretation holds that when advancing yang fire, *zi* (子), *chou* (丑), and *yin* (寅) form the head, while *chen* (辰) and *si* (巳) form the tail; when retreating yin符, *wu* (午), *wei* (未), and *shen* (申) form the head, while *xu* (戌) and *hai* (亥) form the tail, with the central palace as the center—thus two heads, two tails, and one center. Neither interpretation is sound.

*Two sevens gather*—one edition reads *gather* as *orifice* (窍), which is incorrect.
*Examining the brain’s principles*—one edition reads *examining* (赡) as *gazing* (瞻), another as *gallbladder* (胆), both incorrect.

**Preface**
The *Three Correlations* (三相䚸) refer to the mutual resemblance in yin-yang transformation among the *Great Change* (易), the *Yellow and Lao* (黄老), and furnace fire. Since Peng Zhenyi depicted the five phases with wood as three, fire as two, earth as five, metal as four, and water as one—forming a diagram of *five positions mutually attuned, each with its harmony*—later generations all followed Peng’s interpretation, mistakenly attributing it to Wei Boyang’s original text and conflating it with the *correlation of the five phases* (五行之相䚸). This error has persisted to this day without correction. Yet none have considered what Wei Boyang truly meant by *correlation*. Moreover, the *Change*’s *five positions mutually attuned, each with its harmony* refers to the harmonization of the five phases’ generating and completing numbers. Peng’s system uses only the generating numbers—how can this be called *five positions mutually attuned, each with its harmony*? Some argue that wood as three and fire as two form one five, metal as four and water as one form another five, with central earth as five completing three fives—this might still be called *three fives correlated* (三五相䚸). But to speak of the *correlation of the five phases* (五行之相䚸) is untenable, for water is one, fire is two, and the numbers of metal, wood, and earth each differ—how can they be correlated? One edition reads *mutually correlated* (互相䚸), which is somewhat plausible, though far less clear than the *Three Correlations*.

Examining the subsequent text, it states: *"The Three Ways emerge from the One, sharing the same path"* and *"Arrayed as three branches, stems interconnected, differing in name yet issuing from one gate"*—the meaning of *Three Correlations* is thus unmistakably evident.

*Guo of Kuai*—Wei Boyang’s self-reference. Wei was from Shangyu in Guiji; by calling it *Guo of Kuai* instead of Guiji, he avoided revealing his native place. To read it directly as Guiji would be a later emendation, not Wei’s original wording.

*Embracing simplicity*—one edition reads *embracing simplicity and holding purity* (怀朴抱素).
*Unfond of power and glory*—meaning he refused to delight in power and glory. *Delight* (乐) should be read in the departing tone; one edition reads *power* (权) as *joy* (欢), which is incorrect.
*Profit and fame*—one edition reads *profit* (利) as *command* (令).
*Leisurely dwelling in seclusion*—one edition reads *distant traveler in leisure* (远客燕闲).
*Observing his inclinations*—one edition reads *inclinations* (所) as *purport* (旨).
*Shared principles*—one edition reads *principles* (伦) as *discourse* (论).
*Disseminating transformation*—one edition reads *disseminating* (施) as *spiritual* (神).
*Returning to the root*—one edition reads *root* (根) as *foolish* (愚), which is incorrect.
*My heart*—one edition reads *heart* (心) as *form* (形).
*Eight minerals*—one edition reads *eight* (八) as *five* (五).
*Perfecting things*—one edition reads *things* (物) as *achievement* (功).

The text "Committing to the times, departing from harm, relying on hills and mountains, roaming the vast expanse, neighboring with spirits" through "securely attaining long life"—these sixteen phrases encapsulate the three characters of Wei Boyang. "Each mutually bearing" refers to: *committing to spirits* mutually bearing forms *Wei*; *people* and *white* mutually bearing forms *Bo*; *阝* and *sun* mutually bearing forms *Yang*. One edition reads *spirits* (鬼) as *immortals* (仙), *narrow pass* (阨) as *calamity* (厄). "Each mutually bearing, securely attaining long life" is rendered as "Auspicious people mutually bearing, peacefully attaining long life." Another edition reads: "Auspicious people mutually bearing, securely attaining long life." Both are incorrect. Yet another edition reads *east, west, south, and slanting* (东西南倾) as *east, west, south, and north* (东西南北), and *drought and flood* (水旱) as *drought, flood, and warfare* (水旱兵革).

**Eulogy and Preface**
*Establishing governance*—one edition reads *governing the realm* (御极).
*Rice and salt*—this adopts the language of the *Tianguan Shu* in the *Records of the Grand Historian*. "Rice and salt" signifies clear division, like black and white. One edition reads *rice and salt* (米盐) as *Kan and Li* (坎离), which is incorrect.
*With the Yi*—one edition reads *Yi* (易) as *classic* (经).
*Establishing commentary*—one edition reads *commentary* (注) as *method* (法), which is incorrect.
*For this*—one edition reads *this* (此) as *I* (吾).

After the three chapters of the *Zhouyi Cantong Qi Fahui* were printed, Master Chongxu, Zhang Benzhen, commissioned a craftsman to carve twenty pages of explanatory notes to resolve doubts, enabling readers to have a reference. Chongxu may be said to be deeply devoted to the Dao and courageous in upholding righteousness.

*Zhouyi Cantong Qi Fahui Shiyi*
